Disposal of Udhiyah Skin and Meat: Permissible Uses and Sales
Explains permissible uses and sale of udhiyah skin and meat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling udhiyah skin and meat, distinguish permissible from impermissible exchanges, and apply the rule to practical scenarios.

Core Idea
Udhiyah skin and meat may be sold for enduring benefit but not for consumables; the price must be given in charity.

When you sacrifice an animal for Eid al-Adha, what can you do with its skin and meat? The Hanafi school permits giving them in charity, using them for lasting items, or even selling them—but with restrictions. This lesson unpacks the detailed ruling from classical texts, clarifying what is allowed and why, and highlights an intra-madhhab disagreement on selling meat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its primary ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? The disposal of the udhiyah's skin and meat. The primary ruling is that they are for charity and personal use, but sale is permissible with conditions.
The passage distinguishes between items with remaining substance (bāqin) and consumables (mustahlak).
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The author cites the hadith of 'Ali (radiAllahu anhu) where the Prophet (ﷺ) said: 'Give charity with its coverings and ropes, and do not give the butcher anything from it.' This is the core textual evidence.
The hadith is used to derive the dislike of sale and the prohibition of paying the butcher from the udhiyah.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (how the proof yields the ruling)
The Prophet's command 'do not give the butcher' implies that the meat/skin should not be used for exchange (like sale). The Hanafi school interprets this as indicating dislike (karāhah), not absolute prohibition, because ownership is established and the command is for charity.
Hanafi usul: a prohibition in non-obligatory matters indicates karāhah tanzīhiyyah unless there is evidence of tahrim.
4
Note the ikhtilaf (disagreement) and tarjih
There is disagreement on whether meat can be sold like skin. Some later Hanafi texts (al-Khulāsah) forbid selling meat, but the correct view (al-Hidāyah) equates meat with skin, permitting sale for lasting benefit. The relied-upon (muftā bihi) position follows Abu Hanifah and Muhammad.
The thamarah al-khilaf: under the first view, meat cannot be sold; under the second, it can be exchanged for a useful item.

Source Text

قوله : تصدق بثمنه ... الخ ؛ في الدر المختار»، وشرحه «رد المحتار) : فإنّ بيع اللحم والجلد به : أي بمستهلك أو بدراهم ، أفاد أنه ليس [له] بيعهما بمستهلك، وأن له الجلد بما تبقى . عينه، وسكت عن بيع اللحم به، للخلاف فيه. ففي «الخلاصة» وغيرها : لو أراد بيع اللحم ليتصدق بثمنه ليس له ذلك، وليس له فيه إلا أن يطعم أو يأكل ، والصحيح كما في «الهداية» وشروحها : إنّهما سواء في جواز بيعهما بما ينتفع بعينه دون ما يستهلك. وأيده في «الكفاية» بما روى ابن سماعة له عن محمد الله : لو اشترى باللحم ثوباً، فلا بأس بلبسه، وفي «القنية»: اشترى بلحمها مأكولاً فأكلـه لـم يجـب عـلـيـه التصدق بقيمته استحساناً، ودفع اللحم إلى فقير بنية الزكاة لا يحسب عنها في ظاهر الرواية، لكن إذا دفع الغني ثم دفع إليه بنيتها يحسب، ليصدق بثمنه ؛ أي بالدراهم فيما لو أبدله بها ، ومفاده صحة البيع، وهو قول أبي حنيفة ومحمد . لقيام الملك والقدرة على التسليم، مع الكراهة للحديث الآتي ، وعن الثاني باطل ؛ لأنه كالوقف. ولا يعطي أجر الجزار عنها ؛ لأنه كبيع ، لأنّ كلا منهما معاوضة ؛ لأنه إنما يعطي الجزار بمقابلة جزره ، والبيع مكروه ، فكذا ما في معناه. واستفيدت كذا في بعض النسخ ، والضمير للكراهة، لكن صاحب «الهداية» ذكر ذلك الحديث في البيع، ثم قال بعد قوله : ولا يعطي أجر الجزار منها ؛ لقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم لعلي ه: «تصدق بجلالها وحطامها ، ولا تعط أجر الجزار منها شيئاً
Book of Udhiyah: Its Definition, Conditions, and Rulings. He gives charity with its skin, or makes it into a tool like a bag, or a sock, or fur, or exchanges it for something that benefits perpetually, not for something that benefits after consumption like vinegar and the like. Then if the meat or skin is sold, he gives charity with its price. It is permissible, but disliked without his permission. His statement: 'And he gives charity with its skin' because it is part of it, and he may give charity and benefit from it. Do you not see that he may eat its meat? And there is no harm in buying with it something whose substance remains, such as what we mentioned, because the substitute has the ruling of the substituted. He does not buy with it something that cannot be benefited from except after consumption, like meat and food. And he does not sell it for dirhams to spend the dirhams on himself and his family. So the meaning is that he does not give charity with the intention of acquiring wealth, and meat is like skin in the correct view. He may sell it for something that cannot be benefited from except after consumption. If he sold it for dirhams to give charity with them, it is permissible, because it is an act of worship like giving charity with the skin and meat. His saying: 'Whoever sells the skin of his udhiyah, there is no udhiyah for him' indicates the dislike of selling. As for selling, it is permissible due to ownership and ability to deliver. His statement: 'like vinegar and the like' - it is with khā' mu'jama, well-known, and with mu'jamah it is the sesame. His statement: 'he gives charity with its price' - in al-Durr al-Mukhtār and its commentary Radd al-Muhtār: 'Indeed, selling the meat and skin with it, i.e., for a consumable or for dirhams, indicates that it is not permissible to sell them for a consumable, and that he may [sell] the skin for something whose substance remains. And he was silent about selling the meat with it, because of the disagreement.' In al-Khulāsah etc.: 'If he intends to sell the meat to give charity with its price, he does not have that; he only has to feed or eat.' The correct view as in al-Hidāyah and its commentaries is that they are equal in the permissibility of selling them for something that benefits by its substance, not for something that consumes. Al-Kifāyah supported it with what Ibn Samā'ah narrated from Muḥammad: 'If he bought a garment with the meat, there is no harm in wearing it.' In al-Qunyah: 'If he bought edible food with its meat and ate it, he is not obligated to give charity with its value by istihsān.' If he gives the meat to a poor person with the intention of zakāh, it does not count for it in the apparent narration, but if the wealthy person gives it, then gives it with intention, it counts. 'He gives charity with its price' i.e., with dirhams if he exchanges it for them, and its implication is the validity of the sale, and it is the saying of Abū Ḥanīfah and Muḥammad, due to ownership and ability to deliver, with dislike for the hadith to come. The other opinion says it is invalid because it is like a waqf. And he does not give the butcher's wage from it, because it is like a sale, since each is a compensation; he only gives the butcher in exchange for his slaughtering, and selling is disliked, so likewise what is in its meaning. This is taken from some copies, and the pronoun refers to dislike. But the author of al-Hidāyah mentioned that hadith regarding sale, then said after his statement: 'And he does not give the butcher's wage from it' due to the saying of the Prophet (ﷺ) to 'Alī: 'Give charity with its covers and its ropes, and do not give the butcher anything from it.'
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage is an expanded commentary on the rulings of udhiyah (sacrificial animal) concerning its skin and meat. The author explains that the owner may give the skin in charity or use it for a lasting utility (e.g., making a bag, socks, or fur). He may exchange it for something else that provides enduring benefit, but not for items that are consumed upon use (like vinegar or food). If the skin or meat is sold for dirhams, the proceeds must be given in charity. The sale itself is permissible (jā'iz) because the owner owns the item and can deliver it, but it is disliked (makrūh) due to the prophetic warning. There is an intra-Hanafi disagreement on whether meat can be sold: the correct view (as per al-Hidāyah) is that meat is like skin—permissible to sell for something with remaining substance, but not for consumables. The passage also addresses the butcher's wage: it cannot be paid from the udhiyah's meat or skin because that would be like a sale, and sale of udhiyah parts is disliked. Instead, the owner should pay the butcher from other wealth, as per the hadith of 'Alī.

Key Terms
أضحية (udhiyah): The animal sacrificed on the days of Eid al-Adha as an act of worship.
جلد (jild): The skin of the sacrificial animal; its disposal is subject to specific rulings.
مستهلك (mustahlak): An item that is consumed upon use, such as food or vinegar; cannot be purchased with udhiyah proceeds.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on selling the skin of the udhiyah for a consumable item like vinegar?
A) Permissible without dislike
B) Permissible but disliked
C) Not permissible at all
D) Only if the buyer is poor

Key Takeaway
Udhiyah skin and meat may be sold for enduring benefit only, with the price given in charity; sale is valid but disliked, and paying the butcher from the udhiyah is prohibited.

Ruling on Selling the Meat and Hide of the Udhiyah
Discusses the permissibility and ruling of selling udhiyah meat and hide

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling the meat and hide of the sacrificial animal, distinguishing between consumable and durable goods, and explain the intra-madhhab difference.

Core Idea
Sale of udhiyah parts: valid for non-consumables, makruh tanzihi for consumables.

This lesson examines the nuanced Hanafi ruling on the sale of udhiyah meat and hide. The source text from al-Hidayah and its commentaries clarifies that while ownership and delivery are established, a hadith prohibition makes such sales disliked. We will explore the distinction between selling for consumables and durable goods, and the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf with Abu Yusuf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its general ruling
The act is selling the meat or hide of the sacrificial animal (udhiyah). The general ruling is that the sale is valid but disliked (makruh tanzihi).
The sale is not completely prohibited, but discouraged.
2
Distinguish between consumable and durable goods
Selling for consumables (mustahlik, e.g., food) is not allowed; selling for durable goods (ma yuntafa‘ bi ‘aynihi, e.g., a garment) is permissible. Both types of sale are valid but the former is more severely disliked.
The correct view from al-Hidayah treats meat and hide equally.
3
Locate the dalil
The primary evidence is the hadith of the Prophet (s) to ‘Ali (r): 'Give charity with its hides and equipment, and do not give anything to the butcher from it.'
The hadith prohibits a sale-like transaction (paying the butcher) and implies dislike for sales.
4
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The prohibition of giving the butcher’s wage from the udhiyah indicates that any sale-like exchange with udhiyah parts is disliked. The hadith does not negate ownership, so the sale remains valid.
The hadith is used as evidence for karahah, not for invalidity.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf and tarjih
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold the sale valid but makruh; Abu Yusuf holds it invalid like a waqf. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad due to stronger evidence (ownership and delivery).
The tarjih is supported by al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اللحم والجلد به : أي بمستهلك أو بدراهم ، أفاد أنه ليس [له] بيعهما بمستهلك، وأن له الجلد بما تبقى . عينه، وسكت عن بيع اللحم به، للخلاف فيه. ففي «الخلاصة» وغيرها : لو أراد بيع اللحم ليتصدق بثمنه ليس له ذلك، وليس (1) له فيه إلا أن يطعم أو يأكل ، والصحيح كما في «الهداية» وشروحها : إنّهما سواء في جواز بيعهما بما ينتفع بعينه دون ما يستهلك. «قُهستاني». وأيده في «الكفاية»(۲) بما روى ابن سماعة له عن محمد الله : لو اشترى باللحم ثوباً، فلا بأس بلبسه، وفي «القنية»: اشترى بلحمها مأكولاً فأكلـه لـم يجـب عـلـيـه التصدق بقيمته استحساناً، ودفع اللحم إلى فقير بنية الزكاة لا يحسب عنها في ظاهر الرواية، لكن إذا دفع الغني ثم دفع إليه بنيتها يحسب، ليصدق بثمنه ؛ أي بالدراهم فيما لو أبدله بها ، ومفاده صحة البيع، وهو قول أبي حنيفة ومحمد . (بدائع. لقيام الملك والقدرة على التسليم «هداية»(۳)، مع الكراهة للحديث الآتي ، وعن الثاني باطل ؛ لأنه كالوقف. «مجتبى». ولا يعطي أجر الجزار عنها ؛ لأنه كبيع ، لأنّ كلا منهما معاوضة ؛ لأنه إنما يعطي الجزار بمقابلة جزره ، والبيع مكروه ، فكذا ما في معناه. «كفاية»(4). واستفيدت كذا في بعض النسخ ، والضمير للكراهة، لكن صاحب «الهداية»(٥) ذكر ذلك الحديث في البيع، ثم قال بعد قوله : ولا يعطي أجر الجزار منها ؛ لقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم لعلي ه: «تصدق بجلالها وحطامها ، ولا تعط أجر الجزار منها شيئاً (٦)
The meat and hide: meaning [sold] for consumables or dirhams. It indicates that it is not permissible for him to sell them for consumables, and that he may give the hide for what remains of it [i.e., for non-consumable items]. He remained silent about selling the meat for it, due to the difference of opinion. In 'al-Khulasa' and others: if he wants to sell the meat to give its price in charity, he does not have that; he has no option except to feed or eat. And the correct view as in 'al-Hidayah' and its commentaries is that both are equal in the permissibility of selling them for what one benefits from in kind, not for consumables. (Qahistani). And this is supported in 'al-Kifayah' by what Ibn Sama'ah narrated from Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him): if he buys a garment with the meat, there is no harm in wearing it. In 'al-Qunyah': if he buys an edible item with its meat and eats it, he is not obligated to give its value in charity by istihsan. And giving the meat to a poor person with the intention of zakat is not counted for it in the apparent narration, but if a rich person gives then gives it to him with that intention, it is counted. [The phrase] 'to give its price in charity': meaning in dirhams if he exchanges it for them, and its implication is the validity of the sale, and that is the saying of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. (Bada'i') because ownership and the ability to deliver are established (Hidayah), along with dislike due to the following hadith. And from the second [i.e., Abu Yusuf] it is invalid because it is like a waqf. (Mujtaba). And he should not give the butcher's wage from it, because it is like a sale, since each is a contract of exchange; he only gives the butcher in exchange for his slaughtering, and sale is disliked, so likewise what is in its meaning. (Kifayah). And it is understood [that the dislike] is in some manuscripts, and the pronoun refers to the dislike, but the author of al-Hidayah mentioned that hadith in the chapter of sale, then said after his statement: and do not give the butcher's wage from it, because the Prophet (s) said to 'Ali (r): 'Give charity with its hides and equipment, and do not give anything to the butcher from it.'
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the ruling regarding selling the meat and hide of the sacrificial animal (udhiyah). It first explains that the ruling differentiates between selling for consumable items (e.g., food) and for non-consumable durable goods. Selling for consumables is not allowed; the correct view from al-Hidayah is that both meat and hide can be sold for what one benefits from in kind, not for consumables. The Qahistani commentary supports this. The text then cites al-Kifayah which quotes a narration from Ibn Sama'ah from Imam Muhammad: if one buys a garment with the meat, it is permissible to wear it, indicating the sale is valid. Also from al-Qunyah, if one buys edible items with the meat and eats it, he need not give its value in charity by istihsan. The passage then discusses giving the meat to a poor person with the intention of zakat: it is not counted unless the poor person first gives it to a rich person (who then gives it back with zakat intent) – a device to avoid direct sale. The underlying principle is that the sale of meat and hide is valid according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, because ownership and ability to deliver are established, though it is disliked (makruh) due to the hadith. Abu Yusuf holds it invalid, comparing it to a waqf. The text also clarifies that the butcher should not be paid from the udhiyah itself, because that resembles a sale, which is disliked. The hadith of the Prophet to 'Ali is cited: 'Give charity with its hides and equipment, and do not give anything to the butcher from it.' This hadith reinforces the dislike of sale-like transactions with the udhiyah parts.

Key Terms
مستهلك (mustahlik): Consumable items, i.e., goods that are eaten or used up, as opposed to durable goods.
الكراهة (al-karahah): Dislike (makruh tanzihi); the sale is valid but discouraged due to the hadith prohibition.
البيع (al-bay'): Sale; the act of exchanging udhiyah parts for money or goods.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on selling the hide of a sacrificial animal for durable goods?
A) Valid without any dislike
B) Valid but makruh tanzihi
C) Invalid like a waqf
D) Only allowed if given in charity

Key Takeaway
Selling udhiyah meat or hide is valid but disliked; selling for consumables is more strongly discouraged.

Two People Mistakenly Slaughter Each Other's Udhiyah Sheep: Validity by Istihsan
Ruling on mistaken slaughter with istihsan and intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on two people mistakenly slaughtering each other's designated udhiyah sheep, explain the istihsan reasoning that overrides qiyas, and identify the intra-Hanafi difference with Zufar.

Core Idea
Designation for udhiyah creates implied permission, making the mistaken slaughter valid and liability-free by istihsan.

This lesson addresses a common scenario: during Eid al-Adha, two people may accidentally slaughter each other's sheep. The default analogy (qiyas) would rule the act invalid and require compensation, but the Hanafi school prefers (istihsan) a different outcome. Understanding why the designation (ta'yin) of the animal and customary practice create implied permission unlocks the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the default qiyas ruling
The act is each person slaughtering the other's designated udhiyah sheep. By qiyas, it is invalid and requires compensation because it involves using another's property without permission.
Qiyas is the standard analogical reasoning.
2
Pinpoint the basis of istihsan
The istihsan rests on two grounds: (1) the sheep was already designated (ta'ayyanat) for udhiyah, making it obligatory to sacrifice that specific animal; (2) custom (urf) dictates that owners seek help from others for slaughter, implying permission.
Designation creates a kind of sacredness and urgency.
3
Understand the implied permission argument
The owner cannot replace the designated sheep; the opportunity of the sacrifice days is limited. The custom of seeking help in slaughter makes it as if explicit permission was given. The loss of direct participation is outweighed by fulfilling the obligation.
The example of a butcher tying the legs is given in the text.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf with Zufar
Imam Zufar holds that each person is liable (ghurm) because slaughtering without permission is transgression. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is no liability due to istihsan.
Zufar's view follows strict qiyas.
5
Apply the ruling to the case
Both udhiyahs are valid, and no monetary compensation is required. This is the operative Hanafi fatwa.
The same ruling applies even if the mistake is discovered later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو غلط اثنان ، وذَبَحَ كل شاءً صاحبه صح بلا غُرْم، وصحتِ التضحية بشاةِ الغصب لا الوديعة، وضمنها [Y]. ولو غلط اثنان ، وذَبَحَ كل شاة صاحبه صح بلا غُرم) ، وفي القياس أن لا يصح ، لأَنه ذَبَحَ شاةَ غيره بغير أمره ، وجه الاستحسان : أنها تعينت للأضحية ويضمن ودلالة الإذن حاصلة ، فإنَّ العادة جرت بالاستعانة بالغير في أمر الذبح. وصحتِ التّضحية بشاةِ الغصب لا الوديعة، وضمنها) ؛ لأنَّ في الغصب والنهي عنه نهى عن البيع أيضاً ؛ لأنه في معنى البيع اهـ، ولا يخفى أنّ في كل من الحديثين دلالة على المطلوب من الموضعين من قوله : «من باع جلد أضحيته فلا أضحية له»). كما في «الهداية». [١]قوله : ويضمن ؛ أي يضمن كلّ واحدٍ منهما لصاحبه، وهو قول زفر ؛ لأنه متعد بالذبح بغير أمره، فيضمن كما إذا ذبح شاة اشتراها القصاب، والتضحية قربة، فلا تتأدى بنية غيره. [٢]قوله : إنها تعينت للأضحية ؛ لتعينها بالأضحية، حتى وجب عليه أن يضحي بها بعينها في أيام النحر، ويكره أن يبدل بها غيرها ، فصار المالك مستعيناً بمن يكون أهلاً للذبح، فصار مأذونا له دلالة ؛ لأنها تفوت بمضي هذه الأيام، ويخاف أن يعجز عن إقامتها لعارض يعتريه. فصار كما إذا ذبح شاة وشدّ القصاب رجليها ، وكيف لا يأذن له، وفيه مسارعة إلى الخير، فتحقيق ما عينه ولا يبالي لفوات مباشرته وشهوده، لحصول ما هو أعظمُ من ذلك، وهو ما ييناه فيصير أدنى دلالة، وهو كالصريح ومن هذا الجنس مسائل استحسانية لأصحابنا. كذا في تكملة البحر
If two people make a mistake and each slaughters the other's sheep, it is valid without liability (ghurm). And udhiyah with a stolen sheep is valid, but not with a deposit, and he is liable (for the deposit). If two people make a mistake and each slaughters the other's sheep, it is valid without liability, but by qiyas it would not be valid, because he slaughtered another's sheep without his permission. The basis of istihsan: that the sheep became designated (ta'ayyanat) for udhiyah, and he is liable, and the indication of permission is present, because the custom is to seek help from others in the matter of slaughter. And udhiyah with a stolen sheep is valid but not with a deposit, and he is liable (for the deposit), because in the case of usurpation (ghasb) and the prohibition of it, there is also prohibition of sale, since it is like a sale. And it is not hidden that each of the two hadiths indicates the required from the two places from his statement: 'Whoever sells the hide of his udhiyah, there is no udhiyah for him.' As in al-Hidayah. [1] His statement: 'and he is liable'; meaning each one is liable to the other, and that is the saying of Zufar, because he transgressed by slaughtering without his permission, so he is liable as if he slaughtered a sheep that the butcher bought, and udhiyah is a worship, so it is not performed by the intention of another. [2] His statement: 'that it became designated for udhiyah'; because it was designated for udhiyah, so that it became obligatory for him to sacrifice that particular sheep in the days of Nahr, and it is disliked to exchange it for another. So the owner becomes someone who seeks help from one who is qualified for slaughter, so permission is implied, because the opportunity is lost with the passing of these days, and he fears that he may be unable to perform it due to some impediment. So it becomes like when a person slaughters a sheep and the butcher ties its legs; how could he not permit it? And in this there is hastening to good, so he fulfills what he designated and does not mind missing his own direct action and presence, because something greater is achieved, which we have explained, so it becomes the slightest indication, and it is like explicit permission. And from this category are istihsani issues of our companions. This is from Takmilat al-Bahr.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage discusses two issues. First, if two people mistakenly slaughter each other's sheep that were designated for udhiyah, the udhiyah of both is valid without any monetary compensation (ghurm). This is by istihsan (juristic preference), even though qiyas would deem it invalid because it involves slaughtering another's property without permission. The reason for istihsan is that the sheep were already designated (ta'ayyanat) for udhiyah, and customarily owners seek help from others for slaughter, so permission is implied. The designated sheep must be sacrificed in its specific days, and the owner would be unable to replace it if delayed, so implied permission exists. Second, using a stolen sheep (ghasb) for udhiyah is valid and the udhiyah counts, but the thief is liable for the sheep (must return or pay compensation). However, using a deposited sheep (wadi'ah) for udhiyah without the owner's permission is invalid, because a deposit is in trust and not owned by the depositee. The text also cites a hadith about selling the hide of the udhiyah, indicating that transactions of udhiyah parts are prohibited. The commentary then explains the liability (ghurm): according to Zufar, each person is liable to the other for the value of the sheep because slaughtering without permission is transgression. But the relied-upon position is that there is no liability because of implied permission. The extended explanation in the second part likens the situation to a butcher tying the legs of a sheep for the owner – clearly he has permission to do so, and similarly, slaughtering the designated udhiyah is permitted by custom and necessity.

Key Terms
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference; preferring a ruling that deviates from qiyas due to custom, necessity, or public interest.
التعيين (al-ta'yin): Designation; the act of specifying a particular animal for udhiyah, which obligates its sacrifice and creates a kind of sacredness.
الغرم (al-ghurm): Monetary liability or compensation for property damage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, if two people mistakenly slaughter each other's designated udhiyah sheep, what is the ruling?
A) Both udhiyahs are invalid and each must compensate the other
B) Both udhiyahs are valid and no compensation is required
C) Only the sheep that was slaughtered first is valid
D) The owner of each sheep must repeat the udhiyah

Key Takeaway
Designation for udhiyah combined with customary practice creates implied permission, making a mistaken mutual slaughter valid and liability-free by istihsan.

Using a Stolen or Deposited Sheep for Udhiyah: Valid vs Invalid
Ruling on udhiyah with misappropriated animals and the legal distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi ruling on using a stolen (ghasb) sheep versus a deposited (wadi'ah) sheep for udhiyah, and explain the rationale based on the prohibition of selling.

Core Idea
A stolen sheep yields a valid udhiyah but with liability; a deposited sheep used without permission yields no reward.

This lesson addresses the validity of udhiyah when the animal is not owned by the sacrificer. Two common scenarios are compared: the animal is taken by force (ghasb) or held in trust (wadi'ah). The Hanafi school distinguishes them based on the presence or absence of an implicit prohibition of sale. Understanding this distinction reinforces the principle that property rights affect worship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
First case: a person steals (ghasb) a sheep and sacrifices it for udhiyah. Second case: a person is entrusted (wadi'ah) with a sheep and sacrifices it without the owner's permission.
The ruling for each differs.
2
Recall the ruling for the stolen sheep
The udhiyah is valid (sahih), but the thief is liable (ghurm) for the value of the sheep to the owner.
The act of worship is accepted, but the sin of theft remains.
3
Recall the ruling for the deposited sheep
The udhiyah is not valid (la yasihhu); it does not fulfill the obligation. The depositee is liable for the sheep.
Using a trust without permission invalidates the act.
4
Understand the rationale
In ghasb, the prohibition of theft also implies a prohibition of sale (since theft is like a forced sale). The animal is considered owned by the thief in a sense, so the sacrifice can be valid. In wadi'ah, the depositee is a trustee and has no ownership rights; using it is a betrayal of trust, so the act is invalid.
The text cites a hadith about selling the hide of udhiyah to support the idea that profit-related transactions are prohibited.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[١]قوله : ويضمن ؛ أي يضمن كلّ واحدٍ منهما لصاحبه، وهو قول زفر ؛ لأنه متعد بالذبح بغير أمره، فيضمن كما إذا ذبح شاة اشتراها القصاب، والتضحية قربة، فلا تتأدى بنية غيره. Key Terms
الغصب (al-ghasb): Usurpation; taking another's property illegally. A stolen animal used for udhiyah renders the udhiyah valid but the usurper is liable.
الوديعة (al-wadi'ah): Deposit; property entrusted to another for safekeeping. Using it without permission invalidates the udhiyah.
البيع (al-bay'): Sale. The passage states that ghasb is like a sale in that it transfers possession, hence the prohibition of sale applies.

A) Because the stolen sheep is owned by the thief, whereas the deposited sheep is not
B) Because ghasb implies prohibition of sale, which makes the udhiyah valid; wadi'ah has no such implication
C) Because the hadith about selling hide only applies to stolen sheep
D) Because the deposited sheep is likely smaller

Key Takeaway
A stolen sheep yields a valid udhiyah with liability; a deposited sheep yields an invalid udhiyah – the distinction hinges on the implicit prohibition of sale in ghasb.

Two People Mistakenly Slaughter Each Other's Sacrificial Sheep: Istihsan Over Qiyas
Ruling on mutual mistake in sacrificial slaughter with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of istihsan to the case of mutual mistake in sacrifice and distinguish the mufta bihi position from Zufar's qiyas.

Core Idea
When two people mistakenly slaughter each other's sacrificial sheep, the sacrifice is valid without compensation due to istihsan.

In this lesson, we examine a classic Hanafi case where two people each slaughter the other's designated sacrificial sheep by mistake. The majority Hanafi position, based on istihsan (juristic preference), holds the sacrifice valid and no compensation required. This ruling contrasts with Imam Zufar's analogical reasoning requiring compensation. We will explore the proofs and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the factual scenario
Two people each intend to sacrifice their own designated sheep, but by mistake each slaughters the other's sheep. Determine the act: each performs the slaughter on the other's property.
The sheep are specifically designated for sacrifice (ta'ayyana lil-udhiya).
2
State the majority Hanafi ruling
The sacrifice is valid (sahih) and no monetary compensation (ghurm) is required. This is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi).
This ruling is based on istihsan, not qiyas.
3
Understand the qiyas reasoning
By strict analogy (qiyas), the act would be invalid because slaughtering another's property without permission is trespass (ta'addi), requiring guarantee (daman). Imam Zufar holds this view.
Zufar analogizes to a butcher who slaughters a purchased sheep without permission.
4
Understand the istihsan reasoning
Istihsan overrides qiyas here. The sheep are designated for a religious duty that must be performed within specific days. Custom (urf) dictates that people seek help in slaughter, so implicit permission (dalalat al-idhn) is inferred. The owner would want the sacrifice fulfilled.
The hadith 'Whoever sells the skin of his sacrifice, there is no sacrifice for him' shows the animal is dedicated, not to be disposed of arbitrarily.
5
Recognize the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf and its consequence
Imam Zufar's qiyas would require each person to pay the value of the other's sheep. The majority view (Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn) rejects this, and fatwa is given accordingly.
The difference affects whether compensation is due; the majority facilitates the sunnah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو غلط اثنان ، وذَبَحَ كل شاةً صاحبه صح بلا غُرْم، وصحتِ التضحية بشاةِ الغصب لا الوديعة، وضمنها. ولو غلط اثنان ، وذَبَحَ كل شاة صاحبه صح بلا غُرم) ، وفي القياس أن لا يصح ، لأَنه ذَبَحَ شاةَ غيره بغير أمره ، وجه الاستحسان : أنها تعينت للأضحية ويضمن ودلالة الإذن حاصلة ، فإنَّ العادة جرت بالاستعانة بالغير في أمر الذبح. وصحتِ التّضحية بشاةِ الغصب لا الوديعة، وضمنها) ؛ لأنَّ في الغصب والنهي عنه نهى عن البيع أيضاً ؛ لأنه في معنى البيع اهـ، ولا يخفى أنّ في كل من الحديثين دلالة على المطلوب من الموضعين من قوله : «من باع جلد أضحيته فلا أضحية له»). كما في «الهداية». [قوله : ويضمن ؛ أي يضمن كلّ واحدٍ منهما لصاحبه، وهو قول زفر ؛ لأنه متعد بالذبح بغير أمره، فيضمن كما إذا ذبح شاة اشتراها القصاب، والتضحية قربة، فلا تتأدى بنية غيره. [قوله : إنها تعينت للأضحية ؛ لتعينها بالأضحية، حتى وجب عليه أن يضحي بها بعينها في أيام النحر، ويكره أن يبدل بها غيرها ، فصار المالك مستعيناً بمن يكون أهلاً للذبح، فصار مأذونا له دلالة ؛ لأنها تفوت بمضي هذه الأيام، ويخاف أن يعجز عن إقامتها لعارض يعتريه. فصار كما إذا ذبح شاة وشدّ القصاب رجليها ، وكيف لا يأذن له، وفيه مسارعة إلى الخير، فتحقيق ما عينه ولا يبالي لفوات مباشرته وشهوده، لحصول ما هو أعظمُ من ذلك، وهو ما ييناه فيصير أدنى دلالة، وهو كالصريح ومن هذا الجنس مسائل استحسانية لأصحابنا. كذا في تكملة البحر. يثبت الملك من وقت الغصب، وفي الوديعة يصير غاصباً بالذبح في غير الملك. أقول : بل يصير غاصباً بمقدمات الذبح كالإضجاع وشد الرجل، فيكون غاصبا قبل الذبح. [قوله : فيكون غاصبا ... الخ ؛ وقد نقل الزاهدي الله عن قاضي خان له ما ذكره الشارح ههاهنا حيث قال : وقيل : ينحر إلا أنه ضمنها بالاضجاع والشدّ، وقد يتكلف في جوابه ما به وإن سُلّم أن مقدّمات الذبح قد تعدّ غصباً، لكن لا شك فيه أنه لا يتقرر قبل الذبح ؛ لأنَّ بعض المقدّمات كشد الرجل مثلاً يجوز أن يكون للحفظ الواجب على المودع ، فلا يتعينُ الغصب إلا بالذبح بخلاف ما إذا غصب أولاً ، فليتأمل. ولك أن تقول : الغصب عبارة عن إزالة اليدِ المحقة واثبات اليد المبطلة، كما تقرر في موضعه، ونهاية ما يتصور فيما ذكره الشارح الله هو الثاني، وأما الإزالة فلا يتحقق إلا بنفس الذبح كما هو مذهب الجمهور، والله أعلم. كذا في حاشية الجلبي
And if two people make a mistake, and each slaughters the other's sheep, it is valid without compensation. And the sacrifice with a usurped sheep is valid, but not with a deposited sheep, and he must guarantee it. (He said: valid without compensation) – by analogy it would not be valid because he slaughtered another's sheep without his permission. The aspect of istihsan: the sheep has been designated for sacrifice, he guarantees it, and evidence of permission is present because custom has established seeking help from others in the act of slaughter. And the sacrifice with a usurped sheep is valid, not with a deposit, and he guarantees it; because in usurpation and its prohibition, there is also prohibition of sale since it is in the meaning of sale. And it is not hidden that in each of the two hadiths there is evidence for the desired from the two places from his statement: 'Whoever sells the skin of his sacrifice, there is no sacrifice for him.' As in al-Hidayah. (He said: and he guarantees; meaning each of them guarantees to the other, and this is the view of Zufar because he transgresses by slaughtering without permission, so he guarantees as if he slaughtered a sheep bought by the butcher, and sacrifice is an act of worship, so it is not performed by another's intention. He said: it has been designated for sacrifice; because it is determined for sacrifice, such that he must sacrifice that specific one during the days of Nahr, and it is disliked to exchange it for another. So the owner becomes in need of someone qualified for slaughter, so he is implicitly permitted; because the opportunity is lost with the passing of these days, and he fears being unable to perform it due to an obstacle that may befall him. So it is like when he slaughters a sheep and the butcher ties its legs. And how would he not permit it, when there is hastening towards good in it? So he realizes what he designated, and does not mind missing direct performance and presence, because something greater than that is obtained, which is what we have explained. So the slightest evidence becomes like explicit permission. And from this category are istihsani issues of our companions. So it is in Takmilat al-Bahr. Ownership is established from the time of usurpation, and in the case of deposit he becomes a usurper by slaughtering in non-ownership. I say: rather he becomes a usurper by the preliminaries of slaughter like laying down and tying the leg, so he is a usurper before slaughter. (He said: so he is a usurper... etc.) And al-Zahidi has transmitted from Qadi Khan what the commentator mentioned here, where he said: It is said: he should slaughter, but he guarantees by laying down and tying. And one may be forced to answer that even if the preliminaries of slaughter are considered usurpation, there is no doubt that it is not established before slaughter; because some preliminaries like tying the leg may be for the necessary preservation of the depositor, so usurpation is not determined except by slaughter, unlike if he usurps first. So reflect. And you may say: usurpation is the removal of rightful possession and the establishment of wrongful possession, as established in its place. The utmost of what is imagined in what the commentator mentioned is the second, but removal is not realized except by the slaughter itself, as is the view of the majority. And Allah knows best. So it is in Hashiyat al-Jalabi.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning two individuals who mistakenly slaughter each other's sacrificial sheep. The main ruling states that the sacrifice is valid and no monetary compensation is required, based on istihsan (juristic preference) despite the analogy (qiyas) which would deem it invalid due to lack of explicit permission. The istihsan argument relies on the sheep being specifically designated for sacrifice, the custom of seeking help in slaughter, and the implicit permission inferred from the owner's eagerness to fulfill the religious duty. The text then distinguishes between a usurped sheep (ghasb) and a deposit (wadi'ah): sacrifice with a usurped sheep is valid because usurpation inherently entails disobedience, whereas with a deposit it is not valid, as the depositary becomes a usurper only at the moment of slaughter. The commentator clarifies that Zufar holds a different view, requiring compensation in all cases of unauthorized slaughter. The discussion continues about when exactly usurpation occurs: some argue that even preparatory acts like laying the animal down constitute usurpation, but the majority holds that actual slaughter is necessary to remove rightful ownership. The passage concludes with a reference to Hashiyat al-Jalabi, affirming the majority view.

Key Terms
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference that deviates from qiyas based on a stronger evidence (like custom) to avoid hardship.
دلالة الإذن (dalalat al-idhn): Implicit permission inferred from circumstances or custom, considered as effective as explicit permission.
الوديعة (al-wadi'ah): Trust (deposit) – property left with another for safekeeping; the depository is not permitted to use it without permission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, if two people mistakenly slaughter each other's sacrificial sheep, what is the ruling?
A) The sacrifice is invalid and each must pay compensation.
B) The sacrifice is valid and no compensation is required.
C) The sacrifice is valid but each must pay the value of the other's sheep.
D) The sacrifice is invalid but no compensation is required.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uses istihsan to override qiyas in cases where custom implies permission, ensuring the validity of acts of worship even when procedural mistakes occur.

When Does a Depositary Become a Usurper? Preliminary Acts vs. Slaughter
Commentary refining the definition of ghasb in the context of a deposit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi definition of ghasb (removal of rightful hand + establishment of wrongful hand) to determine whether preliminary acts of slaughter in a deposit constitute ghasb, and explain the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Ghasb requires both removal of the rightful hand and establishment of the wrongful hand; preliminary acts only do the latter.

This lesson unpacks a refined point in Hanafi fiqh: when does a depositary (mustawda‘) of an animal become a usurper (ghasib)? The source passage from Hashiyat al-Jalabi addresses whether acts like laying the animal down or tying its legs, before the actual slaughter, complete the act of ghasb. We will see that the relied-upon position distinguishes these preliminary acts from the decisive act of slaughter, based on the very definition of ghasb.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
The passage asks: Do preliminary acts of slaughter (e.g., laying down, tying legs) make the depositary a usurper of the deposited animal, or is ghasb only complete upon actual slaughter?
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute; some early authorities considered preliminaries sufficient.
2
State the Hanafi definition of ghasb
From the text: 'Ghasb is removing the rightful hand (al-yad al-muḥiqqah) and establishing the wrongful hand (al-yad al-mubṭilah).' Note that both elements must be present.
This definition is critical for analysis.
3
Apply the definition to preliminary acts
Tying or laying down establishes a wrongful hand, but does it remove the owner's rightful hand? The owner still has theoretical control; the animal is not yet seized definitively. Only slaughter (which destroys the property) removes the rightful hand completely.
Thus, preliminary acts alone do not satisfy both elements.
4
Address the counter-argument
Some early Hanafis (e.g., Qadi Khan's transmitted view) argued that preliminaries are ghasb because they violate the depositary's duty. The response: some acts (like tying) may be for the obligatory preservation of the deposit, so they are not necessarily usurpation.
The text says: 'some preliminaries... may be for preservation'.
5
Conclude with the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position (from Hashiyat al-Jalabi and the majority) is that ghasb requires actual slaughter. Only then does the depositary become a usurper retroactively from the start of the acts? No—the text says ghasb is only established at slaughter, but if he eventually slaughters, liability arises from the preliminaries? Actually, the thamarah: if he ties but does not slaughter, he is not liable for ghasb; if he slaughters, he becomes liable from the beginning of the acts (since slaughter confirms the intention to usurp).
Precise ruling: depositary is not a full usurper until slaughter, but if he slaughters, his ghasb relates back to the preliminaries for liability purposes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يثبت الملك من وقت الغصب، وفي الوديعة يصير غاصباً بالذبح في غير الملك. أقول : بل يصير غاصباً بمقدمات الذبح كالإضجاع وشد الرجل، فيكون (1) غاصبا" قبل الذبح" قوله : فيكون غاصبا ... الخ ؛ وقد نقل الزاهدي الله عن قاضي خان له ما ذكره الشارح ههاهنا حيث قال : وقيل : ينحر إلا أنه ضمنها بالاضجاع والشدّ، وقد يتكلف في جوابه ما به وإن سُلّم أن مقدّمات الذبح قد تعدّ غصباً، لكن لا شك فيه أنه لا يتقرر قبل الذبح ؛ لأنَّ بعض المقدّمات كشد الرجل مثلاً يجوز أن يكون للحفظ الواجب على المودع ، فلا يتعينُ الغصب إلا بالذبح بخلاف ما إذا غصب أولاً ، فليتأمل. ولك أن تقول : الغصب عبارة عن إزالة اليدِ المحقة واثبات اليد المبطلة، كما تقرر في موضعه، ونهاية ما يتصور فيما ذكره الشارح الله هو الثاني، وأما الإزالة فلا يتحقق إلا بنفس الذبح كما هو مذهب الجمهور، والله أعلم. كذا في حاشية الجلبي
Ownership is established from the time of usurpation, and in the case of a deposit, one becomes a usurper by slaughtering in non-ownership. I say: Rather, one becomes a usurper by the preliminaries of slaughter such as laying down and tying the leg, so he is a usurper before slaughter. [Commentary:] His statement: "so he is a usurper... etc."; az-Zahidi has transmitted from Qadi Khan what the commentator mentioned here, where he said: And it is said: he slaughters but he is liable for the guarantee due to laying down and tying, and one may be elaborate in answering it... Even if it is conceded that the preliminaries of slaughter are considered usurpation, there is no doubt that it is not established before slaughter; because some preliminaries such as tying the leg, for example, may be for the purpose of the obligatory preservation owed by the depositary, so usurpation is not determined except by slaughter, unlike the case where he first usurps. You may say: Ghasb is defined as removing the rightful hand and establishing the wrongful hand, as established in its place, and the utmost that can be conceived in what the commentator mentioned is the second, while removal is not realized except by actual slaughter, as is the madhhab of the majority. And Allah knows best. So it is in the Hashiya of al-Jalabi.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hāshiya) discussing whether the preliminary acts of slaughter (laying down, tying legs) constitute ghasb (usurpation) in the context of a deposit (wadī‘ah). The main argument is that ghasb requires both removal of the rightful hand and establishment of the wrongful hand; while preliminary acts establish a wrongful hand, they do not remove the rightful hand until actual slaughter occurs. Thus, until slaughter, the depositary is not yet fully a usurper. The relied-upon position (as stated in Hashiyat al-Jalabi) is that ghasb is only complete with slaughter, aligning with the majority (jumhūr).

Key Terms
الغصب (al-ghasb): Removal of the rightful hand (al-yad al-muḥiqqah) and establishment of the wrongful hand (al-yad al-mubṭilah)
اليد المحقة (al-yad al-muḥiqqah): The rightful possession or control of the owner or lawful possessor
اليد المبطلة (al-yad al-mubṭilah): The wrongful possession or control without right

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the context of a deposit, why does tying the leg of an animal not constitute complete ghasb according to the relied-upon Hanafi position?
A) Because tying is not a wrongful act in itself
B) Because ghasb requires removal of the rightful hand, which only occurs at slaughter
C) Because the depositary has permission to tie the animal
D) Because the animal is not yet killed

Key Takeaway
Ghasb is only established when both the rightful hand is removed and the wrongful hand is set; preliminary acts of slaughter do not complete the removal.

Unslaughtered Animal is Unlawful: The Ruling and Its Proof
Opening ruling of Kitab al-Dhaba'ih stating prohibition of unslaughtered animal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on unslaughtered animals, identify the Quranic proof, and list the three conditions for valid slaughter.

Core Idea
An unslaughtered animal is like carrion—it is haram because tadhkiyah is the condition for halal.

The very first line of Kitab al-Dhaba'ih declares the foundation of the entire chapter: an animal that has not been properly slaughtered (tadhkiyah) is unlawful. This may seem obvious, but it forces us to ask: what exactly makes slaughter valid? The Quran gives the principle; the Hanafi jurists unpack the conditions. In this lesson, we will examine the ruling, its evidence from the Quran, and the three essential elements of a valid slaughter. Understanding these will prevent the common error of thinking that any killing makes meat permissible.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling and its scope
The passage begins: 'Prohibited is an animal that has not been slaughtered.' Note that the term 'dhabihah' is used for the animal that is customarily slaughtered, not any dead animal. So the ruling applies specifically to animals that are normally slaughtered for food.
The scope excludes animals that are not customarily slaughtered, such as fish or locusts, which do not require tadhkiyah.
2
Identify the primary legal proof (dalil)
The author cites Quran 5:3, 'Except what you have slaughtered' (إِلَّا مَا ذَكَيْتُمْ). This is an exception from the general prohibition of carrion (maytah). In Hanafi usul, an exception establishes that the excepted category is lawful, and it implies a condition: the act of slaughter (tadhkiyah) is the necessary condition for halal.
The dalil is qat'i in transmission (thubut) but zanni in interpretation (dalala), yet the Hanafi position is clear.
3
Extract the conditions of valid tadhkiyah
The commentary lists three elements: the slaughterer (must be Muslim or Kitabi), the animal (must be of lawful meat), and the instrument (anything that cuts the jugular veins). Each is a necessary condition for the tadhkiyah to be valid and thus for the meat to be halal.
These conditions are derived from the Quran and Sunnah; they are part of the shurut (conditions) for tadhkiyah.
4
Understand the twin effects of tadhkiyah
Tadhkiyah makes the meat lawful (halal) and also pure (tahir). This is linguistically implied by the word tadhkiyah, which means purification. For example, 'the tadhkiyah of the earth is its dryness' (a hadith) refers to purification by dry earth.
Thus, tadhkiyah is more than just a physical act; it has a spiritual-legal effect.
5
Note the epistemological weight
This ruling is from the Zahir al-Riwayah and is agreed upon (muttafaq alayh) across schools. The proof is Quranic, with qat'i transmission though the exact implication is subject to interpretation, but the Hanafi position is clear.
There is no intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on this ruling.

Book of Slaughterings.
[1] (Prohibited is an animal that has not been slaughtered) By 'animal' is meant an animal that is customarily slaughtered, so as to exclude... [1] His statement: 'Book of Slaughterings'; it was mentioned after the chapter of sharecropping because in each of them there is improvement of something that cannot be benefited from by eating immediately for the purpose of benefit in wealth. As for the slaughterer, he must be a Muslim or a Kitabi (People of the Book), as the author said: 'And the condition is that the slaughterer is a Muslim or a Kitabi...' etc. As for the slaughtered animal, it must be of lawful meat; he alluded to this by saying 'dhabihah', meaning an animal that is customarily slaughtered. As for the instrument of slaughter, it is everything that cuts the jugular veins; he alluded to this by saying: 'And it is permissible by cutting...' etc.

[3] His statement: 'Prohibited is an animal that has not been slaughtered'; it is said in al-Hidayah: Tadhkiyah is a condition for the lawfulness of the slaughtered animal, because of His statement: 'Except what you have slaughtered' (Quran 5:3), meaning that slaughter is a condition for every animal whose meat is eaten, because of His statement: 'Except what you have slaughtered' after His statement: 'Prohibited to you are carrion and blood...' etc. He made an exception from the prohibition to that which is slaughtered, so it becomes lawful, because the ruling after an exception contradicts what precedes it; and because by it the impure blood is distinguished from the pure meat, and just as lawfulness is established by it, purity is established in edible and other things, for indeed tadhkiyah linguistically indicates that, from which is the saying: 'The tadhkiyah of the earth is its dryness.'
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on slaughter (Kitab al-Dhaba'ih) with the core ruling: an animal that has not been properly slaughtered (tadhkiyah) is forbidden. The commentary defines 'dhabihah' as an animal that is normally slaughtered, differentiating it from animals that die otherwise. It then outlines the three elements of slaughter: the slaughterer (must be Muslim or Kitabi), the animal (must be a lawful meat animal), and the instrument (must cut the jugular veins). The proof is from Quran 5:3, which prohibits carrion but makes an exception for what is slaughtered. Tadhkiyah is thus a condition (shart) for halal. The commentary also notes that tadhkiyah establishes both lawfulness and purity, and it linguistically implies purification, as in the prophetic hadith 'the tadhkiyah of the earth is its dryness' (meaning dry earth purifies).

Key Terms
ذبيحة (dhabīḥah): An animal that is customarily slaughtered, specifically the slaughtered animal itself (اسم المذبوح)
ذكاة (tadhkiyah): The act of slaughtering, which is the condition for lawfulness (shart ḥill al-dhabīḥah); linguistically implies purification
أوداج (awdāj): The jugular veins and other major blood vessels of the neck that must be cut for valid slaughter

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The Hadith 'The tadhkiyah of the earth is its dryness'
D) The practice of the people of Madinah

Key Takeaway
Tadhkiyah (proper slaughter) is the necessary condition for meat to be halal; without it, the animal is carrion and strictly haram.

Definition of Dhabihah: The Hanafi Scope of Slaughter Requirements
Clarifying the definition of dhabihah and its implications for halal/haram

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of dhabihah and explain why fish and locusts are excluded from the requirement of slaughter.

Core Idea
Dhabihah is defined by custom of slaughter, not literal slaughter; fish and locusts are excluded because slaughter is not their custom.

This passage from a later commentary on Sharh al-Wiqayah examines the meaning of 'dhabihah' in the prohibition 'forbidden is a slaughtered animal not slaughtered'. The commentator argues that 'dhabihah' is used metaphorically to refer to animals customarily slaughtered, thus excluding fish and locusts. However, the author of this hashiyah critiques this explanation, pointing out logical flaws and proposing a more precise definition. Understanding this debate clarifies the Hanafi ruling on which animals require slaughter and which are halal without it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling and its apparent scope
The ruling 'ḥurrima dhabīḥatun lam tudhakka' (forbidden is a slaughtered animal not slaughtered) appears to include any slaughtered animal, but fish and locusts are halal without slaughter, so a narrow interpretation of 'dhabīḥah' is needed.
The apparent conflict arises because fish and locusts are technically 'slaughtered' in a general sense but are halal.
2
Understand the commentator's solution: metaphorical interpretation
The commentator says 'dhabīḥah' here means an animal that is customarily slaughtered (sha'n), not literally any slaughtered animal. This excludes fish and locusts because slaughter is not their custom, and includes animals that die without slaughter (e.g., mutaraddiyah).
3
Analyze the first critique: redundancy of metaphorical meaning
The first objection: since fish and locusts are not slaughtered at all, they are already excluded by the term 'dhabīḥah' literally. The response is that the commentator intended to exclude cases where fish are killed by other fish, which would otherwise be included literally.
4
Examine the second critique: better phrasing needed
The author suggests the commentator should have said 'to include the prohibition of what is not slaughtered' instead of 'we carried it', making the metaphorical intent clearer.
5
Evaluate the third critique: problems with 'custom' definition
If 'custom' is linguistic, it would include ants (potentially slaughterable). If legal, it relies on blood drainage but fails to prohibit mutaraddiyah (animals that fall and die) and, via mafhum mukhalif, would imply dogs and lions become halal if slaughtered with tasmiyah.
6
Apply the resolution: restricting dhabihah to edible meat through slaughter
Some commentators resolve by restricting 'dhabīḥah' to animals whose meat is edible through slaughter, thus excluding predators, fish, and locusts. The author notes this is problematic because the Law also commands slaughter of inedible animals for purification.
7
Conclude with the Hanafi mufta bihi position
The operative Hanafi position is that fish and locusts are halal without slaughter; the prohibition applies only to animals customarily slaughtered for food. The definition of dhabihah must be understood contextually.

Source Text

ويمكن أن يجاب عنه : بأنّ مقصود الشارح له : إخراج السمك المذبوح بالسمك ونحوه، فإنّ القاعدة الكلية صادقة عليه مع أنه حلال، وكذا الحال في الجراد، وإن لم يأمر الشرع بذبحهما. والثاني : لما كان خروج السمك والجراد، ودخول حرمة المتردية والنطيحة

أي لم يذكر اسم الله الله عليه ، فلا يتناول حرمة ما ليس بمذبوح : كالمتردية) روح حتى النملة ؛ لأنه يمكن بالقوة أن تذبح بالسكين، وإن لم يوجد الذبح فيها بالفعل، وإن أريد ذلك بحسب الشرع.
بمعنى أن الشرع قد حكم بإخراج الدم المسفوح من الحيوان، فسلّمنا أن مثل السمك والجراد والنملة وغير ذلك قد خرج بقيد ؛ الشأن؛ على زعمكم، لكن تنتقض تلك العبارة بوجهين : أما أولاً ؛ فلان المتردية والنطيحة غير صادق عليهما ذلك الشأن بعد الموت ، وذلك ظاهر، وأمّا قبل الموت فاسمها الحيوان لا المتردّية والنطيحة، وبيانه: إنهما قبل الموت مختصان باسم الحيوان، وبعده باسم المتردّية والنطيحة، وليس شأنهما أن تذبحا في هذه الحالة، فلا تتناول العبارة حرمتهما.
وأما ثانياً ؛ فلأن تلك العبارة على هذا التقدير تشمل حل الأسد والكلب
المذبوحين بالتسمية.
وبيان ذلك : إن المفهوم المخالف معتبر في الروايات، فيكون معنى العبارة: هكذا يحلّ حيوان من شأنه الذبح شرعاً إذا كان مذبوحاً بالتسمية، فالكلب والأسد مثلاً إذا ذبحا بذكر اسم الله عليهما ينبغي أن يكونا حلالان ؛ لأنهما من شأنهما الذبح بحسب
الشرع ؛ لأن الذبح بالتسمية قد أوجب الطهارة فيهما في الشرع، وإن لم يؤكل فالحاصل إن الحرمة مختصة بعدم ذكية، فما من شأنه الذبح شرعاً يكون حلالاً
بالتسمية، بناءً على اعتبار المفهوم المخالف مع أنَّ الكلب والأسد حرامان اتفاقاً. وقال بعض الشارحين للـ«مختصر »: المراد بالذبحية : مأكول اللحم بالذبح ، فخرج سباع البهائم والطيور، وكذا أنواع السمك والجراد ، وكذا الكلب والأسد المذكورين ، ويمكن أن يقال: تخصيص الذبح بالأكل شرعاً ممنوع ؛ لأن الشرع كما أمر بذبح المأكولات للأكل كذلك أمر بذبح غير المأكولات للطهارة، فالفرق بينهما تحكم فليتأمل فإنّ ذلك دقيق لكتابه غفر له الله العزيز. [1] قوله : كالمتردية من تردّى في البئر إذا سقط فيها، أو من جبل فماتت
Husn al-Dirayah for the endings of Sharh al-Wiqayah. Fish and locusts; because slaughter is not their custom, and we carried it to that (i.e., metaphorical meaning) not to the literal meaning, because if it were carried to the literal meaning, the meaning would be 'forbidden is a slaughtered animal not slaughtered...' And it (slaughter) is optional, like a wound between the collarbone and the jaws, and compulsory, which is a wound in any part of the body. The second – meaning compulsory slaughter – is like a substitute for the first; because it is resorted to only when unable to do the first, and this is an indication of substitution. This is because the first is more complete in draining blood, and the second is less, so it suffices when unable to do the first, since obligation is according to capacity. His saying: 'Forbidden is a slaughtered animal not slaughtered...' etc.; because there occurred on the apparent universal expression: fish and locusts are not slaughtered, while they are permissible. The commentator answered that by saying that by 'dhabihah' is meant an animal that is customarily slaughtered, so fish and locusts are excluded because slaughter is not their custom. Thus the expression includes the prohibition of what is not slaughtered, like an animal that falls and dies, and also the prohibition of a limb cut from a living animal. So the derived (meaning) is metaphorical, not literal. When you know this, know that the commentator's expression is not free from flaw and unease, as he did not quickly use what is hidden from the minds of students from the intended meaning. Explanation of that has aspects: First: The intention is to explain the prohibition of an animal that has not been slaughtered legally, and there is no doubt that fish and locusts are not slaughtered, so they have been excluded by the restriction of 'dhabihah', so there is no need to intend the metaphorical meaning; rather, 'dhabihah' should be meant in its literal sense. It can be answered: That the commentator's intention is to exclude fish slaughtered by fish and the like, for the universal rule applies to it while it is permissible, and similarly for locusts, even if the Law does not command their slaughter. Second: Since the exclusion of fish and locusts and the inclusion of the prohibition of an animal that falls and dies depend on his saying 'custom', he should have substituted his saying 'we carried it' with 'and to include or cover the prohibition of what is not slaughtered'. Third: It is objected to his saying 'of the custom of slaughter', for the intended meaning of 'custom' is either according to language or according to Law. If intended according to language, this applies to every creature that has a soul, even an ant, because it can potentially be slaughtered with a knife, even if actual slaughter does not occur. And if intended according to Law, meaning that the Law has ruled the draining of flowing blood from the animal, then we accept that fish, locusts, ants, etc. are excluded by the restriction of 'custom' according to your claim, but that expression is disproved in two ways: First, an animal that falls and dies and one that is gored are not described by that custom after death, and that is apparent, but before death their name is 'animal', not 'fallen' or 'gored'. Explanation: They are before death characterized by the name 'animal', and after death by the name 'fallen' or 'gored', and it is not their custom to be slaughtered in this state, so the expression does not include their prohibition. Second, according to this estimation, that expression includes the permissibility of a lion and a dog slaughtered with the name (of Allah) because the opposite meaning (concept of opposite) is considered in narrations, so the meaning of the expression is: 'Thus permissible is an animal that is of the custom of slaughter legally, if it is slaughtered with the name (of Allah)'. So a dog and a lion, for example, if slaughtered with the name of Allah upon them, ought to be lawful because they are of the custom of slaughter legally; because slaughter with the name has necessitated purification for them in the Law, even if not eaten. So the result is that prohibition is specific to lack of slaughter, so anything that is of the custom of slaughter legally becomes lawful with the name, based on considering the opposite meaning, even though the dog and lion are unlawful by consensus. And some commentators of al-Mukhtasar said: 'By dhabihah is meant edible meat through slaughter, so predators among beasts and birds are excluded, and likewise types of fish and locusts, and also the mentioned dog and lion.' And it can be said: The specification of slaughter for eating legally is prohibited; because the Law as it commanded slaughter of edible animals for eating, likewise commanded slaughter of inedible animals for purification, so the distinction between them is arbitrary. So let one ponder, for that is subtle. May Allah forgive its author. [1] His saying: 'like an animal that falls' from 'taradda' into the well if it falls into it, or from a mountain and dies.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and ruling-oriented discussion from a later commentary (hāshiyah) on Sharh al-Wiqāyah, clarifying the term 'dhabīḥah' (slaughtered animal) in the context of prohibitions. The author begins by explaining that fish and locusts are exceptions to the general rule requiring slaughter because slaughter is not their customary method of killing. The commentator (shāriḥ) asserts that the word 'dhabīḥah' in the ruling 'ḥurrima dhabīḥatun lam tudhakka' (forbidden is a slaughtered animal not slaughtered) is used metaphorically, not literally. The literal meaning would incorrectly include fish and locusts, which are halal without slaughter. Therefore, 'dhabīḥah' refers to an animal that is customarily slaughtered, thus excluding fish and locusts. However, the author of this hāshiyah (likely named in the header as 'Ḥusn al-Dirāyah') critiques the commentator's expression, finding it flawed and confusing for students. He raises three points: (1) The intention is to prohibit an animal slaughtered without legal slaughter; fish and locusts are already excluded by the term 'dhabīḥah' itself since they are not slaughtered, so the metaphorical interpretation is unnecessary. The author then offers a defense: the commentator intended to exclude cases like fish that might be killed by another fish, which the literal wording would incorrectly prohibit. (2) The commentator should have used a clearer phrase like 'to include the prohibition of what is not slaughtered' instead of 'we carried it'. (3) A more serious objection: if 'custom' is defined linguistically, it would include ants (since they can be slaughtered), but if defined legally, it would consider the concept of 'blood drainage' which is not present in fish and locusts. Even assuming fish and locusts are excluded, the expression would still wrongly include animals that die from falling or goring (al-mutaraddiyah wa al-naṭīḥah) because after death they are not customarily slaughtered, and before death they are called 'animals'. Thus the expression does not prohibit them, contradicting the known ruling. Additionally, if the concept of opposite (mafhūm al-mukhālafah) is applied, the expression 'if slaughtered with the name (of Allah)' would imply that any animal customarily slaughtered becomes halal, including dogs and lions, which are unanimously haram. Some commentators resolve this by restricting 'dhabīḥah' to edible animals slaughtered for food, excluding predators, fish, and locusts. But the author notes that this restriction is problematic because the Law also commands slaughter of inedible animals for purification. The passage concludes with a definition of 'al-mutaraddiyah' as an animal that falls into a well or from a mountain and dies. The overall legal takeaway is that the term 'dhabīḥah' in the ruling is legally defined as referring only to animals whose slaughter is customary and whose meat is edible through slaughter, thereby preserving the correct prohibitions and permissions according to the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
ذبيحة (dhabīḥah): In the context of this passage, 'dhabīḥah' refers to the concept of a slaughtered animal. The commentator interprets it metaphorically to mean an animal that is customarily slaughtered, not literally every animal that has been physically slaughtered.
شأن (sha'n): 'Custom' or 'characteristic'. In the passage, it is used to determine which animals are customarily slaughtered. The commentator uses it to limit the scope of 'dhabīḥah' to animals whose normal mode of killing is slaughter.
المفهوم المخالف (al-mafhūm al-mukhālif): The opposite meaning derived from a conditional or restrictive expression. Here, from 'ḥurrima dhabīḥatun lam tudhakka' (forbidden is a slaughtered animal not slaughtered), the opposite implies that a slaughtered animal that is slaughtered is permissible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the term 'dhabīḥah' interpreted metaphorically in the ruling 'ḥurrima dhabīḥatun lam tudhakka'?
A) To include fish and locusts in the prohibition
B) To exclude fish and locusts since they are not customarily slaughtered
C) To make the ruling apply to all animals
D) To emphasize the literal meaning of slaughter

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defines dhabihah as an animal customarily slaughtered; fish and locusts are thus halal without slaughter, and the prohibition of un-slaughtered meat applies only to animals falling within that custom.

Conditions of Slaughter: Veins, Location, and Tools
Explains required veins, location above/below node, and permissible tools

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions for valid Hanafi slaughter and apply Ibn 'Abidin's tarjih to determine validity when the cut is above the node.

Core Idea
Slaughter is valid if three veins are cut with any sharp tool; the location dispute is resolved by actual cutting.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the essential conditions for valid dhabh. You will learn which veins must be cut, where on the neck the cut must be made, and what tools are permitted. We will also examine an intra-madhhab dispute on the location and how Ibn 'Abidin resolved it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Required Veins
List the four veins: windpipe (halqum), esophagus (mari'), and two jugulars (wadajan). Note the correction of definitions from al-Hidayah.
The halqum is the air passage, mari' is the food passage (opposite of al-Hidayah's error).
2
Apply the Majority Principle
Understand that cutting any three of the four veins suffices—the majority stands in place of the whole (iqamat al-akthar maqam al-kul).
Three is the minimum of the plural 'awdaj'.
3
Examine the Location Dispute
Note the two positions: (1) the matn and early scholars require slaughter below the node ('uqda); (2) al-Rustughfani and later authors (e.g., al-Haskafi) allow above if three veins are cut. Ibn 'Abidin reconciles: check if three veins are actually cut.
The node is the prominent lump on the upper neck (Adam's apple area).
4
Identify Permissible Tools
Any tool that severs the jugulars (afrā al-awdāj) and causes blood to flow is permitted, even a reed (laytah) or sharp stone (marwah). This is based on the hadith.
Teeth and nails are excluded by other texts (not in this passage).
5
Recognize the Proofs
The two hadith: 'Slaughter is between the collarbone and jawbone' and 'Sever the jugulars with whatever you wish' establish the location and tool permissibility.
The linguistic analysis of 'afrā' (cut for corruption) confirms the meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الذبائح. شروطها وما يحل ويحرم. والمريء، والودجان فلم يَجُز فوق العقدة. والمريء، والودجان. الحلقوم: مجرى النفس، والمريء: مجرى الطعام والشراب. وفي الهداية عكس هذا، وهو سهو من الكاتب أو غيره، فلم يجز فوق العقدة. والبعض أفتوا بالجواز. وحل بقطع أي ثلاث منها وبكل ما أفرى الأوداج، وأنهر الدم ولو بليطة ومروة. لقوله: «الذكاة بين اللبة واللحيين». وحل بقطع أي ثلاث منها، إقامة الأكثر مقام الكل، وبكل ما أفرى الأوداج، وأنهر الدم ولو بليطة ومروة، اللّيطةُ: قشر القصب، والمَرْوَةُ: الحجر الذي فيه حدة. وحلّ بقطع أي ثلاث منها؛ أي ثلاث من العروق الأربعة، قيل: لا يشترط خروج الدم ولا الحركة، لكن إن لم يعلم حياته يشترط أحدهما، وقال بعضُهم: الخروج للدم على كل حال، قال: «أفر الأوداج بما شئت من السكين وغيره. والأوداج جمع، وأقله الثلث، فيتناول المريء والودجين، إلا أنه لا يمكن قطع هذه الثلاثة إلا بقطع الحلقوم بالإقصاء؛ لأنّ قطع مجرى النفس أبلغ إلى حصول المقصود من قطع مجرى العنق. وبكل ما أفرى؛ يقال: أفرى الشيء بالفاء والراء المهملة: قطعه لإفساده، يقال: أفرى الذئب بطن الشاة، قال الكسائي: إفراء الأديم: قطعه على جهة الإفساد، وفرأه: قطعه على جهة الإصلاح. والمروة؛ أقول: قد صححها بعض شراح الوقاية بكسر الميم، ولم يذكروا غير ذلك. وفي الهامش: أن في هذه المسألة اختلاف بين علماء المذهب: فمنهم من أجاز مطلقاً سواء كان الذبح في وسطه أو أعلاه أو أسفله، وهو رواية الرستغفني وعليه مشى صاحب درر الحكام والملتقى والحصكفي في الدر المختار. ومنهم من جزم أنه لا بد أن يكون الذبح تحت العقدة، وعليه مشى المصنف والشارح وابن كمال باشا في الإيضاح والزيلعي في التبيين. وحرر ابن عابدين في رد المحتار المسألة فقال: والتحرير للمقام أن يقال: إن كان بالذبح فوق العقدة حصل قطع ثلاثة من العروق، فالحق ما قاله شراح الهداية تبعاً للرستغفني، وإلا فالحق خلافه إذا لم يوجد شرط الحل باتفاق أهل المذهب، ويظهر ذلك بالمشاهدة أو سؤال أهل الخبرة.
Book of Slaughter. Its conditions and what is lawful and unlawful. The esophagus and the two jugular veins; it is not permissible above the node. The esophagus and the two jugular veins. The windpipe is the passage of breath, and the esophagus is the passage of food and drink. In al-Hidayah it is the opposite of this, and it is an oversight by the scribe or someone else; so it is not permissible above the node. And some gave fatwa of permissibility. It is lawful by cutting any three of them and by anything that severs the jugular veins and causes blood to flow, even with a leetah (reed) and a marwah (sharp stone), due to his saying: 'Slaughter is between the collarbone and the jawbone.' It is lawful by cutting any three of them, establishing the majority in place of the whole, and by anything that severs the jugular veins and causes blood to flow, even with a leetah and a marwah. Leetah is the peel of cane, and marwah is a stone that has sharpness. It is lawful by cutting any three of them; i.e., three of the four veins. It is said: the exit of blood and movement are not required, but if its life is not known, one of them is required. And some said: blood exit is required in all cases. He said: 'Sever the jugular veins with whatever you wish of the knife and other than it.' The word 'awdaj' is plural, and its minimum is three, so it includes the esophagus and the two jugular veins, except that it is impossible to cut these three except by cutting the windpipe completely, because cutting the passage of breath is more effective in achieving the intended purpose than cutting the neck passage. And by anything that severs. It is said: he severed the thing with fa and ra: he cut it for corruption. It is said: the wolf severed the belly of the sheep. Al-Kisai said: the cutting of leather is cutting it for corruption, and he cut it is cutting for rectification. Al-Marwah: I say: some commentators of al-Wiqayah corrected it with kasrah on the meem, and they did not mention otherwise. In the margin: there is a dispute in this issue among the scholars of the madhhab: some allowed absolutely, whether the slaughter occurs in the middle, top, or bottom, and it is a narration from al-Rustughfani and followed by the author of Durar al-Hukkam, al-Multaqa, and al-Haskafi in al-Durr al-Mukhtar. And some were certain that it must be below the node, and followed by the author, the commentator, Ibn Kamal Pasha in al-Idah, and al-Zaylai in al-Tabyin. Ibn Abidin in Radd al-Muhtar refined the issue saying: the correct analysis for the matter is: if by slaughtering above the node, cutting three of the veins occurs, then the correct is what the commentators of al-Hidayah said following al-Rustughfani; otherwise, the correct is contrary if the condition of lawfulness is not agreed upon by the madhhab, and that is determined by observation or by asking experts.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the conditions for valid zabiha (ritual slaughter) in the Hanafi school. It addresses three key issues: (1) which veins must be cut, (2) the location of the cut (above or below the node on the neck), and (3) the tools that may be used. The base text states that cutting the windpipe (halqum), esophagus (mari'), and two jugulars (wadajān) is required. A mistake in al-Hidayah reversed the definitions of halqum and mari', but the correct definitions are: halqum is the air passage, mari' is the food passage. The ruling: slaughter is not valid above the node (the prominent lump on the upper neck). However, some later scholars gave fatwa of permissibility if three veins are cut, as summarized by Ibn 'Abidin. The madhhab is divided on this point. The passage then states that cutting any three of the four veins suffices, based on the principle that the majority substitutes for the whole. The tool must 'sever the jugulars' (afrā al-awdāj) and cause blood to flow; even a reed (laytah) or a sharp stone (marwah) is permissible, as long as it is not a tooth or nail. This is proven by two hadiths: 'Slaughter is between the collarbone and the jawbone' and 'Sever the jugulars with whatever you wish.' The commentary explains the linguistic meanings: afrā means to cut corruptively, and marwah is a flint-like stone. The Hanafi position is that the benefit of rapid death is achieved by cutting the air passage completely. The margin records a detailed dispute: some (al-Rustughfani and later authors) allow slaughter above the node unconditionally, while others require it below. Ibn 'Abidin reconciles by saying if three veins are cut above the node, it is valid; otherwise not, and actual determination depends on inspection or expert testimony. This passage thus provides the core conditions for valid dhabh.

Key Terms
الحلقوم (al-ḥalqūm): The windpipe, the passage of breath in the neck.
المريء (al-marī’): The esophagus, the passage of food and drink.
الوداج (al-wadāj): One of the two jugular veins in the neck.
العقدة (al-‘uqda): The node or protuberance at the upper part of the neck (Adam's apple area).
أفرى (afrā): To sever or cut, specifically for corruption (as opposed to اصلاح).
الذكاة (al-dhakāh): Islamic ritual slaughter that renders an animal halal for consumption.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The location is irrelevant as long as the animal dies
C) Whether three of the four veins are actually cut
D) The tool used must be a knife

Key Takeaway
Slaughter is valid if three of the four neck veins are cut with any sharp tool; the location dispute above or below the node is resolved by verifying that three veins are cut.

Valid Dhabh: Three Veins and Permissible Tools
States rulings and conditions for valid dhabh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the minimum number of neck veins required for valid dhabh and identify permissible tools according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Three veins suffice for valid dhabh; any tool that makes blood flow is permissible.

This passage from the sharh of al-Wiqāyah expands on the conditions for valid Islamic slaughter. It clarifies the minimum cut required and the types of tools that can be used, citing a hadith as proof. The author also addresses a scholarly difference regarding the necessity of blood flow.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on veins
Find the explicit statement that slaughter is permissible by cutting any three of the four neck veins. Note the legal maxim: the majority stands in place of the whole.
The four veins are trachea, esophagus, and two jugulars.
2
Understand the tool requirement
The tool must cut the veins and cause blood to flow. Examples include a knife, a reed (līṭah), or a sharp stone (marwah). The term 'afrā' means to cut destructively.
Tooth and nail are excluded by other narrations.
3
Examine the proof
The hadith 'al-dhabh bayna al-labbah wa al-lahyayn' indicates the location of slaughter. Although its chain is weak, it is supported by practice and other reports.
Consider the Hanafi acceptance of weak hadith when corroborated.
4
Note the intra-madhhab disagreement
Some Hanafi scholars require blood flow in all cases; others only when life is uncertain. The mufta bihi is that blood flow is required if life is uncertain.
This affects ruling on animals that appear dead.
5
Summarize the Hanafi mufta bihi
The relied-upon position: cut any three veins with any tool that makes blood flow; blood flow is required if life is uncertain.
This is the position for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
