[۲] وحل بقطع أي ثلاث منها، إقامة للأكثر مقامَ الكلّ ، وبكلِّ ما أفرى (۳) الأوداج، وأنهر الدم ولو بليطة ومروة) ، اللّيطةُ : قشر القصب، والمَرْوَةُ : الحجرُ الذي فيه حدة. 11 ] قوله : وحلّ بقطع أي ثلاث منها ؛ أي ثلاث من العروق الأربعة، قيل : لا يشترط خ خروج الدم ولا الحركة، لكن إن لم يعلم حياته يشترط أحدهما ، وقــال بعضُهم : الخروج للدم على كل حال ، قال : «أفر الأوداج بما شئت من السكين وغيره. (۲) والأوداج جمع، وأقله الثلث، فيتناول المريء والودجين، إلا أنه لا يمكن قطعُ هذه الثلاثة إلا بقطع الحلقوم بالإقصاء ؛ لأنّ قطع مجرى النفس أبلغ إلى حصول المقصودِ من قطع مجرى العنق. [٢]قوله : وبكـل مـا أفـرى ؛ يقال : أفرى الشيء بالفاء والراء المهملة : قطعه لإفساده ، يقال : أفرى الذئب بطن الشاة ، قال الكسائي : إفراء الأديم : قطعه على جهة الإفساد، وفرأه : قطعه على جهة الإصلاح. كذا في «المعراجية»(۳). (۳) قوله : والمروة ; أقول : قد صححها بعض شرّاح «الوقاية» بكسر الميم، ولم (۱) قال الزيلعي في نصب الراية (٤) : (۱۸۵) غريب، وإنما في الدارقطني من حديث أبي هريرة بعث رسول الله الله بديل بن ورقاء الخزاعي على جمل أورق يصيح في فجاج منى : ألا إن الذكاة في الحلق واللبة ، قال ابن حجر في الدراية (۲) : (۲۰۷) : إسناده واه، وقد أخرج ابن أبي شيبة في مصنفه (٤) : (٢٥٥ ، وعبد الرزاق في مصنفه (٤ : ٤٩٥) عن عمر عباس له كذلك موقوفاً. : وعن ابن (۲) قال الزيلعي في نصب الراية» (٤) : ۱۸۵): غريب، وفي مصنف ابن أبي شيبة»(٤ : ٢٥٥) : عن ابن مسعود قال : كل ما أفرى الأوداج إلا سن أو ظفر ، وفي «معجم الكبير»، و«الوسط»: قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: هل أفريت الأوداج : قالت نعم قال : كلُّ ما فرى الأوداج ما لم يكن قرض سن أو حز ظفر». (۳) ينظر: «ذخيرة العقبى» (ص ٥٧٠). :  نجده في المعتبرات من اللغات وقد أوردها صاحب «الدستور» في الميم المفتوحة قاله «الجلبي».
And it is permissible by cutting any three of them, and by everything that cuts the jugular veins and makes blood flow, even if with a reed or a stone, due to his statement: 'The slaughter is between the throat and the chest.' [2] And it is permissible by cutting any three of them, establishing the majority in place of the whole, and by everything that cuts the jugular veins and makes blood flow, even if with a reed or stone. Al-Liṭah is the peel of a reed, and al-Marwah is the stone that has sharpness. 11] His statement: 'And it is permissible by cutting any three of them'; meaning three of the four veins. It is said: the flow of blood and movement are not conditions, but if its life is not known, one of them is required. Some said: the flow of blood is required in all cases. He said: 'Cut the jugular veins with whatever you wish of the knife and other than it.' (2) Al-awdāj is a plural, and its minimum is three, so it includes the esophagus and the two jugular veins, except that it is not possible to cut these three except by cutting the trachea with the intention of killing, because cutting the respiratory tract is more effective in achieving the intended purpose than cutting the neck vein. [2] His statement: 'And by everything that cuts'; it is said: afrā al-shay’ (to tear something for spoiling), it is said: the wolf tore the belly of the sheep. Al-Kisā’ī said: ifrā’ al-adīm is cutting it for spoiling, and fara’ahu is cutting it for reform. This is in al-Mi‘rājiyyah. (3) His statement: 'and al-Marwah'; I say: Some commentators of al-Wiqāyah have corrected it with kasrah on the mīm, and it is not found in reliable lexicons, and the author of al-Dustūr mentioned it with fatḥah on the mīm, as said by al-Jalabī. (1) Al-Zayla‘ī in Nasb al-Rāyah (4:185) said it is gharīb, and it is only in al-Dāraquṭnī from the hadith of Abū Hurayrah that the Prophet sent Budayl ibn Warqā’ al-Khuzā‘ī on a grey camel shouting in the valleys of Minā: 'Indeed the slaughter is in the throat and the chest.' Ibn Hajar al-‘Asqalānī in al-Dirāyah (2:207) said its chain is weak, and Ibn Abī Shaybah in his Musannaf (4:255) and ‘Abd al-Razzāq in his Musannaf (4:495) narrated from ‘Umar and Ibn ‘Abbās similarly as a mawqūf. (2) Al-Zayla‘ī in Nasb al-Rāyah (4:185) said it is gharīb, and in Musannaf Ibn Abī Shaybah (4:255) from Ibn Mas‘ūd: 'Everything that cuts the jugular veins except a tooth or a nail.' And in al-Mu‘jam al-Kabīr and al-Awsaṭ: the Prophet said: 'Did you cut the jugular veins? She said: Yes. He said: Eat everything that cuts the jugular veins as long as it is not a tooth or a nail.' (3) See: Dhakhīrat al-‘Uqbā (p. 570). We do not find it in the reliable lexicons and the author of al-Dustūr mentioned it with fatḥah on the mīm, as said by al-Jalabī.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the ruling on valid dhabh. The author explains that the slaughter is valid by cutting any three of the four veins (trachea, esophagus, and two jugulars). This is based on the legal maxim that the majority stands in place of the whole. The tool used must be one that cuts and causes blood to flow, even if it is a reed (līṭah) or a sharp stone (marwah). The proof is the hadith: 'The slaughter is between the throat and the chest' (though its chain is noted as weak, it is supported by other narrations). The commentary further clarifies that it is not necessary for blood to flow or for the animal to move after slaughter, unless the animal's life is uncertain, in which case one of these signs is required. Some scholars, however, consider blood flow always necessary. The term 'afrā' means to cut in a destructive manner, distinguishing it from cutting for reform. The passage also includes references to various hadith collections evaluating the authenticity of the proofs.

Key Terms
أوداج (awdāj): Plural of wadaj, referring to the four neck veins: trachea, esophagus, and two jugular veins.
أفرى (afrā): To cut in a destructive manner, i.e., to tear or slit to cause blood flow.
ليطة (līṭah): The peel of a reed or cane, used as an example of a non-metal tool that can make blood flow.
مروة (marwah): A sharp stone used for slaughter, permissible as a tool.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the minimum number of neck veins that must be cut for a valid dhabh?
A) One vein
B) Two veins
C) Three veins
D) All four veins

Key Takeaway
For a valid Hanafi dhabh, cut at least three of the four neck veins with any tool that makes blood flow; blood flow is only required if the animal's life is uncertain.

Dhabh with Tooth or Nail: Hanafi vs. Shafi‘i
Presents the Hanafi position and Shafi‘i objection with Hanafi response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on slaughter with a detached tooth or nail and evaluate the Shafi‘i counter-argument using the hadith evidence.

Core Idea
A detached tooth or nail is a valid slaughter tool in the Hanafi school, but attached ones kill by weight and are invalid.

This lesson examines a classic inter-madhhab dispute: may a Muslim slaughter an animal using a tooth or nail that has been detached from the body? The Hanafi school permits it (though disliked), while the Shafi‘i school considers the meat carrion. We will analyse the key proofs from the Quran and Sunnah, trace the reasoning of each school, and understand the practical consequence of this difference.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the act (slaughter) and the tool (detached tooth/nail). What is the ruling according to Abu Hanifa and his companions? Is it valid, invalid, or disliked?
The text explicitly states 'يجوز' (permissible) but 'يكره' (disliked).
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Al-Shāfi‘ī holds that the slaughtered animal is carrion (ميتة). What is his evidence from the hadith? Cite the exact wording.
The hadith: 'كل ما أنهر الدم وأفرى الأوداج ما خلا الظفر والسن، فإنها مدى الحبشة'.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Explain why al-Shāfi‘ī interprets the hadith as a general prohibition of tooth/nail, whether attached or detached. How does he apply the principle of generality ('umūm)?
Shafi‘i usul considers the hadith as 'āmm (general) and binding.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school offers two responses: (a) a counter-hadith 'أنهر الدم بما شئت' (general permission) and (b) a specification (takhṣīṣ) of the prohibition to non-detached tooth/nail. Why do they limit the exception? What is the illa (effective cause) for the prohibition?
The Hanafis argue that attached tooth/nail kill by weight (ثقل), like a strangled animal. Detached ones are cutting tools (جارحة) like stone or iron.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference in daily life? Under which circumstances would a Hanafi butcher be allowed to proceed while a Shafi‘i would not?
The meat is halal for Hanafi but haram for Shafi‘i.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : إلا سناً وظفراً قائمتين ; يعني يجوز الذبح بالظفر والسن إذا كانـا منزوعين، حتى لا يكون بأكله بأس، إلا أنه يكره هذا الذبح ، يعني يكره هذا الفعل، وقال الشافعي : المذبوح ميتة ؛ لقوله : «كلّ ما أنهر الدم وأفرى الأوداج ما خلا الظفر والسن، فإنّها مدى الحبشة» (۳) ؛ ولأنه فعل غير مشروع فلا يكون ذكاة، كما إذا ذبح بغير المنزوع. ولنا : قوله : «أنهر الدم بما شئت، ويروى : «أفر الأوداج بما شئت، وما رواه محمول على غير المنزوع، فإنّ الحبشة كانوا يفعلون ذلك ؛ ولأنـه آلـة جارحة فيحصل به ما هو المقصود، وهو إخراج الدم، وصار كالحجر والحديـد بخلاف غير المنزوع ؛ لأنه يقتل بالثقل، فيكون في معنى المنخنقة.
His statement: 'Except a tooth and a nail that are not detached'; meaning it is permissible to slaughter with a nail and tooth if they are detached, so there is no harm in eating it, except that this slaughter is disliked, i.e., this action is disliked. Al-Shāfi‘ī said: The slaughtered animal is carrion, due to his saying: 'Everything that makes blood flow and cuts the jugular veins except the nail and tooth, for they are the knives of the Ethiopians.' And because it is an impermissible act, so it is not a slaughter, just as if one slaughters with a non-detached one. Our proof: his saying: 'Make blood flow with whatever you wish', and it is narrated: 'Cut the jugular veins with whatever you wish.' And what he (the opponent) narrated is interpreted as referring to non-detached ones, because the Ethiopians used to do that. And because it is a cutting instrument, so the intended purpose is achieved, which is the extraction of blood, and it is like stone and iron, unlike the non-detached one, because it kills by weight, so it is in the meaning of the strangled animal.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute regarding slaughter with a tooth or nail. The Hanafi position is that if the tooth or nail is detached from the body, slaughter is valid but disliked (makrūh tanzīhī). The meat is halal. If they are attached (non-detached), slaughter is invalid because the animal is killed by weight/pressure, not by cutting, making it like a strangled animal (which is carrion). Al-Shāfi‘ī holds that even detached tooth or nail render the animal carrion, based on the hadith: 'Every tool that makes blood flow and cuts the jugular veins is permissible except the nail and tooth, for they are the knives of the Ethiopians.' The Hanafi response is that this hadith refers to non-detached tooth/nail, as the Ethiopians used them attached. The Hanafis also cite the general hadith: 'Make blood flow with whatever you wish.' Since a detached tooth or nail is a cutting instrument, it achieves the purpose of extracting blood, similar to stone or iron. Therefore, the Hanafi school permits it, though with dislikeness to avoid resemblance to the Ethiopians' practice.

Key Terms
منزوع (manzū‘): Detached or removed from the body, as in a tooth or nail that is no longer attached.
مدى الحبشة (madā al-ḥabashah): The knives of the Ethiopians, referring to their practice of slaughtering with attached nails or teeth.
جارحة (jāriḥah): A cutting instrument or tool that wounds; used to argue that detached tooth/nail are tools like knives.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling on slaughtering with a detached tooth?
A) Valid and recommended
B) Valid but disliked
C) Invalid and carrion
D) Forbidden under all circumstances

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits slaughter with a detached tooth or nail because it is a cutting tool that makes blood flow, but the act is disliked to avoid resemblance to the Ethiopian practice.

Sunnah of Sharpening the Blade Before Slaughter
Recommendation to sharpen the blade before slaughter

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on sharpening the knife before slaughter and identify the proof from the hadith of ihsān.

Core Idea
Sharpening the blade before slaughter is sunnah, not wajib – the hadith of ihsān encourages excellence beyond obligation.

This lesson clarifies a common point of confusion: the recommendation to sharpen the knife before slaughter. While many assume it is obligatory, the Hanafi school holds it as a sunnah act. We will examine the textual proof from the famous hadith on ihsān and understand why the imperative is interpreted as a recommendation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
Recognize that the act in question is sharpening the blade (iḥdād) before laying the animal down (iḍjā‘). The text explicitly says 'nudiba' (it is recommended).
The ruling is sunnah mu’akkadah.
2
Locate the Proof (Dalil)
The text cites the hadith: 'Indeed Allah has prescribed excellence in everything... so when you slaughter, slaughter well; and let one of you sharpen his blade and give rest to his animal.' This is the primary evidence.
The hadith is sahih (authentic).
3
Understand the Wajh al-Dalālah
The imperative 'liyuhidd' (let him sharpen) is understood as a recommendation, not an obligation, because it appears in the context of ihsān (excellence), which is above the minimum requirement.
Hanafi usul: al-amr al-mujarrad ‘an al-qarīnah yufīḍ al-wujūb, but here the qarīnah of ihsān shifts it to nadb.
4
Recognize the Hukm Level
The hukm is sunnah mu’akkadah. Omitting it does not invalidate the slaughter but is contrary to the sunnah of ihsān.
No dispute within the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وندب إحداد شفرته قبل الإضجاع. [٢] قوله : وندب...الخ ; لقوله : «إنّ الله كتب الإحسان على كل شئ ، فإذا قتلتم فأحسنوا القتلة ، وإذا ذبحتم فأحسنوا الذبيحة ، وليحدّ أحدكم شفرته ويرح غداً
And it is recommended to sharpen his blade before laying down (the animal). [2] His statement: 'And it is recommended...etc.' due to his saying: 'Indeed Allah has prescribed excellence in everything; so when you kill, kill well; and when you slaughter, slaughter well; and let one of you sharpen his blade and give rest to his animal.'
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage states the ruling that it is recommended (sunnah) to sharpen the knife before slaughtering the animal. The proof is the well-known hadith: 'Indeed Allah has prescribed excellence (ihsān) in everything...' The hadith emphasizes kindness to animals by sharpening the blade to ensure a quick and less painful death, and giving rest to the animal (not sharpening in its presence). This is a ruling of istihbāb (recommendation), not obligation.

Key Terms
إحداد (iḥdād): Sharpening, specifically of the blade (shafrah) for slaughter.
إضجاع (iḍjā‘): Laying the animal down on its side for slaughter.
الإحسان (al-iḥsān): Excellence or perfection in action; the hadith commands doing everything with excellence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling (hukm) on sharpening the knife before slaughter according to the Hanafi school?
A) Wajib (obligatory)
B) Sunnah (recommended)
C) Mubah (permissible)
D) Makruh (disliked)

Key Takeaway
Sharpening the blade before slaughter is a sunnah act derived from the hadith of ihsān; it is not wajib but highly recommended for excellence.

Slaughter with Detached Tooth or Nail: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Explains the ruling and Shafi'i dispute on using detached tooth/nail

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on slaughter with a detached tooth or nail, identify the Shafi'i counter-argument, and explain the Hanafi response using hadith interpretation.

Core Idea
A detached tooth or nail is a cutting tool like iron; the hadith's prohibition applies only to attached ones.

Slaughtering an animal requires a sharp implement that cuts the throat. What if the implement is a detached tooth or nail? The Hanafi school permits it but considers it disliked, while the Shafi'i school declares the meat carrion. The disagreement hinges on the interpretation of a key hadith. In this lesson, we will unpack both positions and their proofs so you can understand the legal reasoning behind the ikhtilaf.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling from the text: slaughter with a detached tooth or nail is permissible but makruh tanzihi. The meat is halal.
The text uses 'قائمين' (detached) to specify the condition.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that slaughter with any tooth or nail, whether attached or detached, invalidates the slaughter and the animal becomes carrion.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Shafi'i proof is the hadith: 'ما خلا الظفر والسن فإنهما مدى الحبشة' (nothing except the nail and the tooth, for they are the knives of the Abyssinians). They argue this is a general prohibition.
'مدى الحبشة' refers to the practice of slaughtering with attached nails/teeth.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school interprets the hadith as referring only to attached teeth/nails, because the Abyssinians used them while attached. They rely on another hadith: 'أنهر الدم بما شئت' (cause blood to flow with whatever you wish), which is general and includes detached ones. Additionally, a detached tooth/nail is a cutting tool that achieves blood flow, like stone or iron.
The attached tooth/nail kills by weight, not cutting, thus it is like a strangled animal (munkhaniqah).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Hanafis consider the meat halal (though disliked), while Shafi'is consider it haram (carrion). This affects whether a Muslim may eat the meat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الذبائح شروطها وما يحل وما يحرم
إلا سنّاً وظفراً قائمين وندب إحداد شفرته قبل الإضجاع
٢٦٩
إلا سناً وظفراً قائمين ، أما إذا كان ملزومين تحل الذبيحة عندنا لكن يكره، وعند الشَّافِعِي ) الذبيحةُ ميتة ؛ لقوله : «ما خلا الظفر والسِّن فإنهما مدى الحبشة)، ونحن نحمله على غير المنزوع ، فإنَّ الحبشة كانوا يفعلون ذلك. ( وندب ) إحداد شفرته قبل الإضجاع
نجده في المعتبرات من اللغات وقد أوردها صاحب «الدستور» في الميم المفتوحة قاله
«الجلبي».
[١]قوله : إلا سناً وظفراً قائمتين ؛ يعني يجوز الذبح بالظفر والسن إذا كانـا منزوعين، حتى لا يكون بأكله بأس، إلا أنه يكره هذا الذبح ، يعني يكره هذا الفعل، وقال الشافعي : المذبوح ميتة ؛ لقوله : «كلّ ما أنهر الدم وأفرى الأوداج ما خلا الظفر والسن، فإنّها مدى الحبشة» (۳) ؛ ولأنه فعل غير مشروع فلا يكون ذكاة، كما إذا ذبح بغير المنزوع.
ولنا : قوله : «أنهر الدم بما شئت، ويروى : «أفر الأوداج بما شئت، وما رواه محمول على غير المنزوع، فإنّ الحبشة كانوا يفعلون ذلك ؛ ولأنـه آلـة جارحة فيحصل به ما هو المقصود، وهو إخراج الدم، وصار كالحجر والحديـد بخلاف غير المنزوع ؛ لأنه يقتل بالثقل، فيكون في معنى المنخنقة.
Book of Slaughterings: its conditions and what is permissible and forbidden. Except a tooth and a nail that are detached, and it is recommended to sharpen his blade before laying down. [269] Except a tooth and a nail that are detached. As for if they are attached, the slaughtered animal is lawful according to us but disliked. And according to al-Shafi'i, the slaughtered animal is carrion because of his statement: 'Nothing except the nail and the tooth, for they are the knives of the Abyssinians.' And we interpret it as referring to the non-detached, because the Abyssinians used to do that. And recommendation: to sharpen his blade before laying down. We find it in the reliable [sources] of languages, and the author of al-Dustur mentioned it under the open meem, said al-Jalabi. [1] His statement: 'Except a tooth and a nail that are detached' means it is permissible to slaughter with the nail and tooth if they are detached, such that there is no harm in eating it, except that this slaughter is disliked, meaning this action is disliked. And al-Shafi'i said: the slaughtered animal is carrion because of his statement: 'Everything that causes blood to flow and cuts the jugular veins except the nail and the tooth, for they are the knives of the Abyssinians'; and because it is an unlawful action so it is not a legal slaughter, just as if one slaughters with the non-detached. And our proof: his statement: 'Cause blood to flow with whatever you wish,' and it is narrated: 'Cut the jugular veins with whatever you wish,' and what he narrated is interpreted as referring to the non-detached, because the Abyssinians used to do that; and because it is a cutting tool, so the intended purpose—which is the extraction of blood—is achieved, and it is like a stone and iron, unlike the non-detached because it kills by weight, thus it is akin to a strangled animal.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the permissibility of slaughtering an animal using a detached tooth or nail. The Hanafi position, as stated, is that if the tooth or nail is detached (manzu'), slaughtering with it is permissible but disliked (makruh). The meat is lawful to eat. However, if the tooth or nail is attached to the body, the slaughter is invalid and the animal is carrion. Imam al-Shafi'i holds that slaughter with any tooth or nail, whether attached or detached, renders the animal carrion. His proof is the hadith: 'Everything that causes blood to flow and cuts the jugular veins except the nail and the tooth, for they are the knives of the Abyssinians.' The Hanafi response is that this hadith refers to the non-detached (attached) tooth and nail, because the Abyssinians used to slaughter with attached nails and teeth. The Hanafis rely on another hadith: 'Cause blood to flow with whatever you wish' (or 'Cut the jugular veins with whatever you wish'), which is general and includes detached teeth and nails. Moreover, a detached tooth or nail is a cutting tool, like a stone or iron, so it achieves the purpose of blood flow. The attached one, however, kills by the weight of the animal, making it akin to strangulation (munkhaniqah). Thus the Hanafis uphold the validity of slaughter with detached tooth or nail, though it is disliked.

Key Terms
منزوع (manzū'): Detached or removed from the body (said of a tooth or nail).
مدى الحبشة (madā al-ḥabashah): Knives of the Abyssinians; a reference to the practice of slaughtering with attached nails/teeth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The slaughter is valid and recommended.
B) The slaughter is valid but disliked.
C) The slaughter is invalid and the meat is carrion.
D) The slaughter is haram under all circumstances.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits slaughter with a detached tooth or nail, viewing it as a cutting tool, but considers the act disliked; the Shafi'i school prohibits it entirely, interpreting the hadith as a general bar.

Recommendation and Disliked Actions in Slaughtering
Explains recommendation and disliked actions in slaughter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on sharpening the blade before and after laying down the animal, identify the disliked actions in slaughter, and explain the rationale based on the hadith and the response to the objection.

Core Idea
Sharpening before slaughter is recommended; sharpening after, dragging, and back-of-neck cutting are disliked due to unnecessary pain.

The Prophet ﷺ commanded excellence in slaughter: sharpen the blade beforehand and give rest to the animal. This lesson examines which actions are recommended and which are disliked in the Hanafi school, based on the hadith and an incident where the Prophet criticized a man who sharpened his knife after laying down the sheep. We will also address a subtle objection about animal intellect and learn the underlying principle: anything causing extra pain not needed for slaughter is disliked.

Guided Path
1
Identify the recommended action and its dalil
Locate the phrase 'ندب... الخ' and note that the recommendation to sharpen before laying down is based on the hadith 'إن الله كتب الإحسان...'. The imperative 'وليحد' implies recommendation.
The hadith is from Shaddad ibn Aws, narrated by Muslim, Tirmidhi, etc.
2
Identify the disliked actions and their dalil
Find the phrases 'وكره بعده' (sharpening after), 'والجر برجلها' (dragging), and 'وذبحها من قفائها' (back-of-neck slaughter). Each is supported by reports: the Prophet's criticism of the man sharpening after, and the general principle of avoiding unnecessary pain.
Dragging and back-of-neck slaughter are disliked because they cause extra pain or fear.
3
Analyze the objection and its refutation
Read the objection: animals lack intellect, so how can they fear sharpening? Note the answer: even without intellect, animals have imagination (wahm) that suffices to perceive fear and pain.
The objection is deemed invalid (sū’ adab) because the hadith is clear.
4
Formulate the underlying principle
From the Hidayah quote: 'anything that causes extra pain not needed for slaughter is disliked (makruh)'. Summarize the lesson's core legal principle.
This principle applies to all actions during slaughter, not just these three.

And al-Shafar (with fat-ha on shin, sukun on fa, and raa): a great knife. In al-Mughrib: it is the wide knife that the butcher uses. The meaning here is the latter. [1] His statement: 'and disliked after...etc.' i.e., it is disliked that he lays her down then sharpens the blade, due to what is narrated from the Prophet that he saw a man who had laid down a sheep while sharpening his blade, so he said: 'You wanted to kill it with multiple deaths; why did you not sharpen it before laying it down?' narrated by al-Hakim in his Mustadrak. It means that the sheep dies when it sees the slaughterer sharpening the blade once, and by slaughter another time. He said in Dhakirat al-Uqba: It was objected: This only holds if the slaughtered animal has intellect, so that it understands that the sharpening is for its slaughter, but the matter is otherwise. The answer: This question that he raises against the hadith of the Prophet, despite being bad manners, is not valid at all; because imagination is sufficient for perceiving fear and pain, and intellect is only needed for perceiving universals, and our case is not among them. (2) His statement: 'and dragging...etc.'; the conclusion is that anything that causes extra pain not needed for the slaughter is disliked. (Hidayah) (5). (3) His statement: 'and slaughtering her from the back of her neck...etc.'; the pronouns refer back to the slaughtered animal mentioned at the beginning of the chapter. And the permissibility with dislike in slaughtering from the back of the neck is specific to when the animal remains alive.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage discusses the recommended and disliked actions related to slaughtering. The recommendation (nadb) is to sharpen the blade before laying the animal down, based on the hadith: 'Indeed Allah has prescribed excellence in everything... let one of you sharpen his blade and give rest to his slaughtered animal.' The hadith is narrated by the group except al-Bukhari from Shaddad ibn Aws. The term 'حداد' (hidad) means making the blade sharp; 'شفر' (shafar) refers to a slaughterer's knife. It is disliked (makruh) to sharpen the blade after laying the animal down, as the Prophet saw a man doing so and said: 'You wanted to kill it with multiple deaths'—the animal fears the sharpening and suffers mentally. An objection that animals lack intellect is dismissed: even without intellect, animals have imagination and feel fear. Additionally, it is disliked to drag the animal by its leg to the slaughter place, as it causes unnecessary pain. Likewise, slaughtering from the back of the neck (qafa) is disliked, though if done, the meat is still halal but disliked; this applies only if the animal remains alive after the cut from the back. The core principle is that any action causing extra pain not needed for the slaughter is makruh.

Key Terms
إحداد (iḥdād): Sharpening the blade; making it quick to cut.
شفر (shafar): A wide knife used by butchers.
الذبح من القفاء (al-dhabḥ min al-qafā'): Slaughtering from the back of the neck (cutting the spine from behind).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is recommended.
B) It is disliked.
C) It is prohibited and the meat is haram.
D) There is no ruling on it.

Key Takeaway
Sharpening the blade before laying down the animal is recommended; sharpening after, dragging, and back-of-neck slaughter are disliked due to unnecessary pain or distress.

Makruh Actions During Slaughter
Listing disliked actions during slaughter with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the makruh actions during slaughter according to the Hanafi school and explain the underlying principle that any unnecessary extra pain is disliked.

Core Idea
Any extra pain beyond what slaughter requires is makruh.

This lesson examines the actions that are disliked during slaughter according to the Hanafi school. The source passage lists specific acts – such as sharpening the knife after laying the animal down – and provides both a hadith proof and a general principle: any action causing unnecessary pain beyond what valid slaughter requires is makruh (disliked). The slaughter itself remains valid, but avoiding these actions is better and more merciful.

Guided Path
1
Identify the makruh actions
Read the passage and list all actions that the author states are disliked. These include: sharpening after laying down, dragging by the leg, slaughtering from the back of the neck, severe cutting of the spinal cord (nakh'), and skinning before the animal cools.
The author explicitly mentions each action with 'kariha' (disliked).
2
Locate the Prophetic proof
The author cites a hadith where the Prophet (peace be upon him) rebuked a man for sharpening his knife after laying the sheep down. Identify the exact quote and its source in the passage.
The hadith is a single (ahad) narration, but its meaning is clear and decisive.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's words 'You intend to kill it with multiple deaths' indicate disapproval (karahah). Sharpening after laying causes the animal fear (a form of death) and then actual death, thus extra pain without need.
This shows that the ruling of karahah is derived from the Prophet's explicit rebuke.
4
Understand the general principle
The author extracts a comprehensive rule: 'Everything that contains extra pain not needed for the slaughter is disliked.' Apply this principle to each of the listed actions to see why they are makruh.
This principle is based on the hadith and the general objective of mercy in Shariah.
5
Note the cross-madhhab status
The Hanafi position is that these actions are makruh tanzih (disliked but not sinful). Some other schools may not consider them disliked. The difference does not affect validity of the slaughter.
The meat remains halal in all cases according to Hanafis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكره بعده والجر برجلها الى المذابح، وذبحها من قفائها وكره بعده)؛ إرفاقاً بالمذبوح، والجر برجلها الى المذابح، وذبحها من قفائها. وقد روي عن النبي أنه رأى رجلا أضجع شاة وهو يحد شفرته، فقال: لقد أردت أن تميتها موتات، هلا حددتها قبل أن تضجعها. والنخع والسلخ قبل أن تبرد. ووجه الكراهة أن فيه زيادة الألم من غير حاجة فصار كما إذا جرحها ثم قطع الأوداج. والأصل الجامع في إفادة معنى الكراهة هو أن كل ما فيه زيادة ألم لا يحتاج إليه في الذكاة مكروه.
It is disliked to sharpen after [laying down] and to drag by its leg to the slaughter places, and to slaughter it from its back of the neck (qafa). And it is disliked after [laying down] as an act of kindness to the slaughtered animal; and dragging by its leg to the slaughter places; and slaughtering it from its back of the neck. And it has been narrated from the Prophet that he saw a man laying down a sheep while sharpening his knife, so he said: 'You intend to kill it with multiple deaths; why did you not sharpen it before laying it down?' And [it is disliked] severe cutting of the spinal cord (nakh') and skinning before it cools. And the reason for dislike is that there is extra pain without need, so it becomes like when one wounds it and then cuts the jugular veins. And the comprehensive principle for conveying the meaning of dislike is that everything which contains extra pain not needed for the slaughter is disliked.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage presents a set of actions that are makruh (disliked) during slaughter according to the Hanafi madhhab. The author first lists the actions: sharpening the knife after the animal has been laid down, dragging the animal by its leg to the slaughter place, slaughtering from the back of the neck (as opposed to the throat), severely cutting the spinal cord (nakh'), and skinning before the animal has completely cooled (i.e., before death). The proof for the first three is the hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him) who rebuked a man for sharpening his knife after laying the sheep down, saying he intended to cause multiple deaths (fear from seeing the sharpening and then the actual slaughter). The author then explains the rationale: all these actions cause additional pain beyond what is necessary for a valid slaughter. The general principle is established: any action that inflicts extra pain unnecessary for the slaughter is makruh. This principle is derived from the hadith and the general objective of mercy in shari'ah. The ruling of makruh applies to all these actions, but the slaughter remains valid (sahih) if performed, though it is better to avoid them.

Key Terms
النخع (an-nakh'): Severe cutting of the spinal cord (nakh') until reaching the white thread inside the vertebrae, causing rapid death.
المذابح (al-madhaabih): Places where slaughter is performed; specifically, the throat area where the jugular veins are cut.
الذكاة (adh-dhakaah): The ritual slaughter that makes the animal lawful to eat, which includes cutting the windpipe, esophagus, and both jugular veins.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because it makes the knife less effective.
C) Because it delays the slaughter.
D) Because it shows disrespect to the animal.

Key Takeaway
Actions that cause extra pain beyond what is necessary for valid slaughter are makruh, but the slaughter remains valid.

The Ruling on a Dead Fetus in the Slaughtered Mother: A Hanafi Intra-Madhhab and Inter-Madhhab Dispute
Intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab dispute on permissibility of eating the fetus

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the permissibility of eating a dead fetus from a slaughtered animal, explain the hadith evidence, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
The fetus is not automatically lawful by the mother's slaughter; Hanafis disagree based on conflicting hadith interpretations.

When a pregnant animal is slaughtered, what is the ruling on a dead fetus found inside? This question sparked a notable dispute within the Hanafi school and with other madhhabs. In this lesson, we unpack the positions of Abu Hanifa, his students Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, and the evidence each side relies upon. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your ability to weigh proofs and appreciate the range of valid scholarly opinion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested case
Recognize that the dispute concerns a dead fetus (not one that was alive at slaughter) found in the womb after the mother's ritual slaughter. The question is whether the mother's slaughter renders the fetus lawful (halal) or if it remains carrion (mawta).
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa (along with Zufar and al-Hasan) holds that the fetus is not made lawful by the mother's slaughter. It is carrion and thus haram. Understand his reasoning: the mother's slaughter only covers her own body; the fetus dies independently and is not included.
This is the position stated in the matn: "wa la yahillu janin mayyit..."
3
State the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that if the fetus is fully formed (tamma khalquhu), it becomes lawful by the mother's slaughter. They rely on the hadith: "The slaughter of the fetus is the slaughter of its mother."
The Shafi'i school also adopts this view.
4
Analyse the hadith evidence
Examine the hadith cited: the Prophet (peace be upon him) reportedly said, when asked about a fetus in a slaughtered animal, "Eat it if you wish, for its slaughter is the slaughter of its mother." The Sahibayn take this literally. Abu Hanifa may interpret it as referring to a fetus that was alive at the moment of slaughter or as an expression of the mother's slaughter covering her own parts, not an independent entity.
The Hanafi response to the hadith is that the fetus dies on its own (mawta) and thus falls under the general prohibition of carrion unless explicitly excluded.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
According to the text, the relied-upon position for fatwa is that of the Sahibayn: if the fetus is fully formed, it is halal. This is because the hadith is strong and the practical application aligns with ease. Note that this is a tarjih based on the strength of the dalil.
The text says: "the hadith evidence supports the Sahibayn."

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Book of Slaughterings / Its conditions and what is lawful and unlawful. It is possible to slaughter it, and a dead fetus found in its mother's womb is not lawful. It is possible to slaughter it (1)(2): this is according to us (Hanafis), and according to Malik (3), may Allah have mercy on him, it is not lawful except with optional slaughter. And a dead fetus found in its mother's womb is not lawful: this is according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, and according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and according to al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, if its creation is complete, it is eaten, and the slaughter of the mother is a slaughter for it. And the meaning of 'tawahhush' is: it became wild after being tame. And it is said: al-na'am refers to every tame animal, like the chicken, the pigeon, the camel, the cow, the sheep, and others. This is in al-Fawa'id al-'Arifiyya. [1] His saying: 'this is according to us...etc.'; because we say: what is considered is the reality of incapacity, and it has been realized, so recourse is to the substitute, i.e., slaughter of necessity, and we do not concede to rarity; rather, he is seeking. And Malik said: it is not lawful except with optional slaughter, not with emergency slaughter in both cases; because that is rare, and there is no consideration for the rare in legal rulings. [2] His saying: 'and a dead fetus is not lawful...etc.'; in Takmilat al-Bahr: meaning the fetus does not become slaughtered by the slaughter of its mother, so it is not lawful by her slaughter. And this is according to the Imam (Abu Hanifa), Zufar, and al-Hasan. And Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on them, and other groups said: if its creation is complete, its eating becomes lawful by her slaughter, due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'The slaughter of the fetus is the slaughter of its mother' (6), and due to his saying, peace be upon him, when it was said to him: we slaughter the she-camel and we slaughter the sheep and in its belly is a fetus, shall we throw it or eat it? He said: 'Eat it if you wish, for its slaughter is the slaughter of its mother' and because it is (v) (footnotes: (1) i.e., and its death is known by the wound or it is ambiguous; because the apparent is that death is from it, and if it is known that it did not die from the wound, it is not eaten. See al-Shurnubalaliyya (1:280). (2) Because emergency slaughter is only resorted to when incapable of optional slaughter, and incapacity exists in the second but not the first. See al-Durar (1:280). (3) See Sharh al-Kharashi (3:) (9), and Hashiyat al-Dasuqi on al-Sharh al-Kabir (2:103), and Manh al-Jalil (2:420), and others. (4) See al-Nukat (p.239), and others. (5) Takmilat al-Bahr (8:195). (6) In Sunan Abi Dawud (4:274), and Sahih Ibn Hibban (20), and Mustadrak al-Hakim (4:128), and others. (7) In Sunan Abi Dawud (3:103), and Sunan Ibn Majah (2:1067), and al-Muntaqa (1:277).
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage addresses a dispute within the Hanafi school and with other schools regarding the permissibility of eating a dead fetus found inside a slaughtered animal. The matn states that it is not lawful. The commentary explains that according to Abu Hanifa, Zufar, and al-Hasan, the fetus is not rendered lawful by the mother's slaughter. However, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) along with al-Shafi'i hold that if the fetus is fully formed, it is lawful because the mother's slaughter suffices for it, citing the hadith 'The slaughter of the fetus is the slaughter of its mother.' The Hanafi response to the opposing view is based on the principle that the mother's slaughter is only for her, and the fetus dies on its own (mawta), thus falling under the general prohibition of carrion. The passage also touches on emergency slaughter (dhabh al-istirar) when the animal becomes wild, comparing Hanafi and Maliki positions.

Key Terms
ذبائح (dhaba'iha): Slaughterings; the book dealing with rules of ritual slaughter of animals.
جنين (janin): Fetus; an unborn offspring in the mother's womb.
ذكاة (dhabh (or dhaka)): Ritual slaughter; the act of cutting the throat to make the animal lawful for consumption.
اضطرار (idṭirār): Necessity; emergency situation where one resorts to alternative slaughter methods.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, is a dead fetus found in a slaughtered animal lawful to eat?
A) Yes, if it is fully formed.
B) No, it is not lawful.
C) Yes, always.
D) It depends on the mother's consent.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has an internal dispute on this issue, but the relied-upon position allows eating a fully formed dead fetus from a slaughtered animal, based on strong hadith evidence.

Prohibition of Predators and Crawling Insects in Hanafi Fiqh
Ruling on prohibition of predators, birds of prey, and harmful insects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which animals are prohibited due to fangs/claws or harmfulness, and apply the exception for shooting a runaway chicken.

Core Idea
Predatory animals and harmful insects are haram; necessity permits alternative slaughter.

This passage from ‘Umda al-Ri‘āya details the Hanafi rulings on prohibited animals. It defines key terms like 'nāb' (fang) and 'mikhlab' (claw), provides the hadith proof from the Prophet ﷺ, and discusses an intra-Hanafi dispute about shooting a runaway chicken. Understanding these categories is essential for distinguishing halal from haram meat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling
Read the opening: 'Nor any possessing a fang or claw from a predator or bird, nor crawling insects, nor domestic donkeys and the mule.' Determine the hukm level for these categories.
The ruling is haram (prohibited).
2
Analyze the hadith basis
Locate the reference to the Prophet's prohibition: 'The Prophet ﷺ prohibited the eating of every predator possessing a fang, and every bird possessing a claw.' Identify this as the primary dalil.
The hadith is the core proof, transmitted through authentic chains.
3
Define key terms
Examine the definitions of sabu’ (predator), nāb (fang), mikhlab (claw), and hasharāt (crawling insects). Note that not every animal with a claw/fang is prohibited—only those that habitually hunt and kill.
The definition excludes grain-eating crows (halal by consensus).
4
Handle the intra-Hanafi dispute on shooting a chicken
Study the scenario: a chicken runs wild and is shot. If it cannot find its home, it is lawful. If it can, the ruling depends on where the projectile hits. According to Muhammad, only a hit on the throat makes it lawful; others allow it regardless.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school; the mufta bihi is the more lenient view allowing consumption if incapable of regular slaughter.
5
Apply to modern scenarios
Use the principles to classify cases: a lion (haram), a sparrow (halal if not a bird of prey), a gecko (haram as hasharah), an eel (jurrīth, haram).
The key is the animal's nature and harmfulness, not just physical features.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حسن الدراية لأواخر شرح الوقاية
ولا ذو ناب أو مخلب من سبع أو طير، ولا الحشرات ، والحمر الأهلية ، والبغل (ولا) ذو ناب) أو مخلب من سَبع أو طير ، ولا الحشرات (۱) ، والحمر الأهلية، والبغل جزء من أمه حقيقة ؛ لكونه متصلاً بها وحكماً حتى يدخل في الأحكام الواردة على الأم من البيع والهبة والعتق.
وللإمام قوله : إن الله حرم الميتة، وهو اسم الحيوان مات من غير ذكاة ، والجنين مات حتف أنفه، فيحرم بالكتاب، ويكره ذبح الشاة إذا تقارب ولادتها ؛ لأنه يضيعُ ما في بطنها. الدجاجة إذا تعلقت فرماها وأصابها ، ينظر إن كان لا يهتدي إلى منزله حل أكله ؛ لأنه عجز عن الذكاة الاختيارية، وإن كان يهتدي ذكر الفقيه أبو الليث : إن أصابَ المذبح حلّ، وإن أصاب غيره فعند محمد الله لا يحلّ، وعن غيره : يحلّ. [١] قوله : ولا ذو ناب...الخ ؛ هذا بيان فيما يحلّ وما لا يحل، اعلم أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم نهى عن أكل ذي ناب من السباع، وعن أكل ذي مخلب من الطيور، وكذا سباع الطير والبهائم لا كل ما له مخلب أو ناب.
والمراد بالسبع : كل مختطف منتهب جارح قاتل عادة ، ثم الاختلاب من فعل الطيور، والانتهاب من فعل السباع، والمخلب للطائر كالظفر للإنسان، والنابُ بالفارسي : . : جهار دندان شتر.
والحشرات هي صغار دواب الأرض، جمع الحشرة، كالنملة، والفأرة، والوزغة، وسام أبرص، والحية، والضفدع.
والجريث : بفتح الجيم، وتشديد الراء المهملة، وآخره ثاء مثلثة : نوع من السمكِ هو حرام عندنا، وكذا مارماهي. والانتهاب : غارت کردن والاختطاف ربودن، فالأول ينسب إلى كلاغ يشبه. اعلم أن الغراب على أربعة أنواع : نوع تأكل الحبوب فقط، فهو حلال اتفاقاً ؛ لأنه ليس من سباع الطير، ولا تأكل
Husn al-Diraya li-Awakhir Sharh al-Wiqaya. And not any possessing a fang or a claw from among predators or birds, and not the small crawling insects, and domestic donkeys, and the mule. (And not) possessing a fang or a claw from a predator or bird, and not the small insects (1), and domestic donkeys, and the mule is part of its mother in reality because it is connected to her and in ruling so that it enters into the rulings that apply to the mother regarding sale, gift, and manumission. And for the Imam (Abu Hanifa) is his saying: Verily Allah has forbidden carrion, and it is the name of an animal that died without slaughter, and the fetus died of its own nostril (naturally), so it is prohibited by the Book, and it is disliked to slaughter a sheep when its birth is near because it wastes what is in its belly. Regarding a chicken if it becomes wild (runs away) and he throws at it and hits it, it is considered: if it cannot find its way home, its eating becomes lawful because it is incapable of optional slaughter; but if it can find its way, al-Faqih Abu al-Layth mentioned: if the projectile hits the throat, it becomes lawful; if it hits elsewhere, according to Muhammad it is not lawful, and from others: it is lawful. [1] His saying: 'and not possessing a fang...etc.'; this is an explanation of what is lawful and what is not. Know that the Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibited the eating of every predator possessing a fang, and prohibited the eating of every bird possessing a claw, and similarly predators of birds and beasts, not everything that has a claw or fang. And the meaning of sabu' (predator) is every snatching, plundering, wounding, killing thing by habit; then plucking (ikhtilāb) is the action of birds, and plundering (intihāb) is the action of predators, and the claw for a bird is like the nail for a human, and the fang in Persian: the four teeth of a camel. And the small crawling insects (hasharāt) are the small creatures of the earth, plural of hasharah, like the ant, the mouse, the gecko, the house lizard, the snake, the frog. And the eel (jurrīth): with a fatha on the jim, tashdid on the ra, and ending with thā' muthallatha: a type of fish; it is forbidden according to us, and similarly marmahi (a type of fish). And intihāb: raiding, and ikhtilāb: snatching, the first is attributed to a crow-like bird. Know that the crow is of four types: a type that eats only grains; it is permissible by agreement because it is not among the birds of prey, and it does not eat.

Commentary
This passage details prohibited animals for consumption. It begins by stating that animals with fangs or claws from predators or birds of prey are forbidden, as are harmful insects. The commentary explains that the Prophet (peace be upon him) specifically prohibited eating any predator with a fang and any bird with a claw. The term 'sabu' refers to animals that habitually snatch, plunder, wound, and kill. The claw in birds functions like a human nail. The passage then defines 'hasharat' as small crawling creatures like ants, mice, geckos, etc. It also mentions the ruling of eels and a type of fish as prohibited. Additionally, it discusses the case of a chicken that runs wild and is shot: if it cannot find its home, it may be eaten due to necessity; but if it can, the ruling differs depending on where it is hit. The passage also mentions the dislike of slaughtering a sheep near its due date. Finally, it discusses crows, indicating that those that eat only grains are permissible by consensus.

Key Terms
ناب (nāb): Fang; a long sharp tooth used by predatory animals to kill prey.
مخلب (mikhlab): Claw; the sharp claw of a bird of prey used to grasp and kill.
حشرات (hasharāt): Small crawling insects; harmful creatures on earth.
سباع (sibā'): Predators; wild animals that hunt and kill.
جريث (jurrīth): Eel; a type of fish that is prohibited according to the Hanafi school.
مارماهي (mār māhī): A type of fish (literally 'snake-fish') prohibited in Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) A grain-eating crow.
B) A sheep slaughtered near childbirth.
C) An eel (jurrīth).
D) A rabbit.

Key Takeaway
Predators with fangs or claws and harmful crawling insects are haram; the hadith is the primary proof; necessity can permit shooting a chicken that cannot be captured for slaughter.

Ruling on Floating Dead Fish (al-Tafi) – Belly-Up Condition
Commentary on the condition for prohibition of dead floating fish

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a dead floating fish and identify the critical condition (belly-up vs back-up) that determines its permissibility.

Core Idea
A dead fish floating belly-up is haram; back-up is halal—the position indicates the cause of death.

The Hanafi school holds that fish are generally halal without slaughter, based on the hadith that two types of dead animals are lawful: fish and locusts. However, a dead fish found floating on water raises a question: is it still permissible? The commentarial tradition clarifies that the ruling depends on how the fish floats—specifically, whether its belly or back faces upward. This lesson unpacks that nuance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling on fish
Recall that Hanafis consider all fish halal without ritual slaughter. This is the baseline.
الجراد والسمك حلالان بلا ذكاة.
2
Note the exception for dead floating fish
If a fish is found dead and floating on water (طافٍ), it becomes haram because it likely died from an unnatural cause (e.g., poisoning or disease).
3
Understand the condition of belly-up
The prohibition applies only if the fish floats with its belly facing upward (بطنه من فوق). If its back is up, it is not considered 'floating' (ليس بطاف) and remains halal.
This condition is derived from the commentary in al-Tatimma.
4
Apply the distinction
When encountering a dead fish on water, check its orientation: belly-up = haram; back-up = halal. This practical rule avoids unnecessary prohibition.
The burden is on the one who claims it is haram to prove belly-up.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : لم يطفُ من الطَّفو أي لم يعلّ على الماء ميتاً حتّى إن طفي الماء ميتاً حرم، والجريث: نوع من السمك، وهو غير المارماهي. كذا في «المغرب»). ( وحل الجراد وأنواع السمك بلا ذكاة ...... قوله : حتى إن طفى....الخ ؛ هي إن طفى ميتاً حرم، وقد يشترط أن يكون بطنه من فوق، حتى لو كان ظهره من فوق أكل ؛ لأنه ليس بطاف. كذا في «التتمة»).
His saying: 'lam yatfu' from al-tafw meaning it did not appear on top of the water dead. So if it appears dead on top of the water, it becomes forbidden. And al-Jarith: a type of fish, and it is not the marmahi. Thus in al-Mughrib. (And the permissibility of locusts and types of fish without slaughter... His saying: 'hatta in tafa...' etc.; it is that if it appears dead, it is forbidden, and it may be conditioned that its belly be on top, so if its back is on top, it is eaten because it is not floating. Thus in al-Tatimma.)
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage from the commentary expands on the ruling of a dead fish found floating on water. The base text (al-Wiqaya) stated 'lam yatfu' meaning it did not float. The commentator explains that if a dead fish floats on the water surface, it becomes haram because it likely died from a cause other than being caught, and its floating is a sign of being unfit. However, a condition is derived: the prohibition applies only if the fish floats with its belly up. If its back is up, it is not considered floating (tafi) and thus is halal. This nuance is important practically. The passage also mentions 'al-Jarith' as a type of fish distinct from the common one, but the ruling remains generic.

Key Terms
الطافي (al-tāfī): A dead fish that floats on the water surface
الطفو (al-ṭafw): The act of floating on water

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is a dead floating fish considered haram according to the Hanafi ruling in this passage?
A) When its back is on top
B) When its belly is on top
C) When it does not float at all
D) When it is a specific type like jarith

Key Takeaway
A dead floating fish is only haram if it floats belly-up; otherwise it remains halal.

Ruling on the Abqa' Crow: Halal or Makruh?
Explanation of the fourth type of crow and its permissibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the abqa' crow and explain the reasoning behind the intra-madhhab dispute.

Core Idea
Not all crows are haram: Abu Hanifa allows the abqa' crow; the Sahibayn consider it makruh due to carrion diet.

In this lesson, we examine a specific intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the fourth type of crow, known as al-abqa'. Imam Abu Hanifa ruled it halal, while his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, considered it makruh. We will explore the reasoning behind each position and determine the mufta bihi (operative ruling).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
The passage mentions that the fourth type of crow is halal according to al-A'zam (Abu Hanifa), and makruh according to 'the second' (Abu Yusuf or Muhammad).
Note that 'al-A'zam' refers to Abu Hanifa.
2
Understand the basis for the second opinion
The second opinion holds it makruh because 'most of its food is carrion' (الجيف), implying impurity.
The reasoning (talil) is based on the bird's diet.
3
Note the preference for the first view
The commentator declares 'the first view is most correct' (الأوّل ما أصح), thus the mufta bihi is halal.
Tarjih is explicitly given in the text.
4
Recognize the nature of the dispute
This is an ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school (intra-madhhab), not between schools.
Both opinions are from Hanafi imams.
5
Apply the ruling
In practice, a Hanafi may eat the abqa' crow, but some may avoid it on account of the second opinion.
The thamarah al-khilaf is permissibility vs. dislike.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : والأبقع : - بالباء الموحدة والقاف - ، اعلم أن الغراب أربعة أنواع كما مر آنفاً، والنوع الرابع : حلال عند الأعظم الله ، يقال له بالفارسية: عكه ؛ لأنه كالدجابة، وعن الثاني : إنه مكروه ؛ لأنَّ غالب أكله الجيف، والأوّل ما أصح. كذا في «التبيين»، وفيه نوع مخالفة للعناية . قاله الجلبي
His saying: 'al-Abqa' – with the single ba and qaf – know that the crow is of four types as has passed, and the fourth type: halal according to the Great Imam, it is called in Persian 'akka' because it is like a fly?; and from the second: it is makruh because most of its food is carrion; and the first view is most correct. Thus in al-Tabyin, and there is a type of contradiction to al-Inaya. Said by al-Jalabi.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage discusses the legal status of a specific type of crow called 'al-Abqa'. The commentator notes that crows are of four types, and the fourth type is considered halal by Imam Abu Hanifa (referred to as al-A'zam). The reason given is that it is like a fly (or small insect) and does not primarily eat carrion. However, a second opinion (attributed to Abu Yusuf or Muhammad) holds it makruh because its diet consists mostly of carrion, which would make it impure and undesirable to eat. The text concludes that the first view (halal) is more correct. This is an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
الأبقع (al-abqa'): A specific type of crow, the fourth type, considered halal by Abu Hanifa
الغراب (al-ghurāb): Crow, divided into four types

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It eats mostly carrion
C) It is harmful to crops
D) It is a type of bird of prey

Key Takeaway
The abqa' crow is halal according to the preferred Hanafi view, despite a second opinion holding it makruh due to carrion diet.

Ruling on the Rabbit (al-Arnab)
Evidence for permissibility of eating rabbit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on eating rabbit and identify the two proofs (hadith and rational) that support it.

Core Idea
Rabbit is halal because the Prophet ate it and commanded others, and it is neither a predator nor a carrion-eater.

This lesson examines the permissibility of eating rabbit according to the Hanafi school. The passage presents both a textual proof from the Prophet's action and a rational proof excluding rabbit from prohibited categories. Understanding these proofs helps you apply the Hanafi principle that default permissibility is confirmed unless evidence shows otherwise.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The passage clearly states the hukm: 'there is no harm in eating the rabbit' (la ba'sa bi-akli al-arnab). This is a clear statement of permissibility (ibaha).
2
Locate the first dalil (naqli)
The text cites a hadith: the Prophet ate roasted rabbit when it was gifted to him and commanded his companions to eat. This prophetic action and command serve as evidence of permissibility.
The hadith is from Musnad Abi Hanifa and other collections; its transmission is zanni but the meaning is clear.
3
Locate the second dalil (aqli)
The text adds a rational reason: the rabbit is not from the predatory animals (siba') nor from the carrion-eaters (akilat al-jayf). These are categories that cause prohibition, so their absence confirms permissibility.
This is an example of talil – identifying the underlying reason for the ruling.
4
Understand the Hanafi principle
The Hanafi school holds that everything is halal unless proven haram. The two proofs here confirm that rabbit falls within the permissible default.
No disagreement within the school; the ruling is mufta bihi and cross-madhhab muttafaq.
5
Apply to modern scenarios
This ruling applies to farmed or wild rabbit, provided the meat is obtained through proper Islamic slaughter (dhabihah) or hunting. The proofs address the type of animal, not the method of slaughter.
Conditions for slaughter are derived from other texts, not this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : والأرنب؛ ولا بأس بأكل الأرنب؛ لأنّ النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أكل منه لما أهدي إليه مشويّاً، وأمر أصحابه بالأكل منه (٢)؛ ولأنه ليس من السباع، ولا من أكلة الجيف. قاله الجلبي
His saying: 'and the rabbit'; there is no harm in eating the rabbit because the Prophet (peace be upon him) ate from it when it was gifted to him roasted, and he commanded his companions to eat from it; and because it is not from the predators, nor from the carrion-eaters. Said by al-Jalabi.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling on the permissibility of eating rabbit. The commentator cites two proofs: (1) A hadith that the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) was given roasted rabbit and he ate from it and told his companions to eat as well. (2) The rabbit is not a predator (sabu') nor a carrion-eater, so it is not among the animals that are considered impure or harmful. Therefore, it is halal without any dislike. This ruling is consistent with the general Hanafi principle that animals are halal unless proven otherwise.

Key Terms
السباع (al-sibā'): Predatory animals that hunt with fangs
أكلة الجيف (akilat al-jayf): Animals that eat carrion

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary proof for the permissibility of eating rabbit in this passage?
A) Qiyas on chicken
B) The Prophet ate roasted rabbit and ordered his companions to eat
C) It is a plant-eating animal
D) Consensus of companions

Key Takeaway
Rabbit meat is halal in the Hanafi school based on prophetic action and rational exclusion from prohibited categories.

Ruling on the Magpie and Field Crow
Ruling on permissibility of magpie and field crow subject to slaughter

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the magpie and field crow and identify the condition required for them to be halal.

Core Idea
The magpie and field crow are halal only when properly slaughtered.

This brief commentary excerpt clarifies that specific birds often mistaken for crows are actually permissible. The key condition is that they must be slaughtered (dhabh). This distinguishes them from other crows and emphasizes the importance of proper method.

Guided Path
1
Identify the animals
The passage mentions two specific birds: the magpie (العقعق) and the field crow (غراب الزرع). These are distinct from other crow species.
2
Recognize the ruling
The ruling is that these birds are halal (permissible). This is stated implicitly by including them in the context of animals that require slaughter.
3
Note the condition
The phrase 'معها أي مع الذكاة' indicates that the permissibility depends on proper Islamic slaughter (dhabh). Without it, they are not halal.
Slaughter must be performed by a Muslim or ahl al-kitab.
4
Understand the implication
If not slaughtered, consumption is haram. This condition distinguishes these birds from locusts and fish, which do not require slaughter.

This brief phrase from the commentary indicates that the field crow (ghurab al-zar'), the rabbit (which was already discussed), and the magpie (aq'aq) are all permissible when slaughtered. The phrase 'معها' means 'with them', referring back to the requirement of slaughter. Thus, these birds and animals are halal only if properly slaughtered according to Islamic law. This distinguishes them from locusts and fish, which do not require slaughter.

8 min
Definition of Makruh and Its Two Categories: Tahrimi vs Tanzihi
Defines term makruh and its legal weight in Hanafi school

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between makruh tahrimi and makruh tanzihi according to the Hanafi school and explain the relationship between makruh and haram.

Core Idea
Makruh is divided into two: prohibitively disliked (tahrimi) akin to haram, and lightly disliked (tanzihi) permissible but better avoided.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks the term 'al-makruh' as used by Imam Muhammad. You'll learn why he equates makruh with haram, but later scholars refined this to two distinct categories based on the strength of the textual evidence. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly assessing the legal weight of actions in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the matn's claim
State what Imam Muhammad says about makruh and the reason he uses the term 'haram'.
The matn: 'ما كره حرام عند محمد' – What is disliked is forbidden according to Muhammad.
2
Separate the two categories
Extract from the sharh the definitions of makruh tahrimi and makruh tanzihi.
Tahrimi: must be left like haram, based on speculative evidence. Tanzihi: permissible but better avoided.
3
Compare the proof strength
Understand why haram requires definitive evidence while tahrimi requires speculative evidence.
Definitive proof (qat'i) = haram; speculative (zanni) = makruh tahrimi.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Note how Imam Muhammad's usage differs from the later relied-upon position.
Imam Muhammad applied 'makruh' to acts that were actually haram in his terminology, but later scholars refined this.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Identify the operative ruling for fatwa according to later Hanafi authorities.
The relied-upon position is the distinction between tahrimi and tanzihi, as clarified by al-Taftazani.

The Book of Disliked Acts. [1] What is disliked is forbidden according to Muhammad, but he did not express it [as forbidden] due to the absence of a definitive text. So the relationship of the disliked to the forbidden is like that of the obligatory (wajib) to the obligatory (fard). Source: 1] His saying: 'The Book of Disliked Acts' – linguistically it is the opposite of contentment and will, and it is karaha and karahiyyah as substitutes and anomalies. In the terminology of jurists, it is derived from the author's statement: 'What is disliked is forbidden...' etc. He titled the book with 'karahiyyah' even though it also discusses what is not disliked, because the subject of what is disliked is more important due to the necessity of avoiding it. He placed the Book of Disliked Acts after the chapter on sacrifice (udhiyah) because many issues in each involve dislike. Do you not see that in the timing of sacrifice during the nights of the days of slaughter, and in dealing with the sacrificial animal (shearing wool, milking, etc.) dislike is realized? And in the Book of Disliked Acts as well. [2] His saying: 'What is disliked is forbidden according to Muhammad...' is supported by what is understood from al-Tawdih of the commentator and al-Talwih of al-Taftazani: that what is prohibitively disliked (makruh tahrimi) is not permissible to do, rather it must be left, like the forbidden; except that the prohibition of an act by definitive evidence is forbidden, and by speculative evidence is prohibitively disliked. As for the lightly disliked (makruh tanzihi): it is permissible to do and is not prevented. So what Muhammad, may Allah bless him, stated that every disliked thing is forbidden – that is the lightly disliked (makruh tanzihi) according to Muhammad, and it is not forbidden. So consider the phrase of the Mufti of the Two Weighty Ones in the chapter on division of spoils.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage opens the Book of Disliked Acts (Kitab al-Karahiyyah) from the commentary. It first presents the matn: 'What is disliked is forbidden according to Muhammad, and he did not say it explicitly due to the absence of a definitive text. So the proportion of disliked to forbidden is like that of wajib to fard.' The sharh then explains that linguistically 'karahiyyah' is the opposite of contentment; in Hanafi legal terminology, the author intended a specific definition. The commentator justifies placing this book after the chapter on sacrifice, noting that many issues in sacrifice involve dislike. Then the sharh clarifies the two categories: (1) Makruh Tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) – must be left, like haram, but proven by speculative evidence; (2) Makruh Tanzihi (lightly disliked) – permissible to do but better avoided. The statement of Imam Muhammad that 'every makruh is haram' refers to makruh tanzihi in his terminology, not actual haram. This is a key distinction in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
المكروه (al-makruh): In this passage: what is disliked; divided into prohibitively disliked (makruh tahrimi) akin to haram, and lightly disliked (makruh tanzihi) permissible but better avoided.
الكراهية (al-karahiyyah): The book title; linguistically opposite of contentment; technically the study of disliked actions.
المكروه كراهة تحريمية (al-makruh karahah tahrimiyyah): Prohibitively disliked: an act that must be left like haram, but based on speculative evidence.
المكروه كراهة تنزيهية (al-makruh karahah tanzihiyyah): Lightly disliked: an act permissible to do but better avoided; no sin.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as explained in this passage, what is the difference between makruh tahrimi and makruh tanzihi?
A) Makruh tahrimi is haram, tanzihi is makruh.
B) Makruh tahrimi is based on definitive evidence, tanzihi on speculative.
C) Makruh tahrimi must be left, tanzihi is permissible but better avoided.
D) Both are unlawful.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes two categories of makruh: tahrimi (prohibitively disliked, akin to haram) and tanzihi (lightly disliked, permissible but discouraged).

Definition of Makruh and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the status of makruh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the positions of Imam Muhammad and the Sahibayn on makruh, and identify the two categories of makruh in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
Makruh is not haram, but its proximity to haram depends on the strength of evidence—a key Hanafi distinction.

This passage opens Kitab al-Karahiyah by defining the term 'makruh' and revealing an early intra-Hanafi difference on its legal weight. You will learn why Imam Muhammad equated makruh with haram in a general sense while the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) considered it 'closer to haram.' This distinction forms the basis of the Hanafi subdivision of makruh into tahrimi and tanzihi.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties
The passage presents two views: that of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani and that of the Sahibayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf). Recognize that both are Hanafi authorities.
Imam Muhammad is the student of Abu Hanifa and a major transmitter of the school.
2
Understand Imam Muhammad's position
He stated 'what is disliked is haram' but did not literally use the word 'haram' in his writings due to the absence of a definitive (qati) text. He analogized the relationship of makruh to haram as similar to that of wajib to fard.
This indicates that for Muhammad, makruh is a step below haram, just as wajib is a step below fard.
3
Understand the Sahibayn's position
For the two shaykhs, makruh is not haram but is closer to haram (ila al-haram aqrab). They give precedence to prohibition when evidence for halal and haram conflict, citing the principle attributed to Ibn Mas'ud: 'When the permissible and the forbidden come together, the forbidden overcomes the permissible.'
This reflects a cautious approach in the face of conflicting evidence.
4
Recognize the resolution through categorization
The commentator introduces the two categories of makruh: makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) and makruh tanzihi (lightly disliked). Makruh tahrimi requires avoidance and is close to haram; makruh tanzihi is permissible to do but better to leave.
Imam Muhammad's equation applies to makruh tanzihi, while the Sahibayn's 'closer to haram' refers to makruh tahrimi.
5
Apply the distinction in practice
For matters explicitly ruled in texts, follow the explicit ruling. For unstated matters, the Hanafi approach is: for permissible, say 'la ba's'; for prohibited, say 'akrah' or 'la yu'kal'. This guides muftis in issuing fatwas.
The practical impact is on how a mufti communicates the ruling to a layperson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الكراهية. [١] ما كره حرام عند محمد ولم يلفظ به لعدم النص القاطع، فنسبة المكروه إلى الحرام كنسبة الواجب إلى الفرض. قوله: ما كره حرام عند محمد ... الخ، يؤيده ما استفيد من «توضيح» الشارح، و«تلويح التفتازاني له من أن المكروه تحريماً ما لا يجوز فعله، بل يجب تركه كالحرام، إلا أن النهي عن الفعلِ بدليل قطعي حرام، وبدليل ظنّي مكروه كراهة تحريمية. وأما المكروه كراهةً تنزيهية: ما يجوز فعله ولا يمنع عنه، فما نص محمد من أن كل مكروه حرام، هو المكروه كراهة تنزيهية. عند محمد، وليس بحرام. [٢] وعندهما إلى الحرام أقرب. قوله: وعندهما...الخ؛ لتعارض الأدلة فيه، وتغليب جانب الحرمة؛ لقوله: ما اجتمع الحلال والحرام إلا وقد غلب الحرام الحلال، قالوا معناه: دليل الحل ودليل الحرمة. كذا في «الاختيار». القول في المنصوص بالتحريم والتحليل، وفي غير المنصوص يقول في الحل: لا بأس، وفي الحرمة: أكره، أو لم يؤكل.
Book of Disliked Acts. [1] What is disliked is haram according to Muhammad, and he did not utter it [as haram] due to the absence of a definitive text. So the ratio of the disliked to the forbidden is like the ratio of the obligatory to the compulsory. He said: 'What is disliked is haram according to Muhammad...' etc., supported by what is understood from the clarification of the commentator and the Talwih of al-Taftazani, that prohibitively disliked is that which is not permissible to do, rather it is obligatory to leave like the forbidden, except that prohibition of the act by a definitive proof is haram, and by a probable proof is prohibitively disliked. As for lightly disliked: it is permissible to do and not prevented; so what Muhammad explicitly stated that every disliked is haram refers to lightly disliked. According to Muhammad, it is not haram. [2] And according to the two [Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf], it is closer to haram. He said: 'And according to the two...' etc., due to conflicting evidence and giving precedence to the side of prohibition, because of his statement: 'When the permissible and the forbidden come together, the forbidden overcomes the permissible.' They said its meaning is: evidence of permissibility and evidence of prohibition. Thus in al-Ikhtiyar. The statement regarding what is explicitly mentioned by prohibition and permissibility, and regarding what is not explicitly mentioned, he says regarding permissibility: 'there is no harm', and regarding prohibition: 'I dislike it' or 'it is not eaten.'
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage opens the Book of Karahiyah (Disliked Acts) by defining the term 'makruh' and establishing its relationship to 'haram' according to the early Hanafi masters. The student should first note that the author is commenting on a matn (likely al-Wiqayah) that states: 'What is disliked is haram according to Muhammad.' This is Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani's position. However, the author immediately clarifies that Muhammad did not literally use the word 'haram' for makruh in his writings because there is no definitive text (nass qati) to support calling it haram. Instead, he draws an analogy: the relationship of makruh to haram is like the relationship of wajib (necessary) to fard (obligatory). Just as wajib is a step below fard in obligation, so makruh (according to Muhammad) is a step below haram in prohibition. The commentary then distinguishes two categories of makruh: makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) and makruh tanzihi (lightly disliked). Makruh tahrimi is what is forbidden by a probable (zanni) proof; one must avoid it, and it is close to haram. Makruh tanzihi is what is not forbidden; it is permissible to do but better to leave. Imam Muhammad's equation of makruh with haram is explained as referring to makruh tanzihi—he called it haram in a general sense, but it is not actually haram. The second part presents the view of the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf): for them, makruh is not haram, but it is closer to haram (i.e., makruh tahrimi). This is because when evidence is conflicting, they give precedence to prohibition, based on a saying attributed to Ibn Mas'ud: 'When the permissible and the forbidden come together, the forbidden overcomes the permissible.' The practical consequence: for matters explicitly ruled in the texts, one follows the explicit ruling (halal or haram). For matters not explicitly mentioned, the Hanafi approach is: for permissible things, say 'la ba's' (no harm); for prohibited things, say 'akrah' (I dislike it) or 'la yu'kal' (it is not eaten).

Key Terms
كراهية (karāhiyah): The book title; linguistically opposite of approval and desire; in Hanafi terminology, it denotes an act that is disliked but not necessarily forbidden.
مكروه (makrūh): An act that is disliked; according to Muhammad, it is equated with haram in a general sense but not technically; according to the two shaykhs, it is closer to haram.
كراهة تحريمية (karāhat taḥrīmiyyah): A level of dislike where the act is prohibited by a probable (zanni) proof; one must avoid it, and it is similar to haram in obligating avoidance.
كراهة تنزيهية (karāhat tanzīhiyyah): A level of dislike where the act is not prohibited but is better to leave; it is permissible and does not incur sin.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is exactly haram.
B) It is not haram but closer to haram.
C) It is completely permissible.
D) It is wajib to avoid.

Key Takeaway
Makruh in Hanafi fiqh is not a single level; it is divided into makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked, close to haram) and makruh tanzihi (lightly disliked, permissible), resolving an early difference between Imam Muhammad and the Sahibayn.
