Two Types of Makruh: Tahrimi and Tanzihi
Defining and distinguishing two categories of disliked actions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between makruh tahrimi and makruh tanzihi, and state the intra-Hanafi difference on their definitions.

Core Idea
Makruh tahrimi is near haram; makruh tanzihi is merely discouraged.

This passage from a later Hanafi hashia refines the definition of makruh, which is a distinct legal category in the school. Unlike other schools that treat makruh as one type, Hanafi law splits it into two: tahrimi (prohibitional disliked) and tanzihi (discommendational disliked). The distinction has practical implications for whether an action's doer deserves deterrence or just a mild reproach.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
Find where the passage defines makruh tahrimi and makruh tanzihi. Note that tahrimi is 'not haram but closer to haram' and tanzihi is 'closer to halal'.
2
Note the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf
Observe the difference between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (makruh tahrimi is closer to haram) and Muhammad al-Shaybani (makruh is merely abandonment better, no prohibition).
The mufta bihi follows the two shaykhs.
3
Understand the underlying proof principle
The passage cites the principle that when halal and haram evidence conflict, haram prevails. This is used to justify why makruh tahrimi is treated as near haram.
Refer to the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud mentioned in the footnote.
4
Apply classification to fiqh chapters
The text notes that makruh in acts of worship (like prayer) is tanzihi, while in matters like hunting and general prohibitions it is tahrimi. Apply this rule to other chapters.

I say: the point of his statement is discernment: the prohibited must be abandoned, and the permissible is allowed to be done. Supported by what is understood from al-Talwih (3) where he said: According to their view, either its abandonment is better than its doing: then with a prohibition against the act it is haram, and without it [the prohibition] it is disliked with discommendation-abhorrence; and if it is closer to the permissible, meaning that its doer is not punished but its abandoner is rewarded with the least reward; and dislike with prohibition-abhorrence is if it is closer to the prohibited, meaning that its doer deserves a deterrence without punishment, without [the degree of] prohibition. [2] His statement: 'As for the disliked... etc.': Some learned people said in distinguishing between prohibitional and discommendational: The disliked mentioned in the Book of Prayer and related matters is discommendational, and what is mentioned in the Book of Hunting and Restriction and Permissibility is prohibitional (4). Al-Jalabi said it.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage is a definitional ruling that explains the two categories of disliked (makruh) in the Hanafi school. It begins by stating the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf that makruh is not haram but closer to haram, which is termed makruh tahrimi (prohibitional disliked). Next, it clarifies that makruh tanzihi (discommendational disliked) is closer to halal. The author then discusses how to express rulings in texts: for explicitly halal or haram one uses clear terms; for non-explicit matters, one says 'la ba's (no harm) for halal and 'akrah' (I dislike) for haram. The commentary elaborates on the reasoning: conflicting evidence is resolved by the principle that haram overcomes halal, citing a hadith from Ibn Mas'ud. A distinction is made by some scholars: makruh in acts of worship like prayer is tanzihi, while in matters of hunting and prohibition is tahrimi. Finally, Muhammad's view is that makruh (tanzihi) is when abandonment is better but there is no prohibition, and its opposite is mandub (recommended). The passage aligns with the Hanafi definition where makruh tahrimi is impermissible but without definitive proof, while makruh tanzihi is permissible but discouraged.

Key Terms
المكروه كراهة تحريم (al-makrūh karāhat tahrīm): An action that is impermissible due to a strong but non-definitive proof, closer to haram, and its doer is blameworthy but not punished.
المكروه كراهة تنزيه (al-makrūh karāhat tanzīh): An action whose abandonment is better but is not prohibited; distant from haram and closer to halal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is haram.
B) It is not haram but closer to haram.
C) It is halal.
D) It is makruh tanzihi.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, makruh is not a single category: it splits into tahrimi (near haram) and tanzihi (merely discouraged), a distinction vital for correct fatwa.

Legal Categories of Eating in the Hanafi School
Setting forth the five legal categories of eating.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling categories for eating (fard, rewarded, mubah, haram) and identify the specific intentions that change the ruling.

Core Idea
The ruling on eating is not fixed — it ranges from fard to haram based on intention and necessity.

This passage from the matn of 'Umdat al-Ri'aya outlines the fiqh of eating. The Hanafi school teaches that eating takes on different legal rulings depending on the circumstances and the individual's intention. Understanding these categories helps avoid excess and fulfill obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its possible rulings
The act in focus is 'eating' (al-akl). The passage assigns multiple rulings based on conditions.
2
List the five categories and their conditions
Fard when prevents death, rewarded when enables worship, mubah to satiety for strength, haram beyond need except for two intentions, and makruh for donkey milk.
The exception to haram includes intending strength for the next day's fast or to avoid causing shyness to a guest.
3
Identify the ruling on donkey milk
Donkey milk is makruh (disliked), following the same ruling as donkey meat.
This is a separate but related sub-ruling.
4
Observe the transition to the camel urine dispute
The passage then introduces a more contested issue, indicating that not all rulings are straightforward.
This sets the stage for the second lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الأكل والشرب الأكل فرض إن دفع به هلاكه ومأجور عليه إن مكنه من صلاته قائماً ومن صومه، ومباح إلى الشبع ليزيد قوته، وحرام فوقه إلا لقصد قوة صوم الغد، أو لئلا يستحيي ضيفه، وكره لبن الأتان، وبول الإبل أما لبن الأتان: فحكمه حكم لحمه، وأما بول الإبل فحرام عند أبي حنيفة، وعند أبي يوسف له يحل به التداوي الحديث العرنيين، وعند محمد يحل مطلقاً؛ لأنه لو كان حراماً لا يحل به التداوي، قال: «ما وضع شفاؤكم فيما حرم عليكم» وأبو يوسف له يقول: لا يبقى حينئذ حراماً للضرورة، وأبو حنيفة الله يقول: الأصل في البول الحرمة، وهو الله قد علم شفاء العرنيين وحياً، وأما في غيرهم، فالشـفاء فيه غير معلوم فلا يحل.
Chapter on eating and drinking. Eating is obligatory if it prevents one's destruction, and rewarded if it enables him to perform his prayer standing and his fast, and permissible up to satiety to increase his strength, and forbidden beyond that except with the intention of strength for the next day's fast or so that his guest is not shy. And donkey milk is disliked, and camel urine. As for donkey milk, its ruling is the ruling of its meat. As for camel urine, it is forbidden according to Abu Hanifa, and according to Abu Yusuf it is permitted for treatment due to the hadith of the 'Urayniyin, and according to Muhammad it is permitted absolutely; because if it were forbidden, treatment with it would not be permissible. He said: 'Your cure is not placed in what is forbidden to you.' And Abu Yusuf says: It does not remain forbidden then due to necessity, and Abu Hanifa says: The original ruling on urine is prohibition, and Allah knew the cure of the 'Urayniyin by revelation, but for others, the cure in it is not known, so it is not permitted.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage first presents the matn's categories of eating: obligatory to prevent death, rewarded when it enables worship, permissible for strength, and forbidden beyond need. Then it addresses the specific cases of donkey milk and camel urine. Donkey milk is declared makruh, following the ruling of donkey meat. For camel urine, an intra-Hanafi dispute is detailed: Abu Hanifa holds it haram, Abu Yusuf permits it for medical treatment based on the hadith of the 'Urayniyin, and Muhammad permits it absolutely, citing the principle that cure is not placed in what is forbidden. The passage then records Abu Yusuf's justification by necessity and Abu Hanifa's response that the exception for the 'Urayniyin was a divine secret. The dispute shows that the Hanafi school has multiple views, with the later relied-upon position generally permitting it for necessity.

Key Terms
فرض (fard): Obligatory to the degree that omission leads to destruction; here used for minimal food to prevent death.
مباح (mubah): Permissible without reward or punishment, here up to satiety for strength.
حرام (haram): Prohibition; eating beyond necessity is haram except for valid reasons.
مكروه (makruh): Disliked; donkey milk is makruh, camel urine is disputed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, when does eating become haram?
A) When it is enjoyed
B) When it is beyond need without valid reason
C) When it is done alone
D) When a guest is present

Key Takeaway
The ruling on eating in Hanafi fiqh is not singular but conditional: it ranges from fard to haram based on intention and need.

The Camel Urine Dispute: An Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf on Treatment with Prohibited Substances
Intra-Hanafi dispute on camel urine.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the three Hanafi positions on camel urine (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) and evaluate which position is preferred for fatwa today.

Core Idea
Camel urine is not uniformly ruled – Abu Hanifa says haram, Abu Yusuf permits for treatment, Muhammad permits absolutely; the dispute is over generalizing a specific prophetic exception.

This passage dives into an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding camel urine. While the earlier part of the chapter gave general rules for eating, here the author presents the ikhtilaf among the three eponymous imams. Each relies on different dalil and reasoning. Understanding this dispute sharpens one's ability to weigh textual evidence against juristic principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed matter
The matter is the ruling on consuming camel urine.
The dispute is within the Hanafi school itself.
2
State each imam's position
Abu Hanifa: haram. Abu Yusuf: permitted for medical treatment. Muhammad: permitted absolutely.
Note that Abu Yusuf and Muhammad are the Sahibayn.
3
Analyze the proofs of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad
Abu Yusuf cites the hadith of the 'Urayniyin who were told to drink camel urine as a cure. Muhammad cites the hadith 'Your cure is not placed in what is forbidden to you'.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's response
Abu Hanifa argues that the cure for the 'Urayniyin was a specific divine secret (wahy), not generalizable. The original ruling on urine is prohibition (istishab).
Thus the exception does not prove general permissibility.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and tarjih
The practical difference is whether one may use camel urine for medical treatment. Later Hanafi works prefer Abu Yusuf's view for genuine necessity.
The mufta bihi is permission under necessity.

Prohibition of Using Gold and Silver Vessels
Ruling statement with hadith proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the prohibition of using gold and silver vessels for eating, drinking, applying oil, and perfuming, and cite the hadith evidence.

Core Idea
Using gold or silver vessels is haram for all; the Prophet warned it is like swallowing Hellfire.

This lesson addresses a clear and important ruling: the prohibition of using vessels made of gold or silver for any purpose involving consumption or bodily application. The evidence is a powerful hadith warning of Hellfire. We will examine the scope of the prohibition and its proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibited acts
The passage mentions eating, drinking, applying oil, and perfuming oneself. These are the specific actions prohibited when done using gold or silver vessels.
The prohibition extends to any similar use involving the vessel.
2
Identify the vessel material
The vessels in question are made of gold or silver (إناء ذهب وفضة). The ruling applies regardless of the user's gender.
Both men and women are equally prohibited.
3
State the ruling
The ruling is haram (prohibited). This is based on the explicit threat of Hellfire in the hadith.
Haram means the act is sinful and must be avoided.
4
Cite the evidence
The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Indeed, he who does so swallows into his belly the fire of Hell.' This is narrated by Bukhari and Muslim.
The hadith is ahad but accepted as evidence for prohibition.

Ruling on Gold and Silver Vessels for Anointing and Perfuming
Establishes prohibition and discusses medical exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on using gold and silver vessels for anointing and perfuming, and explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement regarding medical necessity with impure substances.

Core Idea
Using gold/silver vessels is haram, but anointing has a nuance: direct pouring is prohibited; taking oil by hand is not disliked.

The prohibition on using gold and silver vessels is well-established, but how exactly does it apply to anointing with oil? And what happens when medical necessity arises with a prohibited substance? This lesson unpacks the ruling from the Hanafi perspective, including the proof from hadith and a key disagreement between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
State the act under discussion: eating, drinking, anointing, and perfuming from gold or silver vessels. The ruling is haram for both men and women.
The prohibition is general and applies to all forms of use.
2
Locate the Dalil
The commentator cites the hadith: 'He only gurgles the fire of Hell into his belly.' This is the primary proof. Identify its thubut (zanni) and dalala (zanni) and explain wajh al-dalalah.
The hadith is from Umm Salamah, recorded in Bukhari and Muslim.
3
Analyze the Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Medical Necessity
Abu Yusuf holds that necessity can make a prohibited substance (like urine) permissible for treatment if a Muslim doctor prescribes it. Abu Hanifa counters that the original prohibition remains unless the cure is definitively known by revelation (as with the 'Urayniyyin).
The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's position.
4
Understand the Exception for Anointing
The prohibition on anointing applies only when oil is poured directly from the gold/silver vessel onto the head. If one takes the oil with his hand from the vessel and then applies it, it is not disliked (makruh).
This exception is based on avoiding direct contact with the vessel.
5
Determine the Practical Implication
In practice, the relied-upon view is that using gold/silver vessels for any of these purposes is haram, except that indirect anointing (by hand) is permissible. The medical necessity exception is very narrow.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa.

Source Text

قال صاحب «العناية»: وكذا ذكره صاحب «الذخيرة» في «الجامع الصغير»، وروى أنه مخالف كما ذكره في المكحلة ، فإنَّ الكحل لا بد أن ينفصل عنها حين ومع ذلك فقد ذكره في المحرّمات كذا قاله الجلبي [۳] قوله : قال .. الخ الجرجرة : بانك كردن وريختن ، فعلى الأول قوله : نار جهنم ؛ بالرفع، وعلى الثاني بالنصب، واختار في «المغرب»(٥) النصب، فقال: هكذا كان محفوظاً من الثقات بنصب الراء ، ومعناه يردّدها فله جرجر العجل : إذا ردّد صوته في حنجرته قاله الجلبي ) .
Husn al-Diraya for the end of Sharh al-Wiqayah. And eating, drinking, anointing, and perfuming from a gold or silver vessel. [1] And Abu Yusuf said: It does not remain then haram due to necessity. And Abu Hanifa said: The original rule regarding urine is prohibition, and Allah certainly knew the healing of the 'Urayniyyin by revelation. As for others, healing therein is not known, so it is not permissible. And eating, drinking, anointing, and perfuming from a gold or silver vessel: i.e. for men and women. He said: 'He only gurgles the fire of Hell into his belly.' [1] His statement: 'It does not remain then...' etc.; it is said: It is permissible to treat with what is prohibited, like wine and urine, if a Muslim doctor informs him that there is healing in it and we do not find anything else that takes its place. Prohibition is removed by necessity, so he is not treating with what is prohibited, so the hadith of prohibition does not apply to him. It is possible that he said it regarding an illness for which a cure other than the prohibited is known. [2] His statement: 'And anointing...' etc.; it is said: The form of prohibited anointing is that he takes the prohibited vessel and pours the oil from it onto the head directly. But if he inserts his hand into it, takes the oil, then pours it from his hand onto the head, it is not disliked. Kohl. The author of al-'Inayah said: Similarly, it is mentioned by the author of al-Dhakhirah in al-Jami' al-Saghir, and it is narrated that it is contrary as mentioned regarding the kohl container, for kohl must separate from it at that time, and despite that he mentioned it among prohibitions. Thus said al-Jalabi. [3] His statement: 'He said...' etc. Al-Jarajara: to swallow and pour. According to the first interpretation, his statement 'fire of Hell' is in the nominative; according to the second, it is in the accusative. In al-Mughrib, he chose the accusative and said: Thus was it preserved from reliable transmitters with the accusative of the letter ra'. Its meaning: he repeats it. Hence 'the calf jarjara' if it repeats its sound in its throat. Said al-Jalabi.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling of using gold and silver vessels for eating, drinking, anointing, and perfuming. The main text (matn) states that these actions are prohibited. The commentator then discusses two exceptions: First, the case of Abu Yusuf who opines that urine (which is normally impure) becomes permissible for medicinal treatment when there is necessity, because the prohibition is lifted by necessity. Abu Hanifa disagrees, arguing that the original ruling on urine is prohibition, and the healing of the 'Urayniyyin was known by revelation; for others, healing is not known, so it remains prohibited. This shows a principle: that which is originally haram cannot be made halal by mere possibility of cure. Second, the commentator clarifies the manner of anointing: it is only haram if the oil is poured directly from the gold/silver vessel onto the head. If one takes the oil with his hand from the vessel and then applies it, it is not disliked because the direct contact with the prohibited vessel is avoided. The same applies to kohl. The text also explains the word 'jarjarah' (gurgling) mentioned in the hadith, which refers to swallowing with a gurgling sound.

Key Terms
الادهان (al-idhān): The act of anointing oneself with oil, specifically from a gold or silver vessel.
الجرجرة (al-jarajara): To swallow liquid with a gurgling sound, or to pour.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Haram, because the oil came from a gold vessel.
B) Not disliked (makruh), because the oil was not poured directly from the vessel.
C) Makruh tahrimi, because it is similar to drinking.
D) Permissible (mubah), because the hand is not the vessel.

Key Takeaway
The prohibition on gold/silver vessels is absolute for direct use, but indirect anointing (by hand) is permissible; medical necessity does not generally override original prohibition.

Silver-Plated Vessels and Seats: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Explains permissibility conditions and intra-school disagreement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on using silver-plated items, distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, and apply the principle of tabi'iyyah (subordination) to analogous cases.

Core Idea
Silver is a subordinate; avoiding direct touch permits use – Abu Hanifa's tabi' principle.

This passage from the chapter on dislikes (Kitab al-Karahiyya) presents the Hanafi ruling on using vessels and furniture that contain silver. The issue is whether the presence of silver renders the entire object impermissible or disliked. The Imams disagree: Abu Hanifa permits use if one avoids direct contact with the silver, while Abu Yusuf considers it always disliked. The dispute hinges on whether the silver is considered a subordinate (tabi') part or an integral part of the object.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa)
Identify Abu Hanifa's ruling: using silver-plated items is permissible if one avoids the silver itself (e.g., not putting lips or hands on the silver).
The condition is explicit: 'while avoiding the place of silver'.
2
Identify the opposing view and its ground
Abu Yusuf holds that it is absolutely disliked (makruh). His reasoning: using any part of the vessel constitutes using all parts, because the silver is not separate.
Muhammad's position is reported in two narrations: with Abu Hanifa or with Abu Yusuf.
3
Analyse the opposing proof
The proof for Abu Yusuf: if a part of the vessel is used, the whole vessel is considered used, so it is disliked just as if the silver itself were used.
This is based on the idea that the object is a single unit.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response
Abu Hanifa argues that the silver is a subordinate (tabi') part; subordinates are not given independent legal consideration. The analogy is a cloak trimmed with silk: the silk is prohibited for men, but the cloak is permissible.
Subordination means the silver is not the primary substance or intended part.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: Under Abu Hanifa's view, one may eat from a silver-plated dish if the silver is avoided; under Abu Yusuf, any such use is disliked. The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view.
Also, consensus (ijma) that thin plating (tamwih) which cannot be separated is permissible regardless.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الكراهية / الأكل والشرب واللبس وغيرها
۲۸۷
وجلوسه على مفضّضٍ مُتقياً موضعَ الفضة وحل من إناء رصاص، وزجاج، وپلور، وعقيق، ومن إناء مفضض ، وجلوسه على مفضض مُتقياً موضع الفضة وحَلَّ من إناء رصاص ، وزجاج ، ويلور (۱) ، وعقيق (۳)، ومن إناء مفضض (۳) ) ، وعند الشَّافِعِي يُكْرَه ، وجلوسه على مفضض متقياً موضع الفضة)، فقوله : وجلوسه عَطْفٌ على الضمير في حَلّ، وهذا يجوز لوجود الفصل، فعند أبي حنيفة الأكل والشرب من الإناء المفضض ، والجلوس على الكرسي، أو السرير، أو السرج ، أو نحوه مفضضاً إنما يحلُّ إذا كان مُتَّقياً موضع الفضة : أي لا يكون الفضة في موضع الفم، وفي موضع اليد عند الأخذ وفي موضع الجلوس على الكرسي ، وعند أبي يوسف يُكْرَهُ مُطلقاً، ومحمد له قد قيل : إنه مع أبي حنيفة ، وقد قيل : إنه مع أبي يوسف له.
مفضضاً،
[1]
[1]قوله : وقد قيل...الخ ؛ وعلى هذا الخلاف الإناء المضبب بالذهب والفضة ، والكرسي المضبب بهما، وكذا إذا جعل ذلك في السيف والمشحذ وحلقة المرآة، أو جعل المصحف مذهباً أو مفضضاً، وكذا الاختلاف في اللجام والركاب والشفر إذا كان وكذا الثوب فيه كتابة بذهب أو فضة على هذا الخلاف، وهذا الاختلاف فيما يخلص ، فأما التمويه الذي لا يخلص فلا بأس به بالإجماع. لهما : إن مستعمل جزء من الإناء مستعمل جميع الأجزاء فيكره، كما إذا استعمل موضع الذهب والفضة.

Book of Dislikes / Eating, Drinking, Clothing, etc. And his sitting on a silver-plated (furnishing) while avoiding the place of silver, and permissible from vessels of lead, glass, crystal, agate, and from a silver-plated vessel. And his sitting on a silver-plated (furnishing) while avoiding the place of silver. And according to al-Shafi'i, it is disliked. And his sitting on a silver-plated (furnishing) while avoiding the place of silver. So his statement: 'and his sitting' is conjoined to the pronoun in 'permissible', and this is allowed due to the presence of a separation. According to Abu Hanifa: eating and drinking from a silver-plated vessel, and sitting on a chair, bed, saddle, or something similar that is silver-plated, is only permissible if he avoids the place of silver: i.e., the silver should not be at the place of the mouth, nor at the place of the hand when taking, nor at the place of sitting on the chair. According to Abu Yusuf, it is disliked absolutely. And concerning Muhammad, it has been said that he is with Abu Hanifa, and it has been said that he is with Abu Yusuf. [1] His statement: 'And it has been said...' etc. And based on this disagreement is the vessel inlaid with gold and silver, and the chair inlaid with both, and likewise if that is placed on the sword, whetstone, mirror ring, or if the mushaf is made gilt or plated with silver, and likewise the difference regarding the bridle, stirrup, and the beard (?) if it is... and likewise the garment in which there is writing in gold or silver, based on this difference. And this difference applies to that which is [solid] that can be separated. As for plating (coating) that does not separate, there is no harm in it by consensus. For them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): If a part of the vessel is used, all parts are considered used, so it is disliked, just as if the place of gold and silver were used. For Abu Hanifa: That (silver) is a subordinate part, and no consideration is given to subordinates, so it is not disliked, like a cloak trimmed with silk, the badge on a garment, and the gold nail in a gemstone. Thus in al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on using silver-plated vessels and furniture. The commentator explains that according to Abu Hanifa, it is permissible to eat, drink, or sit on a silver-plated item as long as one avoids direct contact with the silver itself (i.e., the silver is not at the mouth, hands, or seat). Abu Yusuf, however, considers it disliked (makruh) in all cases because using any part of the vessel implies using the whole. The position of Muhammad al-Shaybani is reported differently: some say he agreed with Abu Hanifa, others with Abu Yusuf. This difference extends to other items like gold/silver inlays on swords, mirrors, Qur'ans, etc. Importantly, there is consensus (ijma) that mere plating (tamwih) which is not a separable layer is permissible. The reasoning for Abu Hanifa is that the silver is a subordinate (tabi') and not independently intended; analogous to a cloak with silk trim or a gold stud on a gemstone, which are not prohibited. The textbook reference is from al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
المفضض (al-mufaḍḍaḍ): An object plated or inlaid with silver.
التمويه (al-tamwīh): Thin plating or coating that cannot be separated from the base metal.
المضبب (al-muḍabbaḍ): An object with inlaid gold or silver that is separable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the silver is considered a subordinate part and subordinates are not given legal consideration.
B) Because Abu Yusuf agreed with this ruling.
C) Because the chair is made of silver entirely.
D) Because sitting is a separate act unrelated to the vessel.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits using silver-plated items if one avoids the silver, based on the principle that subordinates (tabi') do not carry independent rulings.

Enduring Prohibited Acts to Uphold the Sunnah
Discussion on the ruling of enduring haram to establish a Sunnah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on enduring a prohibited act for the sake of a Sunnah and explain the proper form of patience required.

Core Idea
Enduring a haram act to establish a Sunnah is impermissible; true patience requires turning away and not enjoying the prohibited act.

This passage from a Hanafi commentary addresses a critical ethical question: can a person tolerate a prohibited activity if the intention is to preserve a Sunnah practice? The author firmly rejects this notion, clarifying that the only form of patience permitted by Abu Hanifa is one where the person is physically present but mentally and emotionally disengaged, denouncing the act and deriving no pleasure from it. The discussion is anchored in a hadith that limits permissible leisure to three specific activities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Justification
Recognize that the act in question is enduring a prohibited (haram) activity with the claimed good intention of establishing a Sunnah.
The text uses 'الصبر على الحرام لإقامة السنة' – enduring haram to establish the Sunnah.
2
State the Hanafi Ruling
The ruling is clear: this endurance is not permissible (la yajūz). The act is haram regardless of the noble goal.
Ruling derived from the word 'ابتليت' indicating prohibition.
3
Analyze the Dalil
The hadith of three exemptions is cited: 'لهو المؤمن باطل إلا في ثلاث...' – The believer's leisure is falsehood except in three. The exception (illā) implies that all other leisure is bāṭil and thus prohibited.
This hadith is a Sunnah ahad, zanni in transmission and meaning.
4
Understand the Nuance of Patience
Abu Hanifa's concept of patience here means sitting while turning away from the amusement, denouncing it, not being occupied with it, and not deriving pleasure. This is different from actively enduring or participating.
The word 'صبر' is qualified: it is for the situation of affliction (ibtulayta), not voluntary persistence.
5
Apply the Ruling
In practice, if one finds oneself in a haram environment without choice, the allowed response is to be present but internally detached. One must not voluntarily remain to achieve a religious goal.
The objection about the Quranic verse (play and amusement) is refuted by specifying that only the three exemptions are permissible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ابتليت، يدل على الحرمة، ويمكن أن يقال: الصبر على الحرام لإقامة السنة لا يجوز، والصبر الذي قال أبو حنيفة [؟الا الله؟] أن يكون جالساً معرضاً عن ذلك اللهو منكراً له، غير مشتغل ولا متلذذاً به. (1) لا يقال: إن الحياة الدنيا لعب ولهو؛ لقوله [؟حالا؟] : إِنَّمَا الْحَيَوةُ الدُّنْيَا لَعِبٌ وَلَهو، وهي ليست بحرام؛ لأنَّ الحاصل من هذا القياس أن بعض اللهو واللعب ليس بحرام، وهو ما استثناه النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم في قوله: «لهو المؤمن باطل إلا في ثلاث: تأديبه لفرسه، ورميه عن قوس وملاعبته مع أهله» (3). قاله الجلبي (3). (2) 1] قوله: ويمكن أن يقال؛ هذا اعتراض منه على قول المصنف: وذا قبل أن يقتدي به، يعني: ولو سلمنا أنّ [؟الأعظم الله؟] كان غير مقتدى به حين الابتلاء فلا يجوزُ له أن يصبر على الحرام؛ لإقامة السنة. ثم أجاب عنه بقوله: والصبر الذي قال أبو حنيفة: حاصله إنّ الابتلاء بذاتِ المحرم من حيث هو، والصبر عليه والقبولُ به من غير المقتدى من حيث تجرده عما نشأ عنه الحرمة من اشتغال النفس والتذاذها به. قاله الجلبي (4).
His statement: 'you are afflicted' indicates prohibition. And it could be said: enduring the haram to establish the Sunnah is not permissible. The patience that Abu Hanifa [referred to] is that he sits turning away from that amusement, denouncing it, not occupied nor taking pleasure in it. (1) It cannot be said: 'The life of this world is play and amusement' due to His statement [?] : 'Indeed the life of this world is play and amusement' (Quran 57:20), and it is not haram. Because what results from this analogy is that some amusement and play are not haram, which is what the Prophet (peace be upon him) exempted in his statement: 'The believer's leisure is falsehood except in three: training his horse, shooting his bow, and playing with his wife.' (3). Al-Jalabi said (3). (2) 1] His statement: 'and it could be said' is an objection from him to the author's statement: 'and this before he follows him', meaning: even if we accept that [?] was not followed at the time of affliction, it is not permissible for him to endure the haram to establish the Sunnah. Then he answered him with his statement: and the patience that Abu Hanifa said: its essence is that affliction with the very object of prohibition as it is, and enduring it and accepting it by the one not followed in terms of its being stripped of what gave rise to its prohibition from occupation of the soul and taking pleasure in it. Al-Jalabi said (4).
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from a commentary discussing the permissibility of enduring a prohibited act for the sake of establishing a Sunnah. The author first states that the word 'ابتليت' (you are afflicted) indicates prohibition (hurmah). It then raises an objection: one might argue that enduring haram to uphold a Sunnah is not allowed, but Abu Hanifa's concept of patience here means to be present while turning away from the amusement, not engaging in it or taking pleasure. The objection cites the Qur'anic verse that life is play and amusement, implying that not all amusement is haram. The response clarifies that the analogy would only permit the three specific exemptions mentioned in the hadith (training a horse, archery, playing with family). The commentator al-Jalabi explains that the objection is directed at the author's earlier statement that this applies before one is followed. Even if we assume the person was not being followed at the time, enduring the haram is still not permitted. The patience Abu Hanifa refers to is enduring the affliction itself without engaging in the prohibited activity with any sense of occupation or pleasure. Thus, the ruling is that one must not endure haram actions even for a noble goal; rather, one should be present but internally detached and disapproving.

Key Terms
ابتليت (ibtulayta): You are afflicted or tested; here it refers to being forced into a situation involving a prohibited act.
صبر (ṣabr): Patience or endurance; here it specifically means tolerating the presence of a prohibited act without engaging in it or deriving pleasure.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling on enduring a prohibited act to establish a Sunnah?
A) Permissible
B) Not permissible
C) Permissible only in war
D) Permissible if not enjoying it

Key Takeaway
Enduring a haram act to establish a Sunnah is impermissible; the only acceptable form of patience under affliction requires turning away and not enjoying the prohibited act.

Men Wearing Silk: Prohibition and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on silk in war and size exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on men wearing silk, explain the exception for small amounts, and compare Abu Hanifa's position with that of the Sahibayn regarding war.

Core Idea
Silk is fundamentally haram for men, but small decorative patches are excused, and the Sahibayn permit it in war.

The prohibition of silk for men is well-established in the Hanafi school, based on explicit hadiths. However, within the school there is a notable disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his two principal disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), concerning the permissibility of wearing silk during war. This passage from a commentary on Al-Hidayah presents the general rule, the exception for small quantities, and the differing views on necessity in war.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling and its basis
The passage begins by stating that a man must not wear silk except for four fingers' width. This is the general prohibition, sourced from hadiths forbidding silk for men. Note the exception for small amounts used in decorative patches or hems.
The four-fingers exception is based on a hadith allowing a small area.
2
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on war
Abu Hanifa holds that silk is prohibited in all circumstances, including war. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that in war, necessity (ḍarūrah) permits wearing silk. The commentator says 'we say: necessity,' indicating acceptance of the Sahibayn's view.
This ikhtilaf is rooted in whether the general prohibition is overridden by necessity.
3
Distinguish the two exceptions
There are two distinct concessions: (1) small patches up to four fingers, which are excused due to the hadith; (2) full silk garments in war according to the Sahibayn. The former is agreed upon, the latter is a disagreement within the school.
The small-patch exception applies always; the war exception is only when necessity is genuine.
4
Understand the practical outcome (thamarah)
In peacetime, all Hanafis agree silk is haram except for small patches. In wartime, the mufta bihi (operative view) often follows the Sahibayn, permitting silk due to necessity, though Abu Hanifa's stricter view is also transmitted.
This is an example of how necessity can override a prohibition in Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في اللبس (لا يلبس رجل حريراً إلا قدر أربعة أصابع) : أي في العرض، أراد مقدار العلم، وروي أنه [؟لا له؟] : «البس جُبَّةٌ مكفوفة بالحرير)، وعند أبي حنيفة : لا فرق بين حالة الحرب وغيره وعندهما يحلُّ في الحرب ضرورة. قلنا : الضرورة. ١ ] قوله : لا يلبس رجل...الخ ؛ والأصلُ في لبس الحرير ما في «الهداية»: لا يحل للرجال لبس الحرير ؛ لأن النبي [؟ل؟] نهى عن لبس الحرير والديباج ، وقال : إنما يلبسه من لا خلاق له في الآخرة» (۳). وإنما حل للنساء بحديث آخر، وهو ما رواه عدة من الصحابة [؟له؟] ؛ [؟علي له؟] : إن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم خرج وبإحدى يديه حرير وبالأخرى ذهب، وقال: «هذان محرمان على ذكور أمتي ، حلال لإناثهم» (۳) ، ويروى : «حل لإناثهم». إلا أن القليل عفو، وهو مقدار ثلاثة أصابع أو أربع كالأعلام والمكفوف بالحرير، لما روي أنه نهى عن لبس الحرير إلا موضع إصبعين أو ثلاث أو أربع )) (٤) (0)
Chapter on Clothing] (A man must not wear silk except the amount of four fingers): i.e., in width, he intended the amount of a sleeve. And it is narrated that [he?] said: 'Wear a cloak hemmed with silk.' According to Abu Hanifa: there is no difference between the state of war and other times, while according to them (the two disciples) it is permissible in war out of necessity. We say: necessity. 1] His statement: 'A man must not wear silk...' etc.; the basis for wearing silk is what is in Al-Hidayah: It is not permissible for men to wear silk because the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade wearing silk and brocade, and said: 'It is worn by one who has no share in the Hereafter.' (3) And it was made permissible for women by another hadith, which was narrated by several companions: 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him): The Prophet (peace be upon him) came out with silk in one hand and gold in the other, and said: 'These two are forbidden for the males of my ummah, permissible for their females.' (3) And it is narrated: 'halal for their females.' Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage is from a commentary expanding on the ruling of men wearing silk. It begins by stating the general rule: a man may not wear silk except the width of four fingers. This exception refers to the width of sleeves or decorative patches. The commentary then presents an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa holds that there is no difference between war and peace — silk is prohibited in both. However, his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, hold that silk becomes permissible in war due to necessity (ḍarūrah). The commentator says 'we say: necessity' possibly siding with the Sahibayn. The text then quotes the foundational ruling from Al-Hidayah: silk and brocade are prohibited for men based on the hadith that those who wear them have no share in the Hereafter. An exception is made for women, supported by a hadith where the Prophet declared gold and silk forbidden for males but halal for females. Finally, a small quantity is excused — specifically the amount of two, three, or four fingers — such as the embroidered 'alamāt (patches) and hems (makfūf). This exception is based on a narration that forbade wearing silk except for a patch of that size. Thus, the Ḥanafī position: silk is fundamentally haram for men, but a small decorative amount is permitted, and in war the Sahibayn permit it entirely.

Key Terms
حرير (ḥarīr): Silk (fabric produced by silkworms); its wearing is prohibited for men except in specific exceptions.
ضرورة (ḍarūrah): Necessity; a situation that permits normally prohibited actions (like wearing silk in war).
علم (ʻalam (pl. aʻlām)): Emblem or patch; here it refers to decorative embroidered patches on clothing that may be made of silk.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, in which situation is wearing silk permissible for men?
A) At all times
B) In war due to necessity
C) In peacetime for formal events
D) Never, regardless of circumstances

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prohibits silk for men, but permits small decorative patches up to four fingers, and the Sahibayn extend permission to wartime due to necessity.

Ruling on Mixed Silk Garments and the Three Cases
Intra-Hanafi dispute on mixed silk garments with three-case framework

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on mixed silk garments based on the weft/warp distinction and apply the three-case framework to determine permissibility.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school rules on mixed silk by the weft, with context (war/peace) altering the ruling.

This passage from al-Jalabi's hashiya on al-Wiqayah explores the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding mixed silk garments. The central legal principle is that the weft (luhma) is the decisive element because it is the proximate cause, but the ruling also depends on whether the garment is worn in war or peace. Understanding these distinctions helps avoid common misunderstandings about silk in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the intra-Hanafi dispute
Determine where Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) differ on garments with silk weft and non-silk warp. Abu Hanifa permits using them as pillows or spreads; the Sahibayn deem it disliked.
The matn initially seems to side with Abu Hanifa, but later fatwa relies on the Sahibayn.
2
Understand the three-case framework
Classify the three scenarios: (1) fully silk (brocade), (2) silk warp + non-silk weft, (3) non-silk warp + silk weft. Apply different rulings for war and peace.
Case 3 is only permissible in war due to necessity (instilling awe).
3
Apply the proximate cause (al-illa al-qariba)
The weft is the dominant attribute when it exists; the ruling attaches to the last element in existence. If weft is silk, the garment is treated as silk (makruh per Sahibayn); if weft is non-silk, it is permissible even if warp is silk.
This principle is key for mixed fabrics.
4
Note the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
Contrast the outcomes: Abu Hanifa allows using a silk-weft cushion; the Sahibayn say it is disliked. The relied position is the Sahibayn's view.
The difference affects daily practice: one should avoid silk-weft items outside war.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويتوسده ويفترشه ويلبس ما سداه إبريسم ولحمته غيره، وعكسه في الحرب فقط. تندفع بما لحمته إبريسم وسداه غيره، ويتوسده ويفترشه، هذا عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله؛ لما روي أنه جلس على مرفقة من حرير، وقالا: يكره. ويلبس ما سداه إبريسم ولحمته غيره، وعكسه في الحرب فقط، إنما أراد الأعلام، وعنه: إنه كان يلبس جبة مكفوفة بالحرير، يقال: ثوب مكفف، لما كف جيبه وأطراف كميه بشيء من الديباج. كما صرحه صاحب الذخيرة العقبى. اعلم أن هذه المسألة على ثلاثة أوجه: الأول: ما يكون كله حريراً وهو الديباج، لا يجوز لبسه في الحرب اتفاقاً، أما في غير الحرب فعند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله لا يجوز، وعندهما يجوز، ودليل الفريقين مذكور في الهداية. والثاني: ما يكون سداه حريراً ولحمته غيره، ولا بأس بلبسه في الحرب وغيره؛ لأن الحكم إذا تعلق بعلة ذات وصفين يضاف إلى آخرهما وجوداً واللحمة كذلك. والثالث: عكس الثاني، وهو مباح في الحرب، والضرورة وهي إيقاع الهيبة في عين العدو لبريقه ولمعانه، ولا ضرورة في غيره، فيكون مكروهاً. كذا في الأكملية. قاله الجلبي. واعتبروا في المخلوط اللحمة حتى لو كانت من الإبريسم لا يحل، وإن كانت من غيره يحل اعتباراً للعلة القريبة.
He uses it as a pillow and spreads it, and wears what its warp is silk and its weft is other, and the opposite only in war. It is repelled by what its weft is silk and its warp is other, and he uses it as a pillow and spreads it. This is according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, due to the narration that he sat on a silk cushion. And the two said: it is disliked. And he wears what its warp is silk and its weft is other, and the opposite only in war. He only intended the flags. And from him: he used to wear a robe trimmed with silk. It is said: a trimmed garment, because he trimmed his collar and sleeve edges with some brocade. As stated by the author of al-Dhakhirah al-Uqba. Know that this issue has three aspects: first: what is entirely silk, i.e. brocade, it is not permissible to wear it in war by agreement. As for outside war, according to Abu Hanifa it is not permissible, and according to the two it is permissible, and the evidence of the two groups is mentioned in al-Hidayah. Second: what its warp is silk and its weft is other, there is no harm in wearing it in war and elsewhere, because when a ruling attaches to a cause with two attributes, it is attributed to the last of them in existence, and the weft is like that. Third: the opposite of the second, it is permissible in war due to necessity which is instilling awe in the eyes of the enemy by its sheen and luster, and there is no necessity in other contexts, so it is disliked. Thus in al-Akmaliyyah. Al-Jalabi said it. And they considered in the mixed [garment] the weft, so if it is of silk it is not permissible, and if it is of other it is permissible, considering the proximate cause.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute and explanation of the ruling on mixed silk garments. The matn (from al-Wiqayah) states that one may use a garment with silk weft (luhma) and non-silk warp (sada) as a pillow or spread, and wear it; the opposite (silk warp, non-silk weft) is only allowed in war. Abu Hanifa permits the first case based on his practice of using a silk cushion. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) deem it disliked. The hashiya (by al-Jalabi) then clarifies three cases: (1) Fully silk (brocade): unlawful in war by consensus; outside war, Abu Hanifa says unlawful, the Sahibayn say permissible. (2) Silk warp, non-silk weft: permissible always because the weft is the dominant attribute. (3) Non-silk warp, silk weft: permissible only in war due to necessity for intimidation. The final sentence emphasizes that the weft governs the ruling as the proximate cause (al-illa al-qariba).

Key Terms
لُحْمَة (luhma): The weft thread in weaving, running across the fabric from side to side.
سَدَى (sada): The warp thread in weaving, running lengthwise in the fabric.
مَرْفَقَة (marfaqa): A cushion or pillow used for resting the elbow; a small cushion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on wearing a garment whose warp is silk and weft is cotton in a non-war situation?
A) Permissible (halal) because the weft is non-silk.
B) Disliked (makruh) according to Abu Hanifa, permissible according to the two companions.
C) Unlawful (haram) because silk is present.
D) Permissible only if the silk is less than half.

Key Takeaway
The weft is the determining factor in mixed silk garments, and necessity in war permits silk weft garments; the mufta bihi considers wearing silk-weft items outside war as makruh.

Men's Adornment with Gold and Silver: Exceptions and Prohibitions
States the prohibition and exceptions for men's gold/silver use.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on men wearing gold and silver ornaments and identify the three exceptions.

Core Idea
Men may only wear gold or silver for a signet ring, belt, or sword ornament – nothing else.

The Hanafi school clearly distinguishes between permitted adornment for men and women. While women may wear gold and silver freely, men are restricted to functional items only. This lesson unpacks the exact exceptions and the reasoning behind the prohibition of other forms of ornamentation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule for men
The passage states that a man does not adorn himself with gold or silver. This is a prohibition (haram) covering all ornaments except those explicitly mentioned.
2
List the three exceptions
The exceptions are: a signet ring (khatam), a belt (mintaqah), and a sword ornament (hilyat sayf). A gold nail for securing a gemstone is also mentioned, but it is part of the signet ring.
These exceptions are for functional purposes.
3
Understand the ruling for women
Women are permitted all forms of gold and silver ornaments – there is no restriction.
4
Identify prohibited materials for signet rings
Men may not wear a signet ring made of stone, iron, or brass. Only silver (or gold for the exceptions) is allowed.
The term 'sufr' refers to a type of impure gold mixed with copper.
5
Note the preference for non-rulers
The passage says leaving off the signet ring for non-rulers is preferred (ahabb). This is because the ring is primarily for sealing documents, a function of rulers and judges.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يتحلى بذهب أو فضة إلا بخاتم، ومنطقة، وحلية سيف منها، ومسمار ذهب لثقب فص، وحل للمرأة كلها. ولا يتختم بالحجر والحديد والصفر وتركه لغير الحاكم أحب.
And he (a man) does not adorn himself with gold or silver except for a signet ring, a belt, a sword ornament from them, and a gold nail for piercing a gemstone. And it is permissible for a woman entirely. And he does not wear a signet ring of stone, iron, or brass; and leaving it (the ring) for other than the ruler is preferred.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from al-Wiqayah (the matn) concerning adornment with precious metals and signet rings for men. It states that men may not wear gold or silver ornaments except in specific cases: a signet ring (khatam), a belt (mintaqah), a sword ornament (hilyat sayf), and a gold nail used to secure a gemstone. For women, all gold and silver ornaments are permissible. Additionally, men should not wear signet rings made of stone, iron, or brass; however, if the ring band is silver and the stone is from these materials, it is permissible. The phrase 'and leaving it for other than the ruler is preferred' means that only rulers and judges need to wear signet rings for sealing documents; others may avoid them as they are a form of adornment. The commentary in the original hashiya further explains the terms: 'mintaqah' is a belt, 'mismar' is a nail, and 'sufr' is an inferior type of gold impure with copper. This ruling aligns with the general Hanafi principle that men are prohibited from gold and silver except for functional uses.

Key Terms
مَنْطِقَة (mintaqah): A belt or girdle, often ornamented, worn around the waist.
حِلْيَة سَيْف (hilyat sayf): The ornamentation or fittings of a sword, such as the hilt, scabbard, or guard.
صُفْر (sufr): A type of impure gold mixed with copper, inferior in quality; also brass.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) A silver signet ring with a stone.
B) A gold belt.
C) A sword hilt ornamented with gold.
D) A gold chain necklace.

Key Takeaway
Men may only wear gold or silver for a functional signet ring, belt, or sword ornament; all other forms are haram.

The 'Awrah of a Man for Another Man: Hanafi Definition and Dispute
Defines the 'awrah of a man for another man and disputes with Shafi'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of a man's 'awrah for another man and explain the hadith evidence that distinguishes it from the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
A man's 'awrah for another man is from below the navel to below the knee—the navel is not 'awrah, the knee is.

The precise boundaries of the 'awrah (the parts of the body that must be covered) have been a matter of scholarly discussion. In this passage, the Hanafi school defines the 'awrah of a man in front of another man, drawing on explicit hadith evidence. This definition differs from that of Imam al-Shafi'i, and the passage explains the textual basis for the Hanafi view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its boundaries
The passage defines the 'awrah of a man for another man as 'except what is between his navel to below his knees.' Note that the Hanafi school interprets this to exclude the navel and include the knee.
2
Locate the primary hadith evidence
The author cites the hadith: 'The 'awrah of a man is what is between his navel to his knee' and a variant. Identify the specific wording that supports the Hanafi position.
The hadith is reported by al-Bayhaqi and others.
3
Trace how the hadith excludes the navel and includes the knee
The phrase 'between his navel to his knee' indicates that the navel itself is not included (it is the boundary), and the knee is included as part of the area. The action of al-Hasan ibn 'Ali exposing his navel provides corroboration.
4
Note the Shafi'i disagreement and its proof
Al-Shafi'i holds the opposite: the navel is 'awrah and the knee is not. The passage does not provide the Shafi'i proof, but we can infer it from the disagreement.
The opposition is based on a different interpretation of the same or other evidence.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi school relies on the explicit hadith and the practice of a Companion, making their view stronger. The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) is that for Hanafis, a man may expose his navel but must cover his knee, while for Shafi'is, the navel must be covered and the knee may be exposed.
The mufta bihi (operative ruling) is the Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في النظر واللمس والوطء وينظر الرجل من الرجل سوى ما بين سرته إلى تحت ركبتيه ( وينظر الرجل من الرجل سوى ما بين سرته إلى تحت ركبتيه ) ، السرة ليست بعورة عندنا ، والركبة عورة، وعند الشافعي : على العكس. قوله : وينظر...الخ ؛ اعلم أن مسائل [النظر] أربعة : نظر الرجل إلى امرأة ، ونظرها إليه، ونظر الرجل إلى الرجل، ونظر المرأة إلى المرأة، والأول على أربعة أقسام : نظره إلى الأجنبية الحرة، ونظره إلى من تحل له من الزوج والأمة، ونظره إلى ذوات محارمه، ونظره إلى أمة الغير. كذا في «العناية». (٢) قوله : سوى ما بين سرته إلى تحت ركبيته ... الخ ؛ لقوله : «عورة الرجل ما بين سرته إلى ركبته» (٢) ، ويروى : «ما دون سرته حتى تجاوز ركبته»، وبهذا ثبت أن السرة ليست بعورة خلافاً لما يقوله أبو عصمة والشافعي ، والركبة عورة خلافاً لما قاله الشافعي ، والفخذ عورة خلافاً لأصحاب الظواهر. وما دون السرة إلى منبت الشعر عورة، خلافاً لما يقوله الإمام أبو بكر محمد بن الفضل الكماري معتمدا فيه العادة ؛ لأنه لا عبرة بها مع النص بخلافه. وقد روى أبو هريرة عن النبي أنه قال : «الركبة من العورة» (٣)، وأبدى الحسن بن علي لالتماس أبي هريرة و سرته فقبلها ، ثبت بها أن السرة ليست بعورة. (٤) وقال الجرهد وقد انكشف فخذه : أما علمت أن الفخذ عورة ) ، هذا نص على كون الفخذ عورة ؛ ولأن الركبة ملتقى عظم الفخذ والساق فاجتمع المحرم والمبيح، وفي مثله يغلب المحرم. وحكم العورة في الركبة أخف منه في الفخذ وفي الفخذ أخف منه في السوءة ، حتى أن كاشف الركبة ينكر عليه برفق، وكاشف الفخذ يعنف عليه، وكاشف السوءة يؤدب إن لج. قال التفتازاني في «شرح المقاصد»: في «بحث الأمر بالمعروف»: وفي الفخذ الضرب، وفي السوءة يقتل إن لج ، وكشف إزاره في الموضع المعد للغسل كالحمام للغسل وغيره لا بأس به، ويغض الناظر بصره ، والإثم في الناظر لا الكاشف للضرورة.
Chapter on looking, touching, and intercourse. A man looks at a man except what is between his navel to below his knees. (A man looks at a man except what is between his navel to below his knees). According to us, the navel is not 'awrah, and the knee is 'awrah. According to al-Shafi'i, it is the opposite. His statement: "and he looks... etc." Know that the issues of looking are four: a man looking at a woman, her looking at him, a man looking at a man, and a woman looking at a woman. The first is divided into four categories: his looking at a free foreign woman, his looking at one who is permissible for him like a wife and slave girl, his looking at his mahram relatives, and his looking at another's slave girl. Thus in al-'Inayah. (2) His statement: except what is between his navel to below his knees... etc. Due to his saying: "The 'awrah of a man is what is between his navel to his knee" and it is narrated: "What is below his navel until it exceeds his knee" and by this it is established that the navel is not 'awrah, contrary to what Abu 'Ismah and al-Shafi'i say, and the knee is 'awrah, contrary to what al-Shafi'i said, and the thigh is 'awrah, contrary to the Zahiris. And what is below the navel to the hairline is 'awrah, contrary to what Imam Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl al-Kamari says, relying on custom, because it is not considered with the text contrary to it. And Abu Hurayrah narrated from the Prophet that he said: "The knee is part of the 'awrah" and al-Hasan ibn 'Ali, at the request of Abu Hurayrah, exposed his navel and kissed it, thereby establishing that the navel is not 'awrah. (4) And al-Jarhad said when his thigh was uncovered: Do you not know that the thigh is 'awrah? This is a text that the thigh is 'awrah. And because the knee is the meeting point of the thigh bone and the shin, so the forbidden and permissible meet, and in such cases the forbidden prevails. And the ruling of 'awrah in the knee is lighter than in the thigh, and in the thigh lighter than in the private parts, such that one who uncovers the knee is gently rebuked, one who uncovers the thigh is severely admonished, and one who uncovers the private parts is disciplined if persistent. al-Taftazani said in Sharh al-Maqasid in the section on enjoining good: In the case of the thigh, beating; in the case of the private parts, execution if persistent. And uncovering his garment in a place prepared for washing, like a bathhouse for washing and other than it, is not objectionable, and the observer lowers his gaze, and the sin is on the observer not the uncoverer due to necessity.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the 'awrah of a man when looking at another man. The author begins by stating the matn: a man may look at another man except for the area between the navel and below the knees. He clarifies the Hanafi position: the navel is not 'awrah, but the knee is 'awrah, contrary to al-Shafi'i who holds the opposite. The commentary then expands by categorizing looking into four types and four subcategories for a man looking at a woman. It then provides the textual evidence: the hadith 'The 'awrah of a man is what is between his navel to his knee' and a variant. The author refutes opposing views: Abu 'Ismah and al-Shafi'i (navel is 'awrah), the Zahiris (thigh not 'awrah), and al-Kamari (lower abdomen based on custom). The hadith of Abu Hurayrah and al-Hasan's action further proves the navel is not 'awrah. The thigh is 'awrah based on the statement of al-Jarhad. The knee is ruled 'awrah by combining the forbidden and permissible aspects. Finally, the severity of uncovering each part is discussed: knee (light rebuke), thigh (severe admonition), private parts (punishment). A concession for necessary washing places is given, with sin on the observer.

Key Terms
عورة ('awrah): The parts of the body that must be covered; for a man before another man, it is from below the navel to below the knee including the knee but not the navel.
سرة (surah): The navel; according to Hanafis, it is not part of the 'awrah.
ركبة (rukbah): The knee; according to Hanafis, it is part of the 'awrah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) From the navel to the knee, including both.
B) From below the navel to below the knee, excluding the navel and including the knee.
C) From the navel to the knee, excluding the knee.
D) The entire body from shoulders to ankles.

Key Takeaway
The 'awrah of a man for another man according to the Hanafi school is from below the navel to below the knee, excluding the navel and including the knee.

Kissing and Embracing Men in One Garment: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on dislike of kissing/embracing in one garment and permissibility with shirt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on men kissing and embracing in a single garment, distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf, and apply the distinction to similar scenarios involving physical intimacy.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, kissing and embracing men in one garment is disliked (makruh) according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, but Abu Yusuf permits it — the difference turns on the degree of intimacy.

This passage addresses a specific etiquette ruling: two men sharing a single lower garment (izar) without a shirt. The matn declares kissing and embracing in such a state to be disliked (makruh), yet permissible when a shirt (or handshake) intervenes. The commentary reveals an intra-Hanafi disagreement: Abu Yusuf sees no objection at all. Understanding this dispute clarifies the school's method of weighing modesty and default permissibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The matn states that for a man to kiss and embrace another man while both are under a single garment (izar) is 'makruh' (disliked). This is the stated ruling.
The ruling applies only to the specific scenario; it does not generalize to all physical contact.
2
Recognize the two positions within the Hanafi school
The commentary clarifies that this ruling is the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Abu Yusuf, the third leading Hanafi Imam, disagrees and says 'there is no harm in both (kissing and embracing) in one garment.'
All three agree that with a shirt (or handshake) there is no dislike – this is by consensus.
3
Understand the basis of the difference
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad consider close skin-to-skin contact under a single garment as excessively intimate, potentially resembling the acts of immoral people or leading to temptation, hence disliked. Abu Yusuf sees no such implication and defaults to permissibility unless there is suspicion of evil.
The basis is a difference in assessing the level of modesty required among men.
4
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under the majority view (Abu Hanifa & Muhammad), one should avoid such contact as it is makruh. Under Abu Yusuf's view, it is permissible and not disliked. The mufta bihi (operative position) is the majority view, so in practice, it is recommended to avoid.
The matn itself adopts the makruh ruling, indicating its precedence.
5
Apply the ruling to similar cases
Consider scenarios like hugging with a thin shirt, or kissing on the cheek with a scarf. The key factor is the presence or absence of a barrier (like a shirt). The principle can be extended: close physical contact without a barrier is closely scrutinized.
This step trains the student to analogize within the same legal framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكره تقبيل الرجل وعناقه في إزار واحد، وجاز مع قميص ومصافحته وكرة تقبيلُ الرَّجل) وعناقه في إزار واحد، وجاز مع قميص ومصافحته)، عطف على الضمير في جاز هذا عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد ، وقال أبو يوسف : لا بأس بهما في إزار واحد، وأما مع القميص فلا بأس بالإجماع
And it is disliked (karaha) for a man to kiss and embrace another man in one garment, but it is permissible with a shirt and handshake. And it is disliked for a man to kiss and embrace in one garment, and it is permissible with a shirt and handshake—this is the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Abu Yusuf said: There is no harm in both (kissing and embracing) in one garment. As for with a shirt, there is no harm by consensus.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the ruling on men kissing and embracing each other. The matn states that it is makruh (disliked) for a man to kiss or embrace another man when they are both under a single garment (izar) without a shirt, but it is permissible if they are wearing shirts or shaking hands. The commentary clarifies that this ruling is the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani. However, Abu Yusuf disagrees and says there is no objection to kissing and embracing even in one garment. All three agree that with a shirt, there is no dislike (by consensus). The difference arises from whether physical contact under a single garment without a barrier is considered excessively intimate and therefore disliked, or not. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad consider it makruh tanzihi, while Abu Yusuf sees no harm.

Key Terms
إزار (izar): A garment worn from the waist down, covering the lower half of the body. In this context, it refers to being under a single garment covering both men without a shirt.
عناق ('inaq): Embracing, putting one's arm around another's neck and hugging.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is haram (forbidden).
B) It is makruh (disliked).
C) It is mubah (permissible).
D) It is wajib (obligatory).

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, men kissing and embracing in a single garment without a shirt is disliked (makruh) according to the majority, but permissible according to Abu Yusuf; the presence of a shirt removes the dislike by consensus.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Embracing in a Single Garment
Intra-Hanafi dispute with reconciliation and grammatical refinement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on embracing in a single garment, identify the views of Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, and Abu Yusuf, and apply the distinction between actions out of love and out of lust.

Core Idea
Embracing in an izar is makruh (Abu Hanifa) but mubah (Abu Yusuf) — intention and covering determine the ruling.

This passage addresses the etiquette of physical affection between men in the Hanafi school. While handshake is always permissible, embracing or kissing in minimal clothing is subject to disagreement. Understanding the conditions and the reasoning behind each position helps us apply the correct ruling in daily interaction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic ruling
Read the opening sentence: 'وَكُرِهَ تَقْبِيلُ الرَّجُلِ وَعِنَاقُهُ فِي إِزَارٍ وَاحِدٍ – Kissing and embracing in a single garment is disliked.' Note the default ruling according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: makruh.
This is the zahir al-riwayah position from Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
2
Map the intra-Hanafi dispute
Contrast with Abu Yusuf's statement: 'لَا بَأْسَ بِهِمَا – no problem with both.' Recognize that this is an estimated difference among the Hanafi imams. The disagreement is only when the action is out of love (mahabbah); if out of lust (shahwah), all agree it is haram.
The disagreement is over the ruling for affection, not lust.
3
Understand the reconciliation by Shaykh Abu Mansur
Shaykh Abu Mansur explains that 'makruh' refers specifically to an embrace that is lustful; the author's use of 'in a single garment' highlights a situation that leads to lust. Thus, the karahah is context-dependent.
This reconciles conflicting hadiths on the topic.
4
Analyze the grammatical refinement by al-Jalabi
Al-Jalabi questions whether the dual pronoun in the commentator's phrase refers to the acts (kissing and embracing) or to the two men embracing. He argues it refers to the men, so according to Abu Yusuf, there is no problem with the two men embracing, even in one garment.
This subtle point affects the application of Abu Yusuf's view.
5
Derive the practical implications
The ruling depends on two factors: (1) intention — love or lust, and (2) covering — minimal (izar) or full (qamis/jubbah). With full covering, all agree it is permissible. With minimal covering, the Hanafi mufti follows the majority view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, considering it makruh unless there is a strong need.
The mufta bihi is not explicitly stated, but later practice tends to the cautious view.

وَكُرِهَ تَقْبِيلُ الرَّجُلِ وَعِنَاقُهُ فِي إِزَارٍ وَاحِدٍ، وَجَازَ مَعَ قَمِيصٍ وَمُصَافَحَتُهُ. هَذَا عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَمُحَمَّدٍ، وَقَالَ أَبُو يُوسُفَ: لَا بَأْسَ بِهِمَا فِي إِزَارٍ وَاحِدٍ، وَأَمَّا مَعَ الْقَمِيصِ فَلَا بَأْسَ بِالْإِجْمَاعِ. وَالْخِلَافُ فِيمَا يَكُونُ لِلْمَحَبَّةِ، وَأَمَّا بِالشَّهْوَةِ فَلَا شَكَّ فِي الْحُرْمَةِ إِجْمَاعًا. وَالشَّيْخُ أَبُو الْمَنْصُورِ وَافَقَ بَيْنَ الْأَحَادِيثِ فَقَالَ: الْمَكْرُوهُ مِنَ الْمُعَانَقَةِ مَا كَانَ عَلَى وَجْهِ الشَّهْوَةِ، وَعَبَّرَ عَنْهُ الْمُصَنِّفُ بِقَوْلِهِ فِي إِزَارٍ وَاحِدٍ، فَإِنَّهُ سَبَبٌ يُفْضِي إِلَيْهَا، وَأَمَّا عَلَى وَجْهِ الْبِرِّ وَالْكَرَامَةِ إِذَا كَانَ عَلَيْهِ قَمِيصٌ أَوْ جُبَّةٌ فَلَا بَأْسَ. وَنَحْنُ نَقُولُ: لَا نُسَلِّمُ أَوَّلًا أَنَّ ضَمِيرَهُمَا فِي عِبَارَةِ الشَّارِحِ لَهُ رَاجِعٌ إِلَى التَّقْبِيلِ وَالْعِنَاقِ، فَلِمَ لَا يَجُوزُ أَنْ يَرْجِعَ إِلَى الْمُتَعَانِقَيْنِ الَّذِينَ أَشَارَ إِلَيْهِمَا الْمُصَنِّفُ لَهُ بِلَفْظِ: وَعِنَاقِهِ. فَالْمَعْنَى: وَلَا بَأْسَ عِنْدَ أَبِي يُوسُفَ بِالْمُتَعَانِقَيْنِ بِسَبَبِ عِنَاقِهِمَا، وَإِنْ كَانَ ذَلِكَ فِي إِزَارٍ وَاحِدٍ. وَسُلِّمَ فَلَا رَيْبَةَ أَنَّ التَّقْبِيلَ فِي الثَّوْبِ الَّذِي لَا يَسْتُرُ إِلَّا مِنْ سُرَّتِهِ إِلَى تَحْتِ الرُّكْبَةِ أَفْضَى إِلَى الشَّهْوَةِ مِنَ الثَّوْبِ الَّذِي يَسْتُرُ جَمِيعَ الْبَدَنِ، فَاتَّضَحَ وَجْهُ تَعَلُّقِ الظَّرْفِ بِالتَّقْبِيلِ، كَالْمُعَانَقَةِ. قَالَهُ الْجَلَبِيُّ فِي حَاشِيَتِهِ.
Kissing a man and embracing him in a single garment is disliked (makruh), and it is permissible with a shirt and handshake. This according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, while Abu Yusuf said: There is no problem with both in a single garment. As for with a shirt, there is no problem by consensus. The disagreement is when it is out of love, but when out of lust there is no doubt of prohibition by consensus. Shaykh Abu Mansur reconciled the hadiths and said: What is disliked from embracing is that which is in the manner of lust, and the author expressed it by his saying 'in a single garment' because it is a cause leading to it. As for in the manner of piety and honor, if he is wearing a shirt or a cloak, there is no problem. And we say: We do not first concede that the dual pronoun in the commentator's expression refers to kissing and embracing; why is it not permissible that it refers to the two embracing persons whom the author indicated by his word 'and his embrace'? So the meaning: There is no problem according to Abu Yusuf with the two embracing persons due to their embrace, even if that is in a single garment. And if conceded, there is no doubt that kissing in a garment that only covers from the navel to below the knee leads more to lust than a garment that covers the entire body. So the manner of attachment of the adverb to kissing is clear, like embracing. Al-Jalabi said this in his hashiya.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on a man kissing or embracing another man while both are in a single garment (izar), as well as handshake and embracing with a shirt. The base ruling is that it is makruh (disliked) according to Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad, while Imam Abu Yusuf considered it permissible (mubah). All agree that with a shirt (qamis) it is permissible by consensus. The commentator then clarifies that the disagreement only applies when the embrace is out of love (muhabbah); if it is out of sexual desire (shahwah), it is haram by consensus. Shaykh Abu Mansur reconciles the conflicting hadiths on this topic, explaining that the dislikedness is specifically when the embrace is lustful; the author's use of 'in a single garment' highlights a situation that may lead to lust. A later hashiya (by al-Jalabi) refines the interpretation of the pronoun in the commentator's statement, arguing that it could refer to the two men embracing rather than the acts of kissing and embracing. This nuance affects the ruling: according to Abu Yusuf, there is no problem with two men embracing in one izar, provided the embrace is out of love. The key point is that the ruling depends on intention and clothing: with minimal covering, the action is closer to lust, hence disliked; with full covering, it is permissible.

Key Terms
إِزَار (izar): A waist-wrapper or loincloth that covers from the navel to below the knee; a simple garment without a shirt.
مُعَانَقَة (mu'anaqah): Embracing, putting arms around each other's necks.
مَصَافَحَة (musafahah): Handshake; here it is mentioned as an action that is permissible even without a shirt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, what is the ruling on a man embracing another man in a single garment (izar) when done out of love?
A) Haram
B) Makruh
C) Mubah
D) Sunnah

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, embracing in a single garment is disliked (makruh) out of love, but permissible with a shirt; the dispute with Abu Yusuf reflects different views on the risk of arousal.

Ruling on Kissing the Hand of a Scholar and Standing Out of Respect
Ruling on permissibility of hand-kissing and dislike of standing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on kissing the hand of a scholar, just ruler, or pious person for tabarruk, and explain why standing out of respect is disliked, citing the relevant hadith.

Core Idea
Kissing the hand of a righteous person for blessing is a dispensation, but standing out of respect is disliked.

The Hanafi school provides clear guidance on everyday social interactions, including gestures of respect. This lesson examines two such practices: kissing the hand of a scholar or pious person for blessing, and standing up as a mark of respect. The classical text distinguishes between permissible and disliked acts and highlights a difference of opinion among later scholars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the actions and subjects
Two actions are discussed: kissing the hand (taqbil yad) of a scholar, just ruler, or pious person; and standing (qiyam) as a sign of respect.
The text restricts kissing to these three categories.
2
Determine the ruling for kissing
According to some later scholars, it is permissible (murakhkhas); according to others, it is a sunnah. The purpose must be tabarruk (seeking blessing).
No dispensation for kissing the hand of others.
3
Determine the ruling for standing
The text reports that the Prophet disliked standing, so the ruling is makruh tanzihi (disliked).
The hadith of Anas is the evidence.
4
Analyze the dalil
The Prophet's dislike indicates karahah (dislike) in Hanafi usul, not tahrim (prohibition).
This is a zanni dalil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأمَّا تَقْبِيلُ يَدِ الْعَالِمِ، وَالسُّلْطَانِ الْعَادِلِ، أَوْ شَخْصٍ مَعْرُوفٍ بِالزُّهْدِ وَالْوَرَعِ لِلتَّبَرُّكِ، فَمُرَخَّصٌ عِنْدَ بَعْضٍ مِنَ الْمُتَأَخِّرِينَ، وَسُنَّةٌ عِنْدَ بَعْضِهِمْ. وَأَمَّا يَدُ غَيْرِهِمْ فَلَا رُخْصَةَ فِي تَقْبِيلِهِ. وَلَمْ يُذْكَرِ الْقِيَامُ تَعْظِيمًا لِلْغَيْرِ، وَرُوِيَ أَنَّ النَّبِيَّ صلى الله عليه وسلم كَانَ يَكْرَهُ الْقِيَامَ. قَالَهُ الْجَلَبِيُّ.
As for kissing the hand of a scholar, a just ruler, or a person known for asceticism and piety for seeking blessing, it is permitted according to some later scholars, and a sunnah according to some of them. As for the hand of others, there is no license to kiss it. And standing as a sign of respect for others is not mentioned [as disliked], and it is narrated that the Prophet (peace be upon him) used to dislike standing. Al-Jalabi said this.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling on two common social practices: kissing the hand for respect/blessing, and standing up for someone. Regarding kissing the hand, the text distinguishes between the hand of a scholar, a just ruler, or a person known for piety—these may be kissed for the purpose of seeking blessing (tabarruk). However, there is a difference of opinion among later scholars: some consider it merely permissible (murakhkhas), while others consider it a sunnah. Kissing the hand of any other person is not allowed (no dispensation). As for standing out of respect for someone, the text mentions that the Prophet disliked it (karaha al-qiyam), citing a hadith narrated by Anas. This indicates that standing for others is considered makruh (disliked) in general. The commentator al-Jalabi adds that the discussion of standing was not explicitly mentioned in the original text, but the ruling is derived from the hadith.

Key Terms
تَقْبِيلُ يَدٍ (taqbil yad): Kissing someone's hand as a gesture of respect or seeking blessing.
التَّبَرُّك (al-tabarruk): Seeking blessing (barakah) through contact with a pious person or their body part.
الْقِيَام (al-qiyam): Standing up out of respect for someone entering or present.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Permitted by some, considered sunnah by others
B) Haram
C) Makruh
D) Compulsory

Key Takeaway
Kissing the hand of a scholar, just ruler, or pious person for tabarruk is permissible (some say sunnah); standing out of respect is disliked based on the prophetic hadith.
