Ruling on Embracing and Kissing: Love vs. Desire
Explains rulings on embracing and kissing with distinction between love and desire.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi ruling on embracing and kissing when done for love versus for desire, and state the proof for each.

Core Idea
Embracing and kissing are permitted for love but unanimously prohibited if done with sexual desire.

The Hanafi school makes a critical distinction between acts of affection (mahabbah) and acts of lust (shahwah). While there is clear consensus on the prohibition of any touching with desire, the permissibility of embracing and kissing for love depends on the context and clothing. This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah explains the nuances, including reconciling conflicting hadiths and the views of early Hanafi authorities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the two scenarios
Determine whether the act (embracing or kissing) is done for love (mahabbah) or desire (shahwah).
The ruling hinges entirely on the intention and the presence of desire.
2
State the ruling for desire and its proof
For desire, the ruling is haram by consensus (ijma). The text states 'لا شك في الحرمة إجماعاً'.
Ijma is a strong proof that cannot be overridden by individual opinions.
3
State the ruling for love and its proof
For love, the ruling is permissible (mubah) or disliked (makruh) if it leads to desire. Proof includes the statement of the author of Al-Hidayah 'la ba'sa' and Abu Yusuf's view on embracing in one garment.
The permissibility is conditional on not leading to desire and on proper clothing.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
The commentator argues that the dual pronoun in 'no harm in both' refers to the two persons embracing, not the acts, thus aligning with Abu Yusuf's view. This is a legitimate ikhtilaf in interpretation.
The mufta bihi is the differentiation: permissible for love, haram for desire.
5
Apply the reconciliation by Sheikh Abu al-Mansur
Abu al-Mansur reconciled hadiths by saying disliked embracing is that with desire; if done out of piety with proper clothing, there is no harm. Also, kissing the hand of a scholar or just ruler for blessing is permitted.
This reconciliation clarifies the rulings for practical situations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الكراهية / الأكل والشرب واللبس وغيرها
والخلاف فيما يكون للمحبة ، وأما بالشهوة فلا شك في الحرمة إجماعاً. يأبى عنه قول الشارح : لا بأس بهما لضمير التثنية في جميع النسخ التي رأيناها ، وإن تعلق بالعناق والتقبيل معاً كما هو مقتضى شرح الشارح له، وهو قول صاحب «الهداية» : لا بأس بالتقبيل والمعانقة، فتوجيه معنى كون التقبيل في إزار واحد لا يخلو عن ارتكاب التكلفات.
ونحن نقول : لا نسلم أولاً أن ضميرهما في عبارة الشارح له راجع إلى التقبيل والعناق، فلم لا يجوز أن يرجع إلى المتعانقين الذين أشار إليهما المصنف له بلفظ : وعناقه. فالمعنى: ولا بأس عند أبي يوسف له بالمتعانقين بسبب عناقهما، وإن كان ذلك في إزار واحد، وسلم فلا ريبة أن التقبيل في الثوب الذي لا يستر إلا من سرته إلى تحت الركبة أفضى إلى الشهوة من الذين يستر جميع البدن، فاتضح وجه تعلق الظرف بالتقبيل، كالمعانقة. قاله الجلبي في «حاشيته».
قوله : والخلاف فيما يكون للمحبة ؛ والشيخ أبو المنصور وفق بين الأحاديث ، فقال : المكروه من المعانقة ما كان على وجه الشهوة، وعبر عنه المصنف بقوله : في إزار واحد، فإنه سبب يفضي إليها، وأما على وجه البر والكرامة إذا كان عليه قميص أو جبة فلا بأس.
وأما تقبيل يد العالم، والسلطان العادل، أو شخص معروف بالزهد والورع للتبرك ، فمرخص عند بعض من المتأخرين، وسنة عند بعضهم، وأما يد غيرهم فلا رخصة في تقبيله، ولم يذكر القيام تعظيماً للغير، وروى أنس أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم كان يكره القيام. قاله الجلبي
Book of Disliked Acts / Eating, Drinking, Clothing, etc. The difference is when it is for love; as for desire, there is no doubt about its prohibition by consensus. The commentator's statement contradicts this: 'There is no harm in both' due to the dual pronoun in all the copies we have seen, even if it relates to embracing and kissing together as implied by the commentator's explanation, and it is the statement of the author of Al-Hidayah: 'There is no harm in kissing and embracing.' Directing the meaning of kissing while in one garment is not free from difficulty. We say: First, we do not accept that the dual pronoun in the commentator's phrase refers to kissing and embracing; why can it not refer to the two embracing persons that the author alluded to with his word 'and his embrace'? So the meaning is: According to Abu Yusuf, there is no harm for him concerning the two embracing persons due to their embrace, even if that is in one garment. Even if accepted, there is no doubt that kissing in a garment that covers only from the navel to below the knee leads more to desire than one that covers the whole body, so the manner of relating the circumstantial phrase to kissing is clear, like embracing. Al-Jalabi said this in his gloss. He said: The difference is when it is for love; Sheikh Abu al-Mansur reconciled the hadiths and said: The disliked form of embracing is that which is done out of desire, and the author expressed it by his words 'in one garment' because it is a cause leading to it. As for that done out of piety and honor, if one is wearing a shirt or cloak, there is no harm. As for kissing the hand of a scholar, a just ruler, or a person known for asceticism and piety for blessing, it is permitted by some later scholars and a Sunnah by others. As for the hand of others, there is no license to kiss it. He did not mention standing up out of respect for others, and Anas narrated that the Prophet, peace be upon him, disliked standing up. Al-Jalabi said this.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah explains the ruling on embracing and kissing. The passage type is a ruling with proof, combining consensus (ijma) for prohibition in the case of desire and a difference of opinion (ikhtilaf) for the case of love. The commentator begins by noting that the difference (الخلاف) applies when the act is for love; if it is for desire, there is consensus (إجماعاً) on prohibition. The commentator then responds to the statement of the sharh (Abu al-Mansur) who said 'no harm' based on the dual pronoun, but the commentator argues that the pronoun might refer to the two people embracing, not the acts. He concludes that kissing in minimal clothing leads to desire, so it is disliked. Then a reconciliation from Sheikh Abu al-Mansur is presented: disliked embracing is that with desire; if done out of piety with proper clothing, there is no harm. The passage then discusses kissing the hand of a scholar or just ruler: some later scholars permit it for blessing, some consider it Sunnah; kissing the hand of others is not allowed. Finally, it mentions that standing up out of respect for others is disliked, citing the hadith of Anas that the Prophet disliked standing. The Hanafi school differentiates between acts done for love (permissible) and desire (prohibited by consensus). This ruling is from the zahir al-riwayah as transmitted in Al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
معانقة (mu'anaqah): Embracing or hugging between two persons
تقبيل (taqbil): Kissing, especially of the hand or mouth
شهوة (shahwah): Sexual desire or lust
محبة (mahabbah): Love or affection, not lust

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Permissible (mubah)
B) Disliked (makruh)
C) Prohibited (haram) by consensus
D) Recommended (sunnah)

Key Takeaway
Embracing and kissing are permitted for love but absolutely prohibited when done with desire; the intention and context are decisive in Hanafi fiqh.

Ruling on Gilding the Quran (Tahliyat al-Mushaf)
Ruling on ornamentation of the Quran

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on gilding the Quran and explain the legal reasoning (honor, analogy) supporting it.

Core Idea
Gilding the Quran is permissible because it honors the Quran.

This lesson clarifies the Hanafi position on ornamenting the Quran with gold or silver. While some may consider such decoration excessive, the Hanafi school permits it as an act of reverence. The reasoning is straightforward: the act shows honor to the Quran, similar to decorating mosques, and there is no contrary evidence prohibiting it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is 'tahliyat al-mushaf' – gilding or ornamenting the Quran. The ruling explicitly stated is 'ja'iz' (permissible).
Permissibility is the default unless there is evidence to prohibit.
2
Extract the reason (ta'lil)
The reason given is 'li-ma fihi min ta'zim al-Quran' – because it shows honor to the Quran. This is the legal basis (sabab) for permissibility.
Honoring the Quran is a praiseworthy act, and any means that achieve it are generally permissible.
3
Identify the analogy (qiyas)
The commentator draws an analogy to decorating the mosque with gold ('ka-naqsh al-masjid wa-tazyinihi bi-ma' al-dhahab'). Since decorating mosques is allowed, gilding the Quran follows the same reasoning.
This analogy strengthens the ruling by connecting it to a well-established practice.
4
Note the absence of prohibition
No hadith or evidence is cited to forbid gilding. The ruling relies on the absence of a prohibition and the principle of 'ibaha' (permissibility) for matters of custom and adornment.
Hanafi usul holds that things are permissible until proven otherwise (al-asl fi al-ashya' al-ibaha).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتحلية المصحف [٢] قوله : وتحلية المصحف ...الخ ; أي جاز بتحلية المصحف، لما فيه من تعظيم القرآن، وصار كنقش المسجد وتزيينه بماء الذهب.
And gilding the Quran [2] He said: 'And gilding the Quran... etc.' Means: It is permissible to gild the Quran, because it shows honor to the Quran, and it is like decorating the mosque and adorning it with gold water.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling on gilding the Quran (tahliyat al-mushaf). The commentator states that it is permissible (ja'iz). The reason is that it honors and exalts the Quran. The commentator compares it to decorating the mosque with gold, which is also considered permissible as a form of respect. This reflects the principle that actions showing reverence to religious symbols are encouraged, unless specifically prohibited.

Key Terms
تحلية المصحف (tahliyat al-mushaf): Gilding or ornamenting the bound copy of the Quran with gold or silver.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It increases the value of the Quran
B) It shows honor to the Quran
C) It is mandatory for mosques

Key Takeaway
Gilding the Quran is permissible in the Hanafi school as an act of reverence, supported by analogy to mosque decoration and the absence of prohibition.

Dhimmi Entering the Mosque: Hanafi vs Shafi'i/Maliki
Inter-madhhab dispute on dhimmi mosque entry with Quranic exegesis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a dhimmi entering a mosque and explain the different interpretations of Quran 9:28 among the schools.

Core Idea
Hanafis permit dhimmi entry to all mosques except Masjid al-Haram, based on a specific historical interpretation of Quran 9:28.

This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute about whether a non-Muslim subject (dhimmi) may enter a mosque. The ruling varies based on how the Quranic verse about polytheists' impurity is understood. The Hanafi school takes a permissive stance, while Malik and al-Shafi'i impose restrictions, though they differ in scope. Understanding the reasoning behind each position is crucial for grasping how verses are applied in fiqh.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling according to the Hanafi school: a dhimmi may enter any mosque except the Sacred Mosque (Masjid al-Haram).
The text says 'this is according to us'.
2
Identify the opponents and their views
Imam Malik and Imam al-Shafi'i both hold it is disliked (or prohibited), but for different reasons. Malik prohibits entry to all mosques; Shafi'i prohibits only the Sacred Mosque.
The commentator notes that lumping them together is imprecise.
3
Follow the opponents' proof
Both cite Quran 9:28: 'the polytheists are unclean, so let them not approach the Sacred Mosque.' Malik generalizes the impurity to all mosques; Shafi'i restricts the prohibition to the Sacred Mosque as per the text.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school interprets the verse as a historic prophecy (that polytheists would be prevented from entering in the future), not as a permanent command. Thus, it does not entail ongoing prohibition.
The key is the phrase 'after this year of theirs'.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: Hanafi allows dhimmi in any mosque except the Sacred Mosque; Maliki prohibits all mosques; Shafi'i prohibits only the Sacred Mosque. This affects the daily practice of Muslim communities with non-Muslim visitors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودخول الدمي المسجد)، هذا عندنا، وعند مالك والشافعي ( ) له : يكره " ؛ لقوله : إِنَّمَا الْمُشْرِكُونَ نَجَسٌ فَلَا يَقْرَبُوا الْمَسْجِدَ الْحَرَامَ (٣) ، قلنا : لا يراد نهي الكفار عن هذا ; لأن قوله : إِنَّمَا الْمُشْرِكُونَ نَجَسٌ ) ، لا يوجب الحرمة بعد عامهم هذا ، بل المراد بشارة المسلمين بأنَّ الكفّارَ لا يتمكنون من الدخول بعد عامهم هذا. [٣]قوله : وعند مالك والشافعي يكره.... الخ ; المتبادر من هذه العبارة اتحاد مذهبهما بلا تفاوت ، وليس بذلك ; لأن مذهب مالك له حرمة دخولُ كل مسجد ; لأنه معلّل عنده بالنجاسة، فيعم الحكم، ومذهب الشافعي له حرمة دخول المسجد الحرام ; لورودِ النهي خاصة، فلينظر في «الهداية»(٥).
And the entry of the dhimmi into the mosque: This is according to us (Hanafis). According to Malik and al-Shafi'i, it is disliked, due to His saying: Indeed the polytheists are unclean, so let them not approach the Sacred Mosque (9:28). We (Hanafis) say: The prohibition of the disbelievers from this is not intended, because His saying 'Indeed the polytheists are unclean' does not necessitate prohibition after this year of theirs. Rather, the meaning is a glad tiding for Muslims that the disbelievers will not be able to enter after this year. [3] He said: According to Malik and al-Shafi'i it is disliked... etc. What is apparent from this expression is that their schools are the same without difference, but that is not the case. Because Malik's school holds the prohibition of entering any mosque, since it is reasoned by impurity, so the ruling is general. And al-Shafi'i's school holds the prohibition of entering only the Sacred Mosque, due to the specific prohibition. So one should look into al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute regarding a dhimmi (non-Muslim subject) entering the mosque. The Hanafi position is that it is permissible for a dhimmi to enter any mosque except the Sacred Mosque (Masjid al-Haram) in Mecca. The proof is the Quranic verse (9:28) which says polytheists are unclean and should not approach the Sacred Mosque. The Hanafis interpret this verse as specific to that year, not as a general prohibition forever. They argue the verse was a prophecy that polytheists would not be able to enter in future, not a command to prevent them. In contrast, Imam Malik holds that entry to any mosque is prohibited because impurity is general. Imam al-Shafi'i holds that only the Sacred Mosque is prohibited, based on the explicit wording of the verse. The commentator notes that although both are lumped together as 'disliked', there is a difference. The relied-upon position for fatwa should be checked in al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
دمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim living under protection of the Islamic state.
نجس (najis): Ritually impure.
المسجد الحرام (al-Masjid al-Haram): The Sacred Mosque in Mecca.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, where may a dhimmi enter?
A) Any mosque
B) Any mosque except the Sacred Mosque
C) No mosque
D) Only the Sacred Mosque

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits dhimmi entry to any mosque except the Sacred Mosque, interpreting Quran 9:28 as a historical prophecy rather than a permanent prohibition.

Visiting a Sick Dhimmi: The Permissibility and the Dispute Over Magians
Hanafi dispute on visiting a sick dhimmi, with different views on Magians

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on visiting a sick dhimmi and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding Magians.

Core Idea
Visiting a sick dhimmi is permissible; for Magians the Hanafis differ, but the bond of Muslim rights is preserved.

This lesson explores the Hanafi position on visiting non-Muslim citizens (dhimmis) when they are ill. While the general ruling is clear for Jews and Christians, a disagreement arises concerning Magians (Zoroastrians). We will also compare with visiting a sinful Muslim (fasiq) to understand the underlying principle of Muslim brotherhood.

Guided Path
1
Identify the baseline ruling
The text first states that visiting a Jewish or Christian dhimmi is permissible. This is the default position.
The ruling is mubah (permissible), not recommended or obligatory.
2
Locate the dispute on Majus
The text then indicates a difference of opinion regarding Magians (مجوسي). Identify the two views: one permits, the other prohibits.
The first view is narrated from Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani.
3
Analyze the reasoning for each view
The first view argues from their status as dhimmis. The second argues they are farther from Islam because their meat and marriage are not halal. Understand the basis of each.
The second view uses qiyas with the prohibition of their slaughter and marriage.
4
Compare with the ruling on a fasiq Muslim
The text mentions a similar dispute about visiting a fasiq Muslim, then declares the most sound view (al-asahh) that it is permissible because he is a Muslim and visiting is a right of Muslims.
This reinforces the principle that Muslim identity overrides sinfulness in this context.
5
Determine the mufta bihi if possible
The text does not explicitly resolve the Magian dispute. However, the mention of the fasiq case and the narration from Muhammad suggest permissibility is preferred.
Later Hanafi works may have clarified, but this passage leaves it open.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعيادته [٤]قوله : وعيادته...الخ ; أي يجوز عيادته ; أي عيادة الذمي اليهودي والنصراني، وفي المجوسي اختلاف : قيل : يجوز; لأنهم من أهل الذمة، وهو مروي عن محمد الله. وقيل : هم أبعد عن الإسلام ألا ترى أنه لا يباح ذبيحتهم ولا نكاحهم، واختلفوا في الفاسق أيضاً، والأصح أنه لا بأس به لأنه مسلم ، والعيادة من حقوق المسلمين. كذا في «العناية).
And visiting him [4] He said: 'and visiting him... etc.' Means: It is permissible to visit him, meaning visiting the dhimmi Jew and Christian. Concerning the Magian, there is difference of opinion: it is said it is permitted because they are people of dhimmah, and this is narrated from Muhammad. And it is said they are farther from Islam; do you not see that their slaughtered meat and marriage are not permitted? And they also differed concerning the immoral person (fasiq). The most sound view is that there is no harm because he is a Muslim, and visiting the sick is from the rights of Muslims. This is stated in al-'Inayah.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on visiting a sick dhimmi (a non-Muslim citizen). The general ruling is that it is permissible to visit a Jewish or Christian dhimmi. The dispute arises concerning a Magian (Zoroastrian). There are two opinions: one, reported from Imam Muhammad, permits it because Magians are also dhimmis. The other opinion says they are further from Islam, citing that their slaughtered animals are not lawful to eat nor their women permissible to marry, implying they are more removed. There is also a discussion about visiting a sinful Muslim (fasiq). The most sound view (al-asahh) is that there is no harm in visiting a fasiq Muslim, because he is still a Muslim and visiting the sick is a right of Muslims. This shows the Hanafi focus on preserving social bonds and fulfilling rights, even across religious lines with some exceptions.

Key Terms
عيادة (iyadah): Visiting a sick person, considered a right of Muslims and recommended.
مجوسي (majusi): A Magian (Zoroastrian), a religious group considered as one of the dhimmis but with some legal differences.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is prohibited because he is sinful
B) It is permissible because he is still a Muslim
C) It is only permissible if he repents
D) It is disliked

Key Takeaway
Visiting a sick dhimmi is permissible; for Magians there is an intra-Hanafi dispute that remains unresolved in this passage.

Castrating Animals and Mating Horses: Permissibility with Linguistic Clarifications
Linguistic clarification and implied permissibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on castrating animals and mating horses, and explain the linguistic issue regarding the term 'khisa' versus 'ikhsa'.

Core Idea
Castration and horse breeding are permissible; correct Arabic usage of 'khisa' matters for legal precision.

This lesson covers two animal-related rulings from a classical Hanafi text. The first concerns castrating animals, where the commentator clarifies a linguistic dispute about the proper term. The second concerns mating male donkeys with female horses, which is also addressed. Both actions are generally permissible in the Hanafi school, though the discussion here focuses on vocabulary and the principle of parallelism in Arabic usage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two actions
Recognize that 'khisa' al-baha'im' means castrating animals, and 'inza' al-humur 'ala al-khayl' means mating male donkeys with female horses. The passage groups them together.
2
Understand the linguistic correction
The commentator prefers 'khisa' over 'ikhsa'' for castration, citing al-Jalaliyyah. He notes that al-Rabbani used the if'al form for parallelism with 'inza'. This shows that even precise legal texts can prioritize stylistic harmony.
Parallelism (izdiwaj) is a respected Arabic literary feature.
3
Derive the ruling
The text does not explicitly state the hukm, but context implies permissibility. There is no prohibition mentioned. The discussion is linguistic, not about prohibition. This suggests the acts are mubah (permissible).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخصاءُ البهائم، وإنزاء الحمير على الخيل، والحقنة، ورزقُ القاضي [١] قوله : وخصاء البهائم...الخ، ولقد أحسن حيث غير لفظ : الإخصاء الواقع من ابن الساعاتي وغيره إلى الخصاء ; لأنه صرَّحَ في «الجلالية» بأنه يقال : أخصاه إذا نزع خصيته، يخصيه خصاء، والإخصاءُ في معناه خطأ، ولكن يمكن أن يقال : إن عبارة الرباني في مؤلّفاته على صيغة الإفعال. ولا ريب أنه أوثق من المطرزي هو مستند صاحب «النهاية» و«الجلال»، وأيضاً يجوز أن يكون اختيار الإخصاء هنا لرعاية الازدواج بينه وبين الإنزاء الذي هو إفعال من النزل، وهو وثبة الذكر على الأنثى، ورواية الازدواج عندهم من الأهمية بحيث يخرجون لها الكلم عن أوضاعها الأصلية في المواضع العديدة. قاله الجلبي [٢) قوله : والحقنة...الخ ; يريد التداوي ; لأنَّ التداوي مباح بالإجماع، وقد ورد بإباحة التداوي أحاديث، ولا فرق بين الرجال والنساء، إلا أنه لا ينبغي أن يستعمل المحرَّمُ كالخمر ونحوها; لأن الاستشفاء بالمحرَّم ،حرام، أما إذا عُلِمَ أن فيه شفاء ولا دواءً سواه يجوز الاستشفاء به. وأما قول ابن مسعود له : إن الله لم يجعل شفاءكم فيما حرم عليكم ، فيحتمل أنه قال : فيما كان للمرض دواء آخر غير المحرّم. كذا في «حواشي الهداية». [٣]قوله : فهذا يجوز; لأنّ في المنع الامتناع عن القضاء; ولأنه محبوس لحق (۱) «العناية (١٠: ٦٣ - ٦٤). (۲) في «ذخيرة العقبى»(ص٥٨٤).
And castrating animals, and mating male donkeys with female horses, and the enema, and the salary of the judge. [1] He said: 'and castrating animals... etc.' He did well in changing the wording from 'ikhsa'' used by Ibn al-Sa'ati and others to 'khisa'', because he stated in al-Jalaliyyah that it is said 'akhsahu' when one removes his testicles, but 'ikhsa'' in that meaning is wrong. However, it may be said that the wording of al-Rabbani in his works used the if'al form. There is no doubt that he is more reliable than al-Mutarrizi, who is the authority for the author of al-Nihayah and al-Jalal. Also it is allowed that the choice of 'ikhsa'' here is for the sake of parallelism (izdiwaj) between it and 'inzā' which is if'al from nazala, meaning the male mounting the female, and parallelism according to them is so important that they deviate words from their original meanings in many places. Al-Jalabi said it. [2] He said: 'and the enema... etc.' He means medical treatment, because medical treatment is permissible by consensus, and there are hadiths permitting treatment. There is no difference between men and women, except that one should not use prohibited substances like wine and the like, because seeking cure through what is prohibited is haram. However, if it is known that there is a cure in it and no other medicine exists, it becomes permissible to seek cure with it. As for the statement of Ibn Mas'ud: 'Allah has not made your cure in what He has prohibited for you,' it is likely that he said that when there is another medicine besides the prohibited one. This is from Hawashi al-Hidayah. [3] He said: 'Then this is permissible, because preventing leads to refraining from judgment, and because he is detained for a right...'
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage covers four separate rulings under the topic of disliked acts (karahiyah). First, castrating animals (khisa' al-baha'im): The commentator discusses the correct Arabic terminology (khisa' vs. ikhsa') and notes that al-Rabbani used the if'al form for parallelism with inza'. The ruling itself is not explicitly stated but implied to be permissible or disliked? The focus is linguistic. Second, mating male donkeys with female horses (inza' al-humur 'ala al-khayl): The commentator explains the word inza' as mounting. Again, the ruling is not explicitly given but likely permissible. Third, the enema (huqnah): This is explicitly for medical treatment, which is permissible by consensus. However, using prohibited substances like wine for treatment is generally haram, unless it is known that it has a cure and no alternative exists. Fourth, the salary of the judge (rizq al-qadi): The commentator says it is permissible because preventing payment would lead to judges refraining from their duties, and judges are detained for the rights of people. The text seems cut off but the ruling is that it is permissible for a judge to take a salary from the public treasury (bayt al-mal). All these rulings demonstrate the Hanafi approach of permitting necessary and beneficial actions while prohibiting harmful or prohibited means.

Key Terms
خصاء (khisa'): Castration of animals, correct term preferred over 'ikhsa''.
إنزاء (inza'): Mating male donkeys with female horses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator prefer 'khisa' over 'ikhsa''?
A) Because 'ikhsa' means removing something else.
B) Because 'ikhsa'' in the meaning of castration is grammatically incorrect.
C) Because 'khisa' is easier to pronounce.
D) Because the author of al-Nihayah said so.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits castrating animals and mating horses; linguistic debates do not change the legal permissibility.

Enema for Medical Treatment: Permissibility and the Exception for Prohibited Substances
Ruling with conditions for enema treatment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on using an enema for medical treatment, including the condition that prohibited substances are only allowed if no alternative exists.

Core Idea
Enema for treatment is permissible by consensus; prohibited substances allowed only when no lawful cure exists.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on using an enema (huqnah) for medical treatment. The text states that medical treatment itself is permissible by consensus, supported by hadith. However, a key exception is highlighted: one must not use prohibited substances like wine unless it is known that it contains a cure and no other medicine is available. This ruling balances the principle of seeking cure with the prohibition of using haram substances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its general ruling
The act is 'huqnah' (enema) for the purpose of medical treatment. The text explicitly says 'al-tadawi mubah bi al-ijma' (medical treatment is permissible by consensus). So the enema, as a form of treatment, is mubah.
This consensus includes all lawful methods of treatment.
2
Recognize the exception for prohibited substances
While treatment is mubah, using prohibited substances like khamr (wine) is generally haram. The text states that seeking cure through what is haram is itself haram.
This aligns with the well-known hadith that Allah did not put cure in what He prohibited.
3
Understand the condition for exception
The exception occurs when two conditions are met: (1) it is known that the prohibited substance has a cure, and (2) no other lawful medicine is available. In that case, using the prohibited substance becomes permissible.
The text quotes a possible interpretation of Ibn Mas'ud's statement to allow this exception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Judge's Salary from Public Treasury: Permissibility Based on Necessity
Ruling with two justification for judge's salary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a judge receiving a salary from the public treasury and explain the justification based on preventing harm to the judicial system.

Core Idea
A judge's salary from Bayt al-Mal is permissible to avoid the harm of judges abstaining from duty.

This lesson addresses the permissibility of a judge receiving a salary (rizq) from the public treasury. The text provides two reasons: first, preventing payment would lead judges to refrain from their duties, which harms the community. Second, the judge is detained (mahbus) for the rights of people, implying that compensation is due. This reflects a pragmatic Hanafi approach that supports the judicial system.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and the ruling
The subject is 'rizq al-qadi' (salary of the judge). The commentator says 'hadha yajuz' (this is permissible). So the ruling is permissibility (jawaz).
2
Understand the first reason: preventing abstention
The text states: 'because preventing leads to refraining from judgment' (li-anna al-man'a yu'addi ila al-imtina' 'an al-qada'). If judges are not paid, they might stop performing their duties, causing greater harm.
This is a maslahah (public interest) argument.
3
Understand the second reason: judge as detained for rights
The text continues: 'because he is detained for a right' (wa li-annahu mahbus li-haqq). The judge is held (like a trustee) for the rights of people; therefore, he deserves compensation for his time.
This parallels the ruling on trustees who may take wages.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Al-Jalabi said it. [2] He said: 'and the enema... etc.' He means medical treatment, because medical treatment is permissible by consensus, and there are hadiths permitting treatment. Commentary
This passage covers four separate rulings under the topic of disliked acts (karahiyah). First, castrating animals (khisa' al-baha'im): The commentator discusses the correct Arabic terminology (khisa' vs. ikhsa') and notes that al-Rabbani used the if'al form for parallelism with inza'. The ruling itself is not explicitly stated but implied to be permissible or disliked? The focus is linguistic. Second, mating male donkeys with female horses (inza' al-humur 'ala al-khayl): The commentator explains the word inza' as mounting. Again, the ruling is not explicitly given but likely permissible. Third, the enema (huqnah): This is explicitly for medical treatment, which is permissible by consensus. However, using prohibited substances like wine for treatment is generally haram, unless it is known that it has a cure and no alternative exists. Fourth, the salary of the judge (rizq al-qadi): The commentator says it is permissible because preventing payment would lead to judges refraining from their duties, and judges are detained for the rights of people. The text seems cut off but the ruling is that it is permissible for a judge to take a salary from the public treasury (bayt al-mal). All these rulings demonstrate the Hanafi approach of permitting necessary and beneficial actions while prohibiting harmful or prohibited means.

Key Terms
رزق القاضي (rizq al-qadi): Salary or stipend given to a judge from the public treasury.

Castration of Animals and Covering of Donkeys: Ruling and Linguistic Precision
Commentary on the ruling and linguistic precision

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on castration of animals and covering of donkeys, and explain the correct Arabic terminology as preferred by the commentator.

Core Idea
Castration of animals is permissible in Hanafi fiqh, and the correct Arabic term is 'khisa'' not 'ikhsa''.

In this passage from the commentary, we encounter both a legal ruling and a nuanced discussion of Arabic lexicography. The commentator clarifies that while some use the form 'ikhsa'' for castration, the more correct term is 'khisa''. This attention to language reveals how precise terminology can affect legal texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the actions listed in the matn
Note that the matn lists four actions: castration of animals, covering of donkeys (breeding mules), enema, and judge's salary. Our focus is on the first two. Understand that these are stated as permissible rulings.
2
Analyze the commentator's linguistic correction
The commentator explains that the correct word for castration is 'khisa'' (with a kasra on the kha), not 'ikhsa'' (with a hamza). He cites al-Jalaliyyah to support this.
The word 'ikhsa'' is considered a mistake in meaning, though used by some authors.
3
Recognize the literary device of pairing
The commentator suggests that the author may have used 'ikhsa'' for the sake of pairing with 'inza'' (covering of donkeys), as both are on the pattern of if'al. This shows how stylistic considerations can influence word choice in legal texts.
4
Assess the reliability of authorities
The commentator notes that al-Rabbani (the author of the sharh?) is more reliable than al-Mutarrizi, who is the source of 'al-Nihayah' and 'al-Jalal'. This highlights the hierarchy of scholarly authority in Hanafi fiqh.

Enema Treatment and Use of Prohibited Substances
Commentary on the permissibility of enema and use of prohibited substances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on using prohibited substances for enema and explain the exception when no halal alternative exists.

Core Idea
Using prohibited substances for enema is forbidden unless no halal cure exists.

This commentary from 'Umda al-Ri'aya addresses the permissibility of enema as a medical treatment. While treatment itself is permissible by consensus, using prohibited substances like wine is generally forbidden. However, an exception is made when the prohibited substance is known to be curative and no halal alternative is available. This reflects the Hanafi principle of necessity (darurah) qualified by the condition of no viable alternative.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and general ruling
Enema for treatment is mubah (permissible) by consensus, supported by hadiths. There is no gender distinction.
The basic ruling is that treatment is allowed.
2
Locate the specific prohibition
Using prohibited substances (like wine) for treatment is generally haram. The commentator states: 'seeking cure by what is prohibited is forbidden.'
3
Understand the exception
If it is known that the prohibited substance has a cure and no other medicine (halal alternative) exists, then it becomes permissible to use it.
This is a conditional exception based on darurah.
4
Analyze the proof (Ibn Mas'ood's statement)
The hadith 'Allah did not make your cure in what He forbade you' is interpreted by Hanafis to apply only when a halal alternative exists, not absolutely.
The commentator mentions this interpretation from Hawashi al-Hidayah.
5
Note the cross-madhhab status
Some scholars hold an absolute prohibition, while Hanafis qualify. The Hanafi position is well-founded and accommodates necessity.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٢) قوله : والحقنة...الخ ؛ يريد التداوي ؛ لأنَّ التداوي مباح بالإجماع، وقد ورد بإباحة التداوي أحاديث، ولا فرق بين الرجال والنساء، إلا أنه لا ينبغي أن يستعمل المحرَّمُ كالخمر ونحوها؛ لأن الاستشفاء بالمحرَّم ،حرام، أما إذا عُلِمَ أن فيه شفاء ولا دواءً سواه يجوز الاستشفاء به. وأما قول ابن مسعود له : إن الله لم يجعل شفاءكم فيما حرم عليكم ، فيحتمل أنه قال : فيما كان للمرض دواء آخر غير المحرّم. كذا في «حواشي الهداية».

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the ruling on enema (al-huqna), which is a method of medical treatment. The commentator states that treatment is permissible by consensus, supported by hadiths. There is no gender distinction. However, the general rule is that using prohibited substances (like alcohol) for treatment is forbidden. An exception is made if there is known cure in it and no alternative medicine is available. The narration of Ibn Mas'ood is interpreted to refer only to cases where other lawful medicine exists.

Ruling on Purchasing Necessities for a Minor and Classification of Transactions
Ruling on who may purchase and sell necessities for a minor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which persons may lawfully purchase necessities for a minor and distinguish pure benefit from pure harm transactions according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Only the brother, paternal uncle, and public treasury may buy essentials for a minor to prevent harm.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah discusses a practical scenario: who can step in to provide for a minor when necessities are lacking? The commentary expands on a ruling from the base text, clarifying that certain designated persons may purchase and sell on behalf of the child to prevent suffering. It also lays down a foundational classification of transactions with minors: pure benefit, pure harm, and mixed. Understanding these categories is essential for determining the validity of any transaction involving a minor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and the authorized persons
Read the text to see who may buy and sell for the child: the brother, paternal uncle, and 'mother of the Muslims' (public treasury). The transactions are limited to necessities like food and clothing.
Note that the phrase 'mother of the Muslims' is interpreted as the state treasury (Bayt al-Mal).
2
Understand the reasoning (talil)
Why do these specific persons have this permission? The text states it is to prevent harm (tadarur) to the minor. Allowing the minor to suffer is prohibited.
This is a maslaha-based argument, a common Hanafi tool.
3
Analyze the qiyas
The passage draws an analogy to the executor (wasi) and mudarib who are permitted to spend when traveling with capital. The effective cause (illah) is confinement (habs) that necessitates expenditure.
This qiyas strengthens the ruling by linking to established principles.
4
Learn the threefold classification
Transactions with minors are divided into: (1) Pure benefit (e.g., gifts) – any caretaker or the discerning minor himself may accept; (2) Pure harm (e.g., divorce, manumission) – no one may effect on the minor's behalf; (3) Mixed transactions – require guardianship (not fully detailed in this passage).
The third category is implied; later Hanafi texts elaborate.
5
Apply the classification
Given a transaction, determine which category it falls into and who can validly perform it. For example, accepting a charity is pure benefit; selling the minor's property is mixed.
This step is key for practical fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشراء ما لا بدَّ للطفل منه ، وبيعه لاخ وعم وأم المسلمين، فتكون نفقته في مالهم، وهو مالُ بيت المال ؛ وهذا لأنَّ الحبس من أسباب النفقة، كما في الوصي والمضارب إذا سافر بمال المضاربة. [۲] قوله : وشراء ما لابد للطفل ...الخ ؛ يعني يجوز لهؤلاء الثلاثة أن يشتروا ويبيعوا للصغير ما لا بد منه ؛ وذلك مثل النفقة والكسوة ؛ ولأنه لو يكن لهم ذلك لتضرر الصغير، وهو ممنوع. والأصل فيه أن التصرفات مع الصغير على ثلاثة أقسام : نفع محض، فيملكه كل واحد هو في عياله، وليّاً كان أو أجنبياً كالهبة والصدقة، ويملكه الصبي بنفسه لو كان مميزاً وضرر محض كالعتاق والطلاق، فلا يملكه عليه أحد.
And purchasing what is indispensable for the child, and selling for the brother, paternal uncle, and mother of the Muslims, so his expenditure is in their wealth, which is the treasury; this is because confinement is a cause of expenditure, as in the case of the executor and the mudarib when traveling with mudarabah capital. [2] His statement: 'and purchasing what is indispensable for the child...' etc.; he means that it is permissible for these three to buy and sell for the minor what is indispensable, such as food and clothing. Because if they could not do that, the minor would suffer, which is prohibited. The principle regarding transactions with a minor is that they are of three types: (1) Pure benefit: anyone who is in charge of him, whether guardian or stranger, may own it on his behalf, like gift and charity, and the discerning minor may own it himself; (2) Pure harm: like manumission and divorce, no one may own it on his behalf.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage discusses the permissibility of purchasing and selling necessities for a minor child by certain persons: the brother, paternal uncle, and 'mother of the Muslims' (a phrase possibly referring to the state or treasury). The commentary explains that these individuals may buy and sell necessary items like food and clothing for the child to prevent harm. The general principle (asl) is that transactions with a minor are divided into three categories: pure benefit (like gifts), which any caretaker can do; pure harm (like freeing a slave or divorce), which no one can do on his behalf; and mixed transactions, which require guardianship. This ruling ensures the child's welfare.

Key Terms
نفع محض (naf' mahd): Pure benefit transaction, such as accepting a gift or charity, which a minor may own.
ضرر محض (darar mahd): Pure harm transaction, like divorce or freeing a slave, which no one can effect on the minor's behalf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits brother, paternal uncle, and public treasury to purchase necessities for a minor to prevent harm, and classifies transactions with minors into pure benefit (allowed), pure harm (forbidden), and mixed (requires guardianship).

Disliked Acts: Slaves, Eunuchs, and Loans with Benefit
Listing of disliked acts with brief explanatory commentary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify four disliked acts (makruhat) related to slaves, eunuchs, and loans, and explain the legal reasoning behind each ruling.

Core Idea
A loan that benefits the lender is prohibited as it resembles riba; using a eunuch is disliked for encouraging prohibited castration.

This passage from Kitab al-Karahiyya lists several actions that are considered makruh tanzih (disliked but not sinful) in the Hanafi school. These rulings are based on the principle of blocking the means (sadd al-dhara'i') to prevent harm or prohibited outcomes. Understanding these examples helps you identify when permissible actions cross into disliked territory due to their consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify each disliked act
List the four actions mentioned: (1) a slave clothing another person, (2) a slave gifting money, (3) using a eunuch, (4) a grocer lending with unspecified repayment.
The text also mentions chess and backgammon, but the commentary focuses on these four.
2
Analyze the reasoning for each
For each act, note the reasoning given: (1-2) implies ownership beyond the slave's authority; (3) encourages castration; (4) constitutes a loan that brings benefit (qard jarra naf'an).
The underlying principle is sadd al-dhara'i'.
3
Determine the hukm level
All acts are makruh tanzih (disliked, not sinful). Identify the category: makruh tanzih means better to avoid but not punishable.
This differs from haram or makruh tahrimi.
4
Connect to broader fiqh principles
Relate each act to principles: loans with benefit resemble riba; using a eunuch promotes prohibited castration; slave gifts exceed authority. Recognize the use of sadd al-dhara'i'.
These are examples of how fiqh prevents harm indirectly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكره كسوته ثوباً، وإهداؤه النقدين، واستخدام الخصي، وإقراض بقال شيئاً يأخذ منه ما شاء، واللعب بالشطرنج والنرد وكل لهو
وكره كسوته ثوباً، وإهداؤه النقدين : أي كُرِهَ أن يكسو العبدُ غيره ثوباً ، وأن يهديه النقدين، واستخدام الخصي) ، فإنَّهُ حث على خصاء الإنسان، وهو غير جائز، (وإقراض بقال شيئاً يأخذ منه ما شاء) ، فإنَّه قرض جرَّ نفعاً
And it is disliked: his clothing of a garment, and his giving of the two dirhams, and using a eunuch, and a grocer lending something from which he takes whatever he wishes, and playing chess and backgammon and all amusement. And it is disliked: his clothing of a garment, and his giving of the two dirhams: i.e., it is disliked for the slave to clothe another with a garment, and to give him the two dirhams; and using a eunuch, because it encourages the castration of a human, which is not permissible; and a grocer lending something from which he takes whatever he wishes, because it is a loan that brings benefit.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage from Kitab al-Karahiyya lists several disliked actions (makruhat). The matn states that it is disliked for a slave to clothe someone else or to give money as a gift. The commentator explains that this is because such actions imply ownership beyond the slave's authority. Using a eunuch is disliked because it encourages the prohibited act of castration. A grocer lending goods with the condition that he can take whatever he wants in return is disliked because it constitutes a loan that brings benefit (qard jarra naf'an), which is prohibited as it resembles riba. The passage also mentions chess and backgammon, but the commentary on that is separate. These rulings are based on the principle of blocking the means (sadd al-dhara'i') to prevent harm.

Key Terms
قرض جر نفعاً (qarḍ jarra nafʿan): A loan that brings benefit to the lender, which is prohibited as it resembles riba.
خصاء (khiṣāʾ): Castration, which is prohibited in Islam except for animals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is it disliked (makruh) for a grocer to lend something and then take whatever he wants in return?
A) Because it involves uncertainty (gharar)
B) Because it is a loan that brings benefit to the lender
C) Because it is equivalent to gambling
D) Because it wastes time

Key Takeaway
Disliked acts in fiqh often result from blocking means that lead to prohibited outcomes, such as loans with hidden benefits or employing eunuchs.

Ruling on Playing Chess and Backgammon (Inter-Madhhab Dispute)
Inter-madhhab dispute on permissibility of chess and backgammon

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on chess and backgammon, explain the Shafi'i position, and apply the Hanafi response using the principle of sadd al-dhara'i'.

Core Idea
Chess and backgammon are prohibited in the Hanafi school because they lead to neglect of prayer and waste of time, even without gambling.

The ruling on recreational games like chess and backgammon has been a point of scholarly debate. This passage presents the Hanafi view that both games are prohibited (backgammon by consensus, chess as a likely cause of neglect) in contrast to the Shafi'i view that permits chess under conditions. Understanding this dispute reveals how different schools weigh potential benefits against risks of spiritual harm.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify that the Hanafi school prohibits playing chess and backgammon. Backgammon is haram by consensus based on a hadith; chess is prohibited because it is a likely cause of missing prayer and wasting life.
The ruling for backgammon is explicit; chess is judged by analogy and the principle of blocking means to harm.
2
Identify the opposing school and its view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that chess is permissible, but with conditions: one must not miss prayers and there must be no gambling.
The Shafi'i school focuses on the absence of explicit prohibition and the potential benefit of sharpening the mind.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Shafi'i argument is that chess has a benefit (tašḥīd al-ḵāṭir) and no clear prohibition in the Quran or Sunnah, so it is permissible as long as it does not lead to harm.
This is a typical application of the principle of ibāḥah aṣliyyah (original permissibility of things).
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response argues that chess is a mazannah (likely cause) of missing prayer and wasting time. It engrosses the mind to the point of ignoring hunger and thirst, so its harms outweigh any benefit. This is based on sadd al-dhara'i' (blocking the means to evil).
The Hanafis do not deny that chess may sharpen the mind, but they consider the potential spiritual harm too great.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Hanafis advise against playing chess even without stakes or missing prayers; Shafi'is allow it under those conditions. A Hanafi mufti would rule it impermissible.
The difference stems from how each school assesses risk and the weight given to preventive measures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واللعب بالشطرنج والنّردِ وكلُّ لهو)، هذا عندنا، وعند الشافعي : يباح لعب الشطرنج ؛ إذ فيه تشحيد الخاطر لكن بشرط أن لا تفوتهُ الصَّلاة، ولا يكون فيه ميسر ، قلنا : هو مظنه فوت الصَّلاة، وتضييع العمر، واستيلاء الفكر الباطل ، حتى لا يحس بالجوع والعطش فكيف بغيرهما.
[٢] قوله : ولا يكون فيه ميسر ؛ وهو اسم لكل قمار، وإن لم يقامر بها، فهو عبث ولهو، وإن أردت تفصيل المقام فاعلم أنه قال في الجامع الصغير»: أما النرد فهو حرام بالإجماع، لما روى أبو موسى الأشعري له أنه قال : قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : «من لعب النرد فقد عصى الله ورسوله»)، وسليمان بن بريدة عن أبيه عن النبي :
And playing chess and backgammon and all amusement: this is according to us (Hanafis). According to al-Shafi'i, playing chess is permissible because it sharpens the mind, but on condition that it does not cause him to miss the prayer and there is no gambling in it. We say: it is a likely cause of missing prayer, wasting life, and takeover of false thought, so much so that one does not feel hunger or thirst, so how about other things? [2] His statement: 'And there is no gambling in it'; and it is a term for every kind of game of chance. Even if one does not gamble with it, it is idle play and amusement. If you want detail of the matter, know that he said in al-Jami' al-Saghir: As for backgammon, it is haram by consensus, because Abu Musa al-Ash'ari narrated that the Messenger of Allah said: 'Whoever plays backgammon has disobeyed Allah and His Messenger.' And Sulayman ibn Burayda from his father from the Prophet...
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage presents the inter-madhhab dispute regarding chess and backgammon. The Hanafi position is that playing chess and backgammon is prohibited (haram) or at least prohibitively disliked (makruh tahrimi). The Shafi'i school permits chess specifically, provided it does not cause one to miss prayers and is free from gambling. The Hanafi response refutes this by arguing that chess is a likely excuse for neglecting prayer and wasting time, as it engrosses the mind to the point of ignoring hunger and thirst. The text further clarifies that 'maysir' (gambling) applies to any game of chance; even without monetary stakes, such games are idle play. Backgammon is explicitly haram by consensus, based on the hadith of Abu Musa al-Ash'ari. The commentary emphasizes that the prohibition is to block the means (sadd al-dhara'i') to greater harm.

Key Terms
ميسر (maysir): Gambling or game of chance; includes any activity where stakes are involved.
تشحيد الخاطر (tašḥīd al-ḵāṭir): Sharpening the mind or intellect; a benefit claimed by Shafi'is for chess.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Chess involves gambling
B) Chess leads to missing prayer and wasting time
C) Chess is not mentioned in hadith
D) Chess is a form of backgammon

Key Takeaway
Chess and backgammon are prohibited in the Hanafi school due to their potential to distract from religious obligations, with backgammon being explicitly haram by consensus.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Collar on Slave and Transacting Mecca Land
Ruling with intra-madhhab dispute on two issues

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi dispute regarding the permissibility of placing an iron collar on a slave and selling/renting Mecca land, and identify the relied-upon position (mufta bihi).

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa prohibits two matters the Sahibayn permit: collar on a slave and transactions in Mecca land.

This passage presents two distinct issues where Abu Hanifa's stricter view differs from that of his two main students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. We will examine the reasoning on each side and determine the operative ruling for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two issues
The text covers two separate cases: (1) placing an iron collar on a slave, and (2) selling or renting land in Mecca. Note each issue and the ruling associated with each.
2
State the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa)
According to Abu Hanifa, both actions are makruh (disliked). For the collar, it resembles oppression; for Mecca land, because it is a haram (sacred territory) with ownership restrictions.
The text explicitly says 'because Mecca is a haram'.
3
State the Sahibayn position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani consider both actions permissible (ja'iz). Their reasoning: the land of Mecca is owned property (mamluka), thus sale and lease are valid. For the collar, they see no inherent prohibition.
The text says 'because its land is owned property'.
4
Weigh the positions and determine mufta bihi
Later Hanafi fatwa typically follows the Sahibayn for transactions, as ownership is the default. For the collar, it remains disapproved (makruh tanzihi). However, the text does not explicitly give tarjih; later authorities like al-Nihaya provide more detail.
The relied-upon position for fatwa today is permissibility of transactions, but placing a restrictive collar is still discouraged.
5
Consider the practical implication (thamarah al-khilaf)
If one follows Abu Hanifa, selling or renting Mecca land is not allowed. If one follows the Sahibayn, it is allowed. For the collar, the difference is whether a master can discipline a slave with a collar – Abu Hanifa says no, Sahibayn say yes.
The mufta bihi on collars leans toward prohibition, but the transaction issue is settled permissibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجعل الغل في عنق عبده وبيع أرض مكة وإجارتها)، هذا عند أبي حنيفة؛ لأن مكة حرام، وعندهما: يجوز لأن أرضها مملوكة.
[1] قوله : وجعل الغل...الخ ؛ أي كرة أيضاً أن يجعل في عنق عبده طوقاً مسمراً بسمار عظيم يمنعه عن تحريك رأسه، هو معتاد بين الظلمة، قال في «النهاية»: الغُلّ:
And placing an iron collar around the neck of his slave, and selling land in Mecca and leasing it: this is according to Abu Hanifa because Mecca is a haram (sanctuary); while according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is permissible because its land is owned property. [Commentary:] i.e., it is also disliked to place around the neck of his slave a collar fixed with a large nail that prevents him from moving his head; this is a practice of oppressors. Said in al-Nihaya: al-ghull (collar)...
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute (ikhtilaf) regarding two matters: (1) placing an iron collar on a slave, and (2) selling or leasing land in Mecca. The author states that according to Abu Hanifa, both actions are disliked (makruh). His reasoning for the second is that Mecca is a haram (sacred territory), implying it cannot be owned or transacted. However, according to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad), both are permissible because the land of Mecca is owned property (mamluka) and can be sold or leased. The commentary then explains the collar: it is a metal ring nailed around the neck, preventing movement, typical of tyrants. The passage is a summary of the ruling, without further proof.

Key Terms
الغل (al-ghull): An iron collar placed around the neck, often with a nail, used as punishment or humiliation.
حرام (haram): Sacred territory; used here for Mecca, implying restrictions on ownership and transactions.
إجارة (ijarah): Leasing or renting property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, two issues where Abu Hanifa's strict view of haram status is overruled by the Sahibayn's practicality, leading to permissibility for transactions and discouraged but not prohibited for the collar.
